Particular and Absolute Words: The Foundation of General and Specific Terms in Logic
Definition and defense of the particular/absolute division with an objection on alif lam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mu'ayyan and mutlaq words and explain why alif lam indicates generality even for unique entities.

Core Idea
A word's meaning can be locked to one individual or open to many; the definite article opens it, but reality can close it.

This lesson introduces a key division of words based on their scope of reference: particular (mu'ayyan) words that pin down a single individual, and absolute (mutlaq) words that allow sharing. The author uses examples from everyday language and then tackles a tricky objection: if 'al-shams' (the sun) has the definite article, why isn't it general? The answer reveals how logic and reality interact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories in the Arabic passage.
Find the terms 'mu'ayyan' and 'mutlaq' in the source_arabic. Note how the author defines each: one locks meaning to a single entity, the other permits multiple instances.
The definition of mu'ayyan includes the idea that its concept prevents sharing; mutlaq does not prevent it.
2
Examine the examples given for each category.
For mu'ayyan, the author lists 'Zaid', 'this horse', 'this tree', and also 'this blackness' and 'this motion'. For mutlaq, he gives 'blackness', 'motion', 'human'. Notice how demonstratives or proper names make a word particular.
Examples help internalize the distinction: a demonstrative ('this') points to a specific instance, making the word particular.
3
Understand the rule about alif lam and generality.
The author states that a singular noun with the definite article indicates generality (istighraq al-jins). This is a standard view in Arabic logic. He then presents an objection: words like 'al-ilah', 'al-shams', 'al-ard' have the article but refer to unique entities.
The objection challenges the rule by showing apparent counterexamples.
4
Analyze the author's response to the objection.
The author argues that the prevention of generality in these cases is not due to the word itself but to external reality. If multiple gods or suns were possible, the words would be general. The definite article still functions generically; the uniqueness comes from the world, not language.
This distinction between linguistic form and extra-linguistic reality is crucial for understanding how logic relates to actual existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم الثاني : أنَّ اللفظ بالإضافة إلى خصوص المعنى وشموله ينقسم :
إلى لفظ يدلّ على عين واحدة ونسميه معيّناً
وإلى ما يدلّ على أشياء كثيرة تتفقُ في معنى واحدٍ ونسمّيهِ مطلقاً .
مثالُ الأَوَّلِ : قولُكَ : زيدٌ ، وهذا الفرسُ ، وهذه الشجرةُ ؛ فإنَّهُ لا يدلُّ إِلَّا على شخص معيَّن ، وكذلك قولك : هذا السواد ، وهذه الحركة .
وحدهُ : أَنَّهُ اللفظ الذي لا يمكن أن يكون مفهومُهُ إِلَّا ذلكَ الواحد بعينِهِ ، فإِنْ قُصِدَ إشراك غيرِهِ فيه .. منع نفس مفهوم اللفظ منه .
وأمَّا المطلقُ : فهوَ الذي لا يمنعُ نفس مفهوم اللفظ مِن وقوع مِن وقوع احد المطا الاشتراك في معناه ؛ كقولك : السواد والحركة والإنسان وبالجملة : الاسم المفرد في لغة العرب إذا أُدخل عليه الألفُ واللام .. كانَ لاستغراق الجنس ، وقد يُسمَّى لفظاً عاماً ، ويُقالُ : الألف واللام للعموم . فإن قلت : كيف يستقيمُ هذا ومَنْ يقولُ : ( الإله ) أو تحريجة : بعض الألفاظ الكلية
( الشمس ) أو ( الأرضُ ) .. فقد أدخلَ الألف واللام ولا يدلُّ اللفظ منتمية إلا على وجودِ معيَّن خاص لا شِرْكَةَ فِيهِ ؟!
فاعلم : أنَّ هذا الوهم غلط ؛ فإنَّ امتناع الشِّرْكَةِ ها هنا ليسَ لنفس اللفظ ، بلِ الذي وضعَ اللغةَ لَوْ جوَّزَ في الآلهة عدداً .. لكان يرى هذا اللفظ عاماً في الآلهة كلّها ، فحيثُ امتنع الشمولُ .. لمْ
The second division: the word, with respect to specificity and generality of meaning, is divided: into a word that indicates a single entity and we call it particular (mu'ayyan); and into what indicates many things that agree in one meaning and we call it absolute (mutlaq). Example of the first: your saying 'Zaid', 'this horse', 'this tree'; for it indicates only a specific individual, and likewise your saying 'this blackness', 'this motion'. Its definition: it is the word whose concept cannot be other than that one specific thing; if one intends to include another in it, the very concept of the word prevents it. As for the absolute: it is that whose concept does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in its meaning; like your saying 'blackness', 'motion', 'human'. In sum: the singular noun in the Arabic language, when the definite article (alif lam) is entered upon it, becomes for the generality of the genus (istighraq al-jins), and it is sometimes called a general word (lafz 'amm), and it is said: the definite article indicates generality (alif lam li'l-umum). If you say: how does this stand, considering that one who says 'al-ilah' (the god) or 'al-shams' (the sun) or 'al-ard' (the earth) has entered the definite article but the word does not indicate except upon a specific existence with no sharing in it? Then know: this illusion is an error; for the prevention of sharing here is not due to the word itself, but the one who established the language, if he had allowed multiplicity in gods, he would have seen this word as general over all gods. So when generality is prevented...
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major division of words: particular (mu'ayyan) and absolute (mutlaq). The author defines mu'ayyan as a word that can only refer to one specific individual, giving examples like 'Zaid' or 'this horse'. The definition emphasizes that the very concept excludes partnership. The absolute (mutlaq) is defined as a word whose concept does not prevent sharing in its meaning, e.g., 'blackness' or 'human'. The author then notes that in Arabic, a singular noun with the definite article (alif lam) typically indicates generality (istighraq al-jins), but an objection is raised: words like 'al-shams' (the sun) or 'al-ard' (earth) take the definite article yet refer to a unique specific entity. The author responds that the prevention of generality in these cases is not due to the linguistic form, but due to extra-linguistic reality—if there were multiple suns, the word 'al-shams' would be general. The response is cut off, but the argument aims to show that the definite article's generality is real, but external facts can restrict it.

Key Terms
معين
مطلق
الألف واللام للعموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does 'al-shams' (the sun) not indicate generality despite having the definite article?
A) The definite article in Arabic does not indicate generality for proper nouns.
B) The prevention of sharing is due to external reality, not the word itself.
C) The word 'sun' is intrinsically particular regardless of the article.
D) The definite article indicates generality only for abstract nouns.

Key Takeaway
The particular-absolute distinction hinges on whether a word's concept permits multiple instances; the definite article in Arabic signals generality, but real-world uniqueness can override that signal without invalidating the linguistic rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Essential, Necessary, and Accidental Attributes
Author defines the three types of attributes relative to a meaning: essential, necessary, and accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (dhati), necessary (lazim), and accidental ('arid) attributes, and determine which must be included in a definition.

Core Idea
Essence is defined solely by essential attributes; necessary and accidental attributes are extrinsic.

In this passage, the author introduces a crucial classification for any meaning when compared to another: essential, necessary, or accidental. Mastering this distinction sharpens your ability to construct precise definitions (hadd) and assess arguments in syllogisms. Let's unpack each type with the examples provided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the meaning under analysis
Look at the two meanings being compared. For example, 'tree' and 'body'. Determine which meaning is being defined (the first) and which is the attribute (the second).
The author compares meaning to meaning, so the attribute is relative to the thing.
2
Apply the essentiality test
Ask: Can you understand the first meaning without understanding the attribute? If not, it is essential (dhati). Example: You cannot understand 'tree' without understanding 'body' – body is part of tree's reality.
Essential attributes are constitutive of quiddity.
3
Apply the necessary/accidental test
If the attribute is not essential, ask: Does it always accompany the thing in existence? If yes, it is necessary (lazim). If it can separate, it is accidental ('arid). Example: Shadow is necessary for a horse under normal conditions, but not essential.
Necessary attributes are concomitant but not definitional.
4
Practice with the plant example
The author says a definition of plant must include 'growing body'. That is because body-ness and growth are essential. Test other attributes like 'having roots' – are they essential or necessary?
Use the criteria to classify additional attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم الثاني : المعنى إذا نسب إلى المعنى . . وجدناه : إمَّا ذاتيّاً له ، ويُسمَّى صفةَ النفس ، وإمَّا لازماً له ، ويُسمَّى وصفاً لازماً ، وإمَّا عارضاً لهُ لا يبعد أن ينفصل عنه في الوجود . ولا بدَّ مِنْ إتقان هذه النسب ؛ فإنَّها نافعة في القياس والحدّ جميعاً . أمَّا الذاتي : فإنّي أعني بهِ : كلَّ شيءٍ داخل في حقيقة الشيء وماهيته دخولاً لا يُتصوَّرُ فهم المعنى دونَ فهمِهِ ، وذلك كاللونيَّةِ للسواد ، وكالجسميَّةِ في الفرس والشجرِ ، فإِنَّ مَنْ فهم الشجر . فقد فهم جسماً مخصوصاً، فتكونُ الجسميَّة داخلة في ذاتِ الشجريَّةِ دخولاً بهِ قِوامُها في الوجودِ والعقل على وجهِ لَوْ قُدِرَ عدمُها .. لبطل وجودُ الشجرِ والفرس ، ولوْ قُدِرَ خروجُها عنِ الذهنِ .. لبطلَ عَنِ الذهنِ فهمُ الشجرِ والفرس وما يجري هذا المَجْرى ، فلا بدَّ من إدراجه في حدّ الشيء ، فمَنْ يحدُّ النبات يلزمُهُ أن يقول : إِنَّهُ جسم نام لا محالة . وأمَّا اللازم : فما لا يفارقُ الذات ألبتةَ ، ولكنَّ فهُمُ الحقيقةِ غيرُ موقوفِ عليهِ ؛ كوقوع الظل لشخص الفرس والنباتِ عند طلوع الشمس ، فإنَّ هذا أمر لازمٌ لا يُتصوَّرُ أن يُفارقَ وجودَهُ عندَ مَنْ يُعبّرُ عَن مجاري العاداتِ باللزوم ويعتقده ، ولكنَّهُ مِنْ توابع الذاتِ ولوازمِهِ ، وليس بذاتي لهُ . وأعني بهِ : أَنَّ فهمَ كُنْهِ حقيقته غير موقوف على فهم ذلك
The second division: When a meaning is compared to another meaning, we find it: either essential (dhati) to it, and it is called the attribute of the self (sifat al-nafs); or necessary (lazim) to it, and it is called a necessary attribute (wasf lazim); or accidental ('arid) to it, not far from being separable from it in existence. And it is necessary to master these relations; for they are beneficial in syllogism and definition alike. As for the essential (dhati): I mean by it everything that enters into the reality (haqiqah) and quiddity (mahiyyah) of a thing, an entry such that understanding the meaning is inconceivable without understanding it. That is like color-ness for blackness, and like body-ness in horse and tree. For whoever understands 'tree' indeed understands a particular body, so body-ness is included in the essence of tree-ness, an inclusion by which its subsistence in existence and intellect is such that if its non-existence were supposed, the existence of tree and horse would be invalidated; and if its removal from the mind were supposed, the understanding of tree and horse and what runs in this vein would be invalidated from the mind. So it must be included in the definition of the thing. So whoever defines plant must necessarily say that it is a growing body. As for the necessary (lazim): it is that which does not part from the essence at all, but the understanding of the reality is not dependent upon it. Like the casting of shadow for the person of a horse or plant at the rising of the sun. For this is a necessary matter, not conceivable to part from its existence for one who expresses the courses of habits by necessity and believes it, but it is among the concomitants of the essence and its necessities, and it is not essential to it. And I mean by it: that the understanding of the inner reality (kunh haqiqah) is not dependent on understanding that.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage introduces the second division of meanings, which considers the relationship of one meaning to another in terms of essentiality, necessity, and accident. The author states that mastering these relations is beneficial for both syllogism and definition. He then elaborates on the essential (dhati): it is a constituent of the reality and quiddity of a thing, such that the thing cannot be understood without it. Examples include 'color-ness' for blackness, and 'body-ness' for horse and tree. If you understand 'tree', you already understand a body; body-ness is part of the essence of tree-ness. If body were removed from existence or from the mind, tree and horse would cease to exist or be understood. Hence, any proper definition must include the essential attributes. For instance, a definition of plant must include 'growing body'. Next, he explains the necessary (lazim): it is a concomitant that never separates from the essence, yet the understanding of the essence does not depend on it. Example: the shadow cast by a horse or plant when the sun rises. Shadow is a necessary concomitant under normal natural laws, but understanding the essence of horse does not require understanding shadow. So the necessary attribute is not part of the definition. The author carefully distinguishes between what is essential (dhati) and what is necessary (lazim), emphasizing that the necessary is not constitutive of the thing's reality. This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic for constructing valid definitions (hadd) and for determining what belongs to the essence versus what is a mere concomitant in syllogistic reasoning.

Key Terms
ذاتي
لازم
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is an example of a necessary (lazim) attribute?
A) Body-ness for tree
B) Color-ness for blackness
C) Shadow for a horse at sunrise
D) Being a growing body for plant

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes constitute the definition and are indispensable for understanding; necessary attributes always accompany but do not define; accidental attributes may or may not be present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the concept of 'plant' (nabat) as in the passage. The author says its definition includes 'growing body'. Now test: Is 'having leaves' an essential, necessary, or accidental attribute? Defend your answer using the passage's criteria.
?

Some attributes, like facing qibla, are considered conditions (shurut) that are necessary but not part of the essence. Using the author's criteria, explain why facing qibla is necessary ('lazim') rather than essential.

Identifying the 'Arid (Accident) in Logic
Author defines 'arid (accident) and distinguishes it from lazim (necessary concomitant).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish an 'arid (accident) from a lazim (necessary concomitant) based on separability in conception, using examples from Islamic theology and fiqh.

Core Idea
An accident is what can be separated in the mind, not necessarily in reality.

In logic, not every permanent attribute is essential. The author defines 'arid (accident) as an attribute whose separation from its subject is conceivable, even if it never actually separates. This distinction is crucial for identifying which properties are truly essential to a thing and which are merely accidental. We will learn to apply this test using clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the word 'العارض' (al-'āriḍ) in the Arabic. The author states: 'I mean by it: that which it is not necessary for it to be concomitant, rather its separation is conceivable.' This is the core definition.
The term 'arid is contrasted with essential (dhati) in logic.
2
Extract the defining characteristic
The key characteristic is 'separability in conception' (تُتصور مفارقته). The author emphasizes that this separability is mental, not necessarily actual. Even if an attribute never leaves its subject in reality (like the blackness of the Zanjī), if you can imagine it without that attribute, it is an 'arid.
3
Classify the examples given
The author lists three types: quick separation (redness of blush), slow separation (yellowness of gold), and permanent but separable in conception (blueness of eyes, blackness of Zanjī). All are 'arid because they can be conceived as separate.
The Zanjī example refers to people from East Africa; their skin color is an accident, not essential to their humanity.
4
Distinguish from lazim (necessary concomitant)
The author contrasts 'arid with lazim: attributes like 'the earth being created' or 'a dense body casting a shadow' cannot be conceived as separate from their subjects. If you try to imagine the earth as not created or a dense body as not casting a shadow, it is impossible. These are lazim.
In Islamic theology, the world's createdness is a lazim of its contingent nature.
5
Apply the separability test
Given any attribute, ask: 'Can I conceive this subject without this attribute?' If yes, it is 'arid; if no, it is lazim. Practice with examples from fiqh: Is 'intoxication' an 'arid of wine? (Yes, because wine can be conceived without intoxication, e.g., after vinegarization.)
This test is used in usul al-fiqh to determine effective causes ('illal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العارضُ : فأعني بهِ : ما ليسَ مِنْ ضرورتِهِ أن يلازم ، بل تتصور مفارقتُه ؛ إمَّا سريعاً كحمرة الخجل ، أو بطيئاً كصفرة الذهب ، وربَّما لا يزولُ في الوجود ؛ كزرقة العين وسوادِ الزَّنْجِي ، ولكن يمكن رفعه في الوهم . وأمَّا كونُ الأرض مخلوقةً ، وكونُ الجسمِ الكثيف ذا ظلّ مانع نور الشمس .. فلازمٌ لا تُتصوَّرُ مفارقتُهُ .
As for the 'arid: I mean by it: that which it is not necessary for it to be concomitant, rather its separation is conceivable; either quickly like the redness of blush, or slowly like the yellowness of gold, and perhaps it does not cease in existence, like the blueness of the eye and the blackness of the Zanjī, but it can be removed in estimation. As for the earth being created, and the dense body having a shadow that blocks sunlight... it is a necessary concomitant whose separation is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'āriḍ (accident) in logic. The author explains that an accident is not essentially attached to its subject; it can be separated from it either quickly or slowly, and even if it never actually separates in the external world (like the blueness of eyes or the blackness of certain people), it can still be imagined separate. This distinguishes it from a lāzim (necessary concomitant), like the earth being created or a dense body casting a shadow, which cannot be conceived as separate. The key point is possibility of separation in the mind, not actual occurrence.

Key Terms
العارض
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
An 'arid is any attribute whose separation from its subject is conceivable in the mind, regardless of whether it actually separates in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Certainty and Its First Level
Author defines certainty and distinguishes three states of the soul when assenting to a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of certainty as defined in the passage and distinguish the first level's meta-certainty.

Core Idea
Certainty is not just belief; it requires a second-order assurance that excludes any possibility of error.

In this passage, the author defines certainty (al-yaqin) as a state of the soul that is not merely assent but includes tranquility and a meta-level certainty that the first certainty is correct. He then describes the first of three states, which is the highest level: unshakable conviction that withstands even prophetic miracles. This lesson will help you parse this definition and understand its epistemological significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the Arabic term for 'certainty' in the passage. The author places great importance on understanding it correctly: 'you will not know it except by what I say'.
The term is 'اليقين' (al-yaqin).
2
Extract the definition's components
The definition has three elements: (1) the soul's assent to a proposition (تصديق), (2) tranquility (سكنت إليها), and (3) a second-order certainty that the first certainty is correct and cannot involve error. Write each in your own words.
The second-order certainty is unique to this definition.
3
Distinguish first-order and second-order certainty
There are two levels of 'qat'' (decisiveness): the first is about the proposition itself; the second is about the correctness of the first. Notice that the text says 'a second decisiveness is added to it'.
This meta-certainty makes the belief immune to doubt.
4
Analyze the unshakable nature
The passage says that even if the best people, or even a prophet with a miracle, claim the opposite, the certain person does not doubt but laughs. Consider the strength of this conviction. What does it imply about the grounds for certainty?
Certainty here is self-guaranteeing, not dependent on external authority.
5
Connect with Ghazali's example
Read the footnote: knowing that ten is more than three is so certain that a miracle turning a stick into a snake cannot shake it. How does this example illustrate the definition?
The example shows that genuine certainty is invulnerable to any contrary evidence.

Source Text

الفصل الأول في مراتب الإدراك أمَّا اليقين .. فلا تعرفُهُ إلَّا بما أقولُه ؛ وهوَ أَنَّ النفس إذا ما هو اليقين ؟ أذعنت للتصديقِ بقضيةٍ مِنَ القضايا وسكنتْ إليها .. فلها ثلاثة أحوال : أحدها : أن تتيقَّنَ وتقطعَ بهِ ، وينضاف إليه قطع ثانٍ ؛ وهوَ أن يقطع بأنَّ قطعَهُ بهِ صحيح ، ويتيقن بأنَّ يقينَهُ لا يمكن أن يكونَ فيهِ سهو ولا غلط ولا التباس ، ولا يُجوِّزُ الغلط لا في يقينِهِ بالقضية، ولا في يقينه الثاني بصحة يقينه ، ويكون فيه آمناً مطمئناً ، قاطعاً بأنَّهُ لا يُتصوَّرُ أن يتغيَّرَ فيه رأيه ، ولا أن يطلع على دليل غابَ عنهُ ، فيغيّر اعتقادَهُ . ولؤوْ حُكِي نقيضُ اعتقادِهِ عنْ أفضل الناس .. فلا يتوقف في تجهيله وتكذيبه وخطئِهِ ، بل لوْ حُكِيَ لهُ أنَّ نبيّاً معَ معجزةٍ قدِ ادَّعى أنَّ ما يتيقَنُهُ خطأً ، ودليل خطئِهِ معجزتُه .. فلا يكونُ لهُ تأثير بهذا السماع إلَّا أن يضحكَ منهُ ومن المَحْكِي عنه ( ۲ ) (۱) العنوان زيادة من ( د ) فقط . (۲) انظر ( المنقذ من الضلال ) ( ص ٤٩ ) ، وعبارته فيه : ( فإني إذا علمتُ أن العشرة أكثر من الثلاثة ، فلو قال لي قائل : لا ، بل الثلاثة أكثر من العشرة ؛ بدليل أني أقلب هذه العصا ثعباناً ، وقَلَبَها ، وشاهدت ذلك منه .. . لم أشك بسببه في معرفتي ، ولم يحصل لي منه إلا التعجب من كيفية قدرته عليه ، فأما الشك فيما علمته . . فلا ) .
Chapter One: On the Levels of Perception. As for certainty (al-yaqin) — you will not know it except by what I say: it is that the soul, when it assents to a proposition and is at ease with it, has three states: One of them: that it is certain and decisive about it, and a second decisiveness is added to it; that is, it is decisive that its decisiveness is correct, and it is certain that its certainty cannot involve oversight, error, or confusion, and it does not allow error either in its certainty of the proposition or in its second certainty of the correctness of its certainty, and it is secure and tranquil in it, decisive that it is inconceivable that its opinion could change, or that it could come across a proof that eludes it and change its belief. And if the opposite of its belief is reported from the best of people, it does not hesitate to declare him ignorant, to deny him, and to deem him mistaken. Rather, if it is reported to him that a prophet with a miracle has claimed that what he is certain of is an error, and the proof of his error is his miracle, this hearing has no effect on him except that he laughs at him and at the one who reported it. (1) The title is added only from (D). (2) See al-Munqidh min al-Dalal, p. 49, his statement: 'For if I know that ten is more than three, and someone says to me: No, rather three is more than ten; as evidence I turn this stick into a snake, and he turns it, and I witness that from him, I would not doubt my knowledge because of it, and I would only get wonder at his ability. As for doubting what I know, no.'
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of certainty. The author begins by stating that certainty can only be understood through his explanation. He defines certainty as a state of the soul when it assents to a proposition and is at rest with it. Then he describes three states, but only the first is fully elaborated here. The first state involves two levels of conviction: the initial certainty about the proposition, and a meta-certainty that confirms the correctness of that certainty, leaving no room for doubt, error, or change. The certainty is so firm that even if the best people or a prophet with a miracle claim the opposite, the person does not waver but rather laughs. This illustrates the strongest level of certainty, which is unshakable. The footnote from Ghazali's al-Munqidh reinforces this by giving an example of knowing that ten is more than three, and how even a miracle would not cause doubt, only wonder.

تصديق
قطع

The highest certainty involves being certain that one's certainty is infallible, making it immune to doubt even if challenged by a miracle.

The First Method of Qiyas: Deleting Irrelevant Differences
Author defines the first method of analogical reasoning and illustrates with fiqh examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first method of qiyas (tariq awwal) and apply it to delete irrelevant differences in a fiqh analogy.

Core Idea
The first method of qiyas is deletion of the apparent difference when it has no effect on the ruling.

This lesson introduces the first method of analogical reasoning according to al-Razi. In this method, the person making the analogy (al-mulhiq) only addresses deletion (al-hazf): he deletes the apparent distinguishing feature between the original case (asl) and the new case (far') because that feature is irrelevant to the ruling. The passage provides examples from hadith about urine and water to show how this method works. By understanding this method, you will strengthen your ability to construct sound analogies in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Find the author's definition: 'a la yata'arrada al-mulhiq illa li-l-hazf' – the annexer only addresses deletion. Understand that 'deletion' here means removing the apparent difference from consideration.
2
Distinguish the two sub-cases
The author contrasts (a) when we know the specific cause (e.g., deficiency vs. oversight in prostration) and (b) when the cause is ambiguous among multiple possibilities (e.g., riba example with raisins and dates). Both involve deletion, but the awareness of the cause differs.
3
Analyze the hadith example (boy's urine)
The hadith says sprinkling suffices for a boy's urine but not a girl's. Here the difference (gender) is explicitly made relevant – so we cannot delete it. This shows the boundary of the method.
4
Analyze the water example (prohibition of urinating in still water)
The prophetic address is to men, but we extend it to women because gender does not affect impurity. We delete the male-specific address. This illustrates the method in action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جبرُ النقصانِ من حيثُ إِنَّهُ نقصان ، أوْ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ سهوٌ ، فَإِنَّهُ إنْ كانَ للنقصانِ ؛ فلو ترك شيئاً مِنَ الأبعاض عمداً .. ينبغي أن يسجد ، وإنْ كانَ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ سهو .. لم يسجد في العمدِ ، وقبل أن يتلخص لنا خصوص هذهِ الصفاتِ نعلم أنَّ العصر في معنى الظهر . وأما الوجه الثاني : فهو ألا يتعيَّنَ لا أصل العلة ولا وصفها ، ولكن نعلمُها مبهمةً بين جملةٍ مِنَ المعاني ، كما أنَّا في الربا ربَّما يتبيَّن لنا أنَّ الزبيب في معنى التمر قبل أن يتّضح أنَّ العلَّةَ هي الكيلُ أوِ الطَّعْمُ أو الماليَّةُ أو القوتُ ؛ إِذْ نعلمُ أَنَّهُ كيفَ كَانَ .. فالزبيب مشارك له فيه ، ولا يفارقُهُ إِلَّا في كونِهِ زبيباً ، وهذا لا ينبغي أن يؤثر قطعاً . والدليل على أَنَّهُ لا بدَّ مِن استشعار خيالِ المعنى عَنْ بُعْدِ لا بد في هذا من استشعار خيال وإجمالٍ حتّى يمكن الإلحاقُ : أنَّهُ نص صاحب الشرع صلوات الله عليه وعلى آلِهِ على أنَّ الثوبَ إذا أصابَهُ بولُ الصبي .. كفى الرس عليه (١) ، فلولا أنَّهُ ذكرَ أنَّ الصبيَّةَ بخلافِهِ . . لاكُنَّا نتسارعُ إِلَى أَنَّهَا في معناه ، ولكن لما ذكر الصبيَّة وأنَّها بخلافِهِ .. حسم علينا بابَ توهم المعنى . أما ترانا كيف نحكم بمنع المرأةِ مِنَ البول في الماء الراكدِ أخذاً مِنْ قولِهِ : « لَا يَبُولَنَّ أَحَدُكُمْ فِي الْمَاءِ الرَّاكِدِ » (۱) والخطاب معَ الرجال ؟! وإنْ خطرَ لكَ أنَّ النساء يدخلن تحت هذا الخطاب .. فقدِرْ أَنَّهُ لو قالَ لرجل : لا تبل في الماء الراكد .. للكُنَّا نقولُ ذلك للمرأةِ ؛ لعلمنا بأنَّ الشرعَ لم يفرّق بين فضلات بدنِ الرجال والنساء في النجاسات ، فما رأينا للأنوثة مدخلاً في النجاسات . وعُرِفَ ذلكَ مِنَ الشرِعِ ، وكان جرأتُنا على الشرع للإلحاق لهذا ولتوهُمِنا أنَّ البول مستقذرٌ ، وأنَّ الماء معد للتنظيف ، فلا ينبغي أن يختلط بهِ . فهذه أقسامُ الطريقِ الأَوَّلِ ، وهوَ أَلَّا يتعرض الملحقُ إِلَّا للحذف .
Compensating for the deficiency from the aspect that it is a deficiency, or from the aspect that it is an oversight. For if it is due to deficiency, then if he leaves something of the parts intentionally, he should prostrate; and if it is due to oversight, he does not prostrate in the case of intentional. And before the particularity of these attributes becomes clear to us, we know that 'Asr is in the meaning of Zuhr. As for the second aspect: it is that neither the root of the cause nor its description is specified, but we know it ambiguously among a number of meanings, as in the case of riba we may realize that raisins are in the meaning of dates before it becomes clear whether the cause is measure, taste, property, or sustenance; for we know that however it is, the raisin shares it with it, and does not differ from it except in being a raisin, and this should not affect at all. The evidence that there must be a sensing of the meaning from a distance — there must be in this a sensing of a meaning and a summary so that annexation is possible — is that the Lawgiver (peace be upon him and his family) stated that if a garment is struck by the urine of a boy, sprinkling suffices. Were it not that he mentioned that the girl is contrary to that, we would hasten to that she is in its meaning, but when he mentioned the girl and that she is contrary to that, he closed the door for us from imagining the meaning. Do we not see how we rule on preventing a woman from urinating in still water, taking from his saying: 'Let none of you urinate in still water', and the address is to men?! And if it occurs to you that women are included under this address, then consider that if he said to a man: Do not urinate in still water, we would say that to the woman, because we know that the Law did not differentiate between the excreta of men and women in impurities. We have not seen femininity having an entry in impurities. And that is known from the Law, and our courage towards the Law regarding annexation is due to this and our assumption that urine is filthy and that water is prepared for cleaning, so it should not be mixed with it. These are the divisions of the first method, which is that the annexer only addresses deletion.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines the first method of qiyas (al-tariq al-awwal), which is 'that the annexer only addresses deletion' (al-hazf). The author explains that when making an analogy, one may realize that the difference between two cases is irrelevant to the ruling, so one deletes that difference. For example, in the case of the urine of a boy, the hadith says that sprinkling water suffices, but for a girl it requires washing. This shows that the difference (gender) is relevant. However, elsewhere we see that for urinating in still water, the prohibition addressed to men is also applied to women because the impurity is the same; the difference in gender is deleted. Thus, the first method involves deleting the apparent distinguishing feature when it has no effect on the ruling. The author uses fiqh examples from hadith about urine and water to illustrate.

الملحق
الحذف

Key Takeaway
The first method of qiyas involves deleting the apparent difference between two cases when it has no effect on the ruling, allowing the analogy to hold.

Distinguishing Appropriate vs Effective Meanings in Qiyas
Author introduces the second method and its three divisions, with examples from fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an appropriate meaning (ma'na munasib) and an effective meaning (ma'na mu'aththir) in analogical reasoning (qiyas) and identify examples from Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
An appropriate meaning fits the ruling; an effective meaning actually produces it.

In the second method of qiyas, the jurist directly specifies the underlying meaning ('illa) that justifies the ruling. The author here introduces two types of such meanings: the 'appropriate' (munasib) and the 'effective' (mu'aththir). By understanding this distinction, you will see how jurists like Abu Hanifa constructed analogies without needing to discard dissimilarities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the method category
The passage begins by contrasting this 'second method' with the first (which used deletion of differences). Here, the considered meaning is specified explicitly.
2
Locate the two divisions
The author states there are three divisions but only two appear: (1) the meaning is appropriate (munasib), and (2) the meaning is effective (mu'aththir). Look for the Arabic terms 'munasib' and 'mu'aththir'.
The third division is missing in this text.
3
Analyse the munasib example
The example given for a munasib meaning is 'intoxication' (iskār) as a description for prohibition. Intoxication is appropriate to the prohibition of wine because it is a reason that fits the ruling.
Appropriateness (munasaba) means the meaning has a plausible connection to the ruling.
4
Analyse the mu'aththir example
The mu'aththir meaning is exemplified by a quote from Abu Hanifa, although it is cut off. An effective meaning is one that actually causes the ruling in the original case, such as a specific property that leads to a legal effect.
Effectiveness (ta'thīr) implies a stronger causal link than mere appropriateness.
5
Contrast the two types
A munasib meaning is suitable but may not be the sole cause; a mu'aththir meaning is one that, if present, necessitates the ruling. Both are used in qiyas but with different strengths of evidence.
In later usul, the mu'aththir is often considered a stronger 'illa.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage defines the second method of qiyas, where the analogy is made by directly stating the underlying meaning ('illa). The author lists three divisions, but only two are present in the text: (1) the meaning being appropriate (munasib) such as intoxication as the cause for prohibition; (2) the meaning being effective (mu'aththir) as exemplified by Abu Hanifa's statement regarding... (the example is cut off). This method contrasts with the first method which uses deletion. The third division is not included due to the incomplete text.

The Two Arts of Definition: Principles and Application
Author announces the second division on definition, its necessity, and its two arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the two parts of perception and explain why definition is necessary for grasping singular concepts, as well as distinguish the two arts of definition presented.

Core Idea
Definition (hadd) is the only path to knowledge of singular concepts; it has both theoretical rules and practical tests.

In this opening passage, the author sets the stage for the second major section of the book – on definition. He reminds us that perception divides into two parts, one of which is cognition of singulars (simple concepts), and asserts that such cognition is only attainable through definition. He then announces a two-part structure: first the principles and rules of definition, then their practical examinations. This mirrors the Islamic scholarly tradition of combining legal theory (usul) with its application (furu').

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about perception (idrak)
The author states that one of the two parts of perception is cognition (ma'rifa), which is knowledge of singulars (mufradat). Note that the other part (assent) is not named here, but the focus is on the first part.
The term 'mufradat' refers to simple, indivisible concepts like 'human' or 'white'.
2
Recognize the necessity of definition (hadd)
The author asserts that knowledge of singulars is attained only through definition: 'ذلك لا ينال إلا بالحد'. This is a foundational claim that justifies the entire second part of the book.
Definition here likely includes both hadd (essential definition) and rasm (descriptive definition).
3
Outline the two arts of definition
The author will present two arts (fannan): one about principles and rules (al-qawanin wa al-usul), and the other about examinations (al-imtihanat) of those rules. This is a theory-practice structure.
The word 'imtihanat' suggests testing the rules on real examples, much like legal casuistry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني من الكتاب في الحد قد ذكرنا أن أحد قسمي الإدراك هو المعرفة أعني العلم بالمفردات وأن ذلك لا ينال إلا بالحد فلنورد فيه فنين أحدهما ما يجري مجرى القوانين والأصول والآخر ما يجري مجرى الامتحانات للقوانين.
The second division of the book: on definition. We have mentioned that one of the two parts of perception is cognition, meaning knowledge of singulars, and that that is not attained except through definition. So we will present concerning it two arts: one of them running the course of principles and rules, and the other running the course of examinations to the rules.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the second major section of the book, which is devoted to definition (hadd). The author recalls a prior discussion about the two divisions of perception: one is tasawwur (conception) and the other is tasdiq (assent). Here, he equates 'knowledge of singulars' (al-'ilm bi al-mufradat) with cognition (al-ma'rifa), which is the grasp of simple concepts. He then asserts that such knowledge is only attainable through definition. The section will be split into two 'arts': the first sets forth the general rules and principles of definition, while the second applies these rules through practical examinations, much like testing laws. This structure mirrors the earlier pattern of theory followed by application.

Key Terms
الإدراك (al-idrak): Perception, which encompasses both conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Here it is divided into two parts. [Note: In later logic, idrak is often replaced by 'scientia' or 'ma'rifa' as a technical term for immediate grasp.]
المعرفة (al-ma'rifa): Cognition, identified as the knowledge of singular concepts (mufradat), i.e., simple terms without composition. [Note: In philosophical texts, ma'rifa sometimes implies experiential knowledge; here it is purely logical.]
المفردات (al-mufradat): Singulars, the objects of simple conception, e.g., 'human', 'white', as opposed to compound propositions.
الحد (al-hadd): Definition, the logical process of delimiting the essence of a thing through genus and differentia. [Note: In some later works, hadd is strictly opposed to rasm (description); here it may include both senses.]
القوانين والأصول (al-qawanin wa al-usul): Principles and rules, i.e., the theoretical guidelines for constructing valid definitions. [Note: Usul also refers to legal theory; here it is logical meta-rules.]
الامتحانات (al-imtihanat): Examinations or tests, i.e., practical applications of the definitional rules to real examples.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the only way to attain knowledge of singulars (al-mufradat)?
A) Through demonstration (burhan)
B) Through definition (hadd)
C) Through syllogism (qiyas)
D) Through examination (imtihan)

Key Takeaway
Definition is the only means to grasp simple concepts, and its study is divided into theoretical rules and practical tests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Four Demands: Hal and Ma
Author classifies the four types of demands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four demands (matlab) is being used in a given question and classify its subtype.

Core Idea
Every question in dialogue reduces to one of four demands: existence, description, verbal explanation, or essence.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational classification of questions in logic. Knowing the type of demand helps you understand what kind of answer is appropriate. We focus here on the two main demands: 'hal' (whether) and 'ma' (what), each with its subtypes.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two elements of any question
Every question has a demand (matlab) and a form (sigha). The form is the Arabic interrogative particle, like 'hal' or 'ma'.
The form indicates the type of demand.
2
Identify the demand 'hal' and its subtypes
The demand 'hal' asks either about pure existence (e.g., 'Is God existent?') or about a state/attribute (e.g., 'Is God the creator of humans?').
Both subtypes seek information about existence, but the second adds a qualification.
3
Identify the demand 'ma' and its three uses
The demand 'ma' can ask for: (1) a verbal explanation of an unfamiliar word, (2) a precise description using accidents/concomitants, or (3) the essential quiddity of the thing.
The same question 'What is X?' can be answered in three different ways.
4
Test your understanding with examples
Apply the classification to questions like 'هل الله موجود؟' (hal-existence) and 'ما الخمر؟' (ma-verbal or ma-descriptive or ma-essence depending on context).
The passage provides explicit examples for each case.

الفن الأول في القوانين القانون الأول في المطالب الأربعة وأنواع الحد أنَّ الحدَّ إِنَّما يذكرُ جواباً عن سؤال في المحاورات ، ولا يكونُ الحد جواباً عن كلّ سؤالٍ ، بَلْ عَنْ بَعضِهِ . والسؤالُ طلبٌ ، وله - لا محالة - مطلبٌ وصيغة ، والصيغ أمهات المطالب والمطالب كثيرةٌ ، ولكن أمهات المطالب أربع : المطلب الأوّلُ : مطلب ( هلْ ) : إذْ يُطلب بهذه الصيغة أمران : مطلب (هل) - إمَّا أصلُ الوجودِ : كقول القائل : هل الله موجود ؟ - أو يُطلب الموجود بحال وصفة : كقوله : هل الله خالق البشر ؟ وهل الله حي ؟ المطلب الثاني : مطلب ( ما ) : ويُطلق على ثلاثة أوجه : الأوّلُ : أن يُطلب به شرحُ اللفظ : كما يقولُ مَن لا يدري العُقار : ما العُقارُ ؟ فيُقالُ لَهُ : الخمرُ ؛ إذا كان يعرفُ لفظ الخمر الثاني : أن يطلب لفظاً محرّراً يتميَّرُ بِهِ المسؤول عنهُ عنْ غیره بكلام جامع مانع كيفما كانَ الكلام ، سواء كان عبارةً عَن لوازمِهِ ، أَوْ ذاتيَّاتِهِ ) : كقول القائل : ما الخمرُ ؟ أَوْ : ما حدُّ الخمرِ ؟ فيُقالُ : هوَ المائعُ الذي يقذفُ بالزبد ، ثمَّ يستحيلُ إلى الحموضة ، ويحفظ في الدن والمقصود : ألا يتعرَّضَ لذاتيَّاتِهِ ، ولكن يجمعُ مِنْ عوارضهِ ولوازمه ما يساوي بجملتِهِ الخمرَ ، بحيثُ لا يخرجُ عنه خمرٌ ، ولا يدخلُ فيهِ ما ليس بخمرٍ . والثالثُ : أن يطلب بهِ ماهيَّةَ الشيء وحقيقة ذاتِهِ : كقول القائل : ما الخمرُ ؟ فيُقالُ : ( هو شراب مسكر معتصَرٌ مِنَ العنب ) فيكون ذلك كاشفاً عنْ كُنْهِ حقيقتِهِ الذاتيّةِ ، ويتبعُهُ أيضاً أنَّهُ تمييز جامع مانع ، ولكن ليس المقصود التمييز ، بل تصورُ كُنْه الشيء وحقيقتِهِ ، ثمَّ التمييز يتبعه لا محالة . واسم الحدّ في العادةِ قد يُطلق على هذه الأجوبة الثلاثة على سبيل الاشتراك ، فلنخترع لكل واحد اسماً : ولنسم الأوّلَ : حداً لفظياً ؛ إذ السائل ليسَ يطلبُ إِلَّا شرحَ اللفظ . ولنسم الثاني : حدّاً رسمياً ؛ إذ هو طلب مترسّم بالعلم غيرِ متشوّف إلى دَرَكِ حقيقةِ الشيء .
The first art concerning the laws. The first law concerning the four demands and the types of definition. That definition is only mentioned as an answer to a question in dialogues, and definition is not an answer to every question, but only to some of them. The question is a request, and necessarily it has a demand and a form. The forms are the mothers of demands. And the demands are many, but the mothers of demands are four: The first demand: the demand 'hal' (whether): for by this form two things are sought: the demand 'hal' — either the essence of existence, like one saying: 'Is God existent?' — or the existent is sought by a state and attribute, like his saying: 'Is God the creator of humans?' and 'Is God living?' The second demand: the demand 'ma' (what): and it is applied in three ways: The first: that by it an explanation of the word is sought, as one who does not know 'al-'uqar' says: 'What is al-'uqar?' and it is said to him: 'Wine'; if he knows the word 'wine'. The second: that a precise expression is sought by which the thing asked about is distinguished from others by a comprehensive and exclusive statement, however that statement may be, whether it is an expression of its necessary concomitants or its essential attributes: like one saying: 'What is wine?' or 'What is the definition of wine?' and it is said: 'It is the liquid that produces foam, then turns to acidity, and is stored in the jar.' And the intent is not to touch on its essential attributes, but to gather from its accidents and necessary concomitants what collectively equals wine, such that no wine is excluded by it and nothing that is not wine enters it. The third: that by it the quiddity of the thing and the reality of its essence are sought: like one saying: 'What is wine?' and it is said: 'It is an intoxicating drink squeezed from grapes.' That then discloses the inner nature of its essential reality, and it follows that it is a comprehensive and exclusive distinction, but the intent is not distinction, rather the conceptualization of the inner nature of the thing and its reality; then distinction follows necessarily. And the term 'definition' (hadd) in common usage may be applied to these three answers by way of homonymy. So let us coin for each a name: Let us name the first: verbal definition (hadd lafzi); since the inquirer seeks only an explanation of the word. And let us name the second: descriptive definition (hadd rasmi); since it is a request that sketches by knowledge without aspiring to reach the reality of the thing.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage introduces the first law of logic: the classification of the four types of demands and, within that, the three types of definition. The author begins by stating that definition is only given in response to a certain kind of question in dialogue, not to every question. A question has a demand and a form (sigha), and the mothers of demands (the fundamental categories) are four. The first is the demand 'hal' (whether), which seeks either the existence of something (e.g., 'Is God existent?') or the existence of a thing with a certain attribute (e.g., 'Is God the creator?'). The second is the demand 'ma' (what), which is applied in three ways: First, it can seek a verbal explanation of an unfamiliar word, like someone asking 'What is al-'uqar?' and being told 'Wine' because he knows the word 'wine'. Second, it can seek a precise description that distinguishes the object from others, using a statement that is comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani'), whether it mentions essential attributes (dhatiyyat) or accidental necessary concomitants (lawazim). The author gives the example of describing wine as the liquid that foams, turns acidic, and is stored in jars; such a description picks out wine without using its essence. Third, the demand 'ma' can seek the quiddity (mahiyya) and essential reality (haqiqa dhatiyya) of the thing, as when one defines wine as 'an intoxicating drink squeezed from grapes'. This definition discloses the inner nature, and though it also distinguishes, distinction is not the primary goal—conceptualizing the essence is. Finally, the author notes that the term 'definition' (hadd) is commonly used for all three types by homonymy, and he coins distinct names: the first is 'verbal definition' (hadd lafzi), the second is 'descriptive definition' (hadd rasmi), and the third is 'real definition' (hadd haqiqi, implied). This classification is foundational for understanding the scope and purpose of definition in logic.

الصيغة
مطلب هل
مطلب ما

Hadd and Rasm

5 min
The Three Types of Definition: Lafzi, Rasmi, Haqiqi
Author classifies three types of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given definition as verbal (lafzi), descriptive (rasmi), or real (haqiqi) and explain the intent of each.

Core Idea
Definitions are not only about revealing essence; most are verbal or descriptive, suited to different contexts.

The author distinguishes three types of definition based on the inquirer's need: from simply clarifying a word, to distinguishing an object by its properties, to uncovering its true essence. This classification is crucial for using definitions correctly in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define verbal definition (hadd lafzi)
It replaces an unknown word with a known one, e.g., 'al-'uqar is wine'. It only clarifies the word, not the nature of the thing.
This is answering the demand 'ma' in its first use.
2
Define descriptive definition (hadd rasmi)
It uses accidents or necessary concomitants to comprehensively and exclusively identify the thing without revealing its essence, e.g., describing wine by its properties (foams, turns acidic, stored in jar).
It is the second use of 'ma'.
3
Define real definition (hadd haqiqi)
It states the essential attributes (genus and differentia) and discloses the quiddity, e.g., wine is 'an intoxicating drink squeezed from grapes'.
It is the third use of 'ma'.
4
Apply to the wine example
The passage gives three answers to 'What is wine?': verbal ('wine'), descriptive (liquid that foams...), and essential (intoxicating drink from grapes). Classify each type.
The same question can yield different definitions depending on what the inquirer needs.

The Three Types of Definition: Verbal, Descriptive, and Real
Author distinguishes three types of definition and elevates the real definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between verbal, descriptive, and real definitions, and identify the real definition as the only one that reveals the quiddity.

Core Idea
Only the real definition captures the essence; verbal and descriptive definitions are merely convenient shortcuts.

In this passage, the author introduces the second principle of definition: the need to distinguish essential from accidental. But first, he clarifies that there are three types of definition, and only one—the real definition—is the proper concern of the logician. The verbal definition is for common language, the descriptive definition is easy but imprecise, and the real definition is the difficult and precious one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of definition mentioned.
The author mentions verbal (lafzi), descriptive (rasmi), and real (haqiqi). Note that he says the definer should be perceptive of essential vs accidental, which pertains to the real definition.
2
Characterize each type.
Verbal definition is suitable for the simple of language; descriptive definition only gathers and prevents with any wording; real definition explains the ma‘na of mahiyya by mentioning complete constitutive meanings.
3
Determine which type is the focus of the author.
The author says 'the difficult and precious definition is the real one.' So the rest of the principle focuses on real definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القانون الثاني في ضرورة التفريق بين الذاتيات والعرضيات (١) أنَّ الحادَّ ينبغي أن يكونَ بصيراً بالفرْقِ بين الصفات الذاتية واللازمةِ والعَرَضِيةِ كما ذكرناه في الفنِ الأَوَّلِ من الكتاب أعني به : طالب الحد الحقيقي . (٢) أمَّا الأَوَّلُ اللفظي .. فيليقُ بساذج اللغة (٢) ، وأما الرسمي .. فمؤنته قليلةٌ ، والأمرُ فيهِ سهل ؛ فإنَّ طالبه قانع بالجمع والمنع بأي لفظ كانَ ، وإنَّما العويص العزيز الحد الذي سميناه : حقيقياً ، بیان معنى الماهية وليس ذلكَ إلا ذكر كمال المعاني التي بها قوامُ ماهيَّة الشيء وأعني بالماهيّةِ : ما يطلبُ القائلُ بقولِهِ : ( ما هوَ ) فإنَّ هذهِ صيغة طالب لحقيقة الشيء ، فلا يدخلُ في جواب الماهيَّةِ إِلَّا الذاتي والذاتي ينقسم : إلى عام ، ويُسمَّى : جنساً . وإلى خاص ، ويُسمَّى : نوعاً فإنْ كانَ الذاتي العام لا أعم منه . . سُمِّيَ : جنس الأجناس . وإن كانَ الذاتي الخاص لا أخص منه .. سُمّي : نوع الأنواع وهذا اصطلاح المنطقيين ، ولنصالحهم على هذا الاصطلاح فلا ضَيرَ فِيهِ ؛ فإِنَّهُ كالمستعمل أيضاً في علومنا . ومثاله : إذا قلنا : الجسمُ ينقسمُ : إلى نام ، وغيرِ نام ، والنامي تمثيل لأقسام ينقسم إلى حيوان ، وغيرِ حيوان ، والحيوان ينقسم : إلى عاقل وهو الإنسانُ ، وإلى غير عاقل . فالجسم جنس الأجناس ؛ إذْ لا أعم فوقَهُ . والإنسان نوع الأنواع ؛ إذ لا أخص تحتَهُ . والنامي نوع بالإضافة إلى الجسمِ ؛ لأنَّهُ أخص منه ، وجنس بالإضافة إلى الحيوانِ ؛ لأنَّهُ أعم منه ، وكذا الحيوان بين النامي الأعم ، وبين الإنسان الأخص فإن قيل : كيف لا يكون شيءٌ أعم مِنَ الجسم وقولنا : موجود تحريجة : نجد ما هو أعم وأخص ، وجوهرٌ .. أعم منه ؟ وكيف لا يكونُ شيءٌ أخصَّ مِنَ الإنسان فكيف ذلك ؟ وقولنا : شيخٌ وصبي ، وطويل وقصير ، وكاتب وحجَّامٌ .. أخص منه ؟ قلنا : لم نعن في هذا الاصطلاح بالجنس الأعم فقط ، بل عنينا العبرة بالذاتي لا باللازم أو العارض بهِ الأعم الذي هو ذاتي للشيء ؛ أي : هو داخل في جواب : ( ما هوَ ) بحيثُ لو بطلَ عَنِ الذهنِ التصديق بثبوتِه .. بطل المحدود وحقيقتُهُ عَنِ الذهنِ ، وخرجَ عَنْ كونِهِ مفهوماً للعقل .
The Second Principle: On the Necessity of Distinguishing between Essential and Accidental Attributes. (1) The definer ought to be perceptive of the distinction between essential, necessary, and accidental attributes, as we mentioned in the first art of the book—I mean the seeker of the real definition. (2) As for the verbal [definition], it befits the simple of language. As for the descriptive [definition], its burden is light and its matter is easy; for its seeker is content with gathering and preventing with any wording. Rather, the difficult and precious definition is the one we called 'real': the explanation of the meaning of quiddity (mahiyya), and that is nothing but the mention of the complete meanings by which the quiddity of a thing is constituted. I mean by 'quiddity': what the questioner seeks by his saying 'what is it?'; for this is a formula of one seeking the reality of a thing. Thus nothing enters into the answer to the quiddity except the essential (dhati). And the essential is divided: into universal, called 'genus'; and into particular, called 'species'. If the universal essential has nothing more universal than it, it is called 'genus of genera'. If the particular essential has nothing more particular than it, it is called 'species of species'. This is the terminology of the logicians, and let us agree with them on this terminology, for there is no harm in it; indeed it is also commonly used in our sciences. Its example: if we say: Body divides into growing and non-growing; growing—as a representation of divisions—divides into animal and non-animal; and animal divides into rational—which is human—and non-rational. Thus body is genus of genera, for there is nothing more universal above it. And human is species of species, for there is nothing more particular below it. And 'growing' is a species relative to body, because it is more particular than it, and a genus relative to animal, because it is more universal than it; and similarly animal is between 'growing' which is more universal and 'human' which is more particular. If it is said: How is there nothing more universal than body, given our saying 'existent'? A problematic [phrase]: we find what is more universal and more particular, and 'substance' is more universal than it? And how is there nothing more particular than human? How is that? Given our saying 'old' and 'young', 'tall' and 'short', 'scribe' and 'cupper' are more particular than it? We say: By this terminology we do not mean only the absolute most universal genus; rather we mean consideration of the essential, not the necessary or accidental. Thus the universal that is essential to a thing—i.e., it enters into the answer to 'what is it?'—such that if the mind's assent to its existence were to become invalid, the defined thing and its reality would become invalid from the mind, and it would exit from being a concept of the intellect.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory treatment of the second principle concerning the necessity of distinguishing essential from accidental attributes in the context of definition. The author begins by stating that the definer must be perceptive of the distinction between essential (dhatiyya), necessary (lazima), and accidental ('aradiyya) attributes, referencing earlier discussion. He then distinguishes three types of definition: verbal (lafzi), descriptive (rasmi), and real (haqiqi). The verbal definition is suited for common language, the descriptive is easy and satisfied with any wording that collects and prevents, but the real definition is the difficult and precious one: it is the explanation of the quiddity (mahiyya) by mentioning the complete meanings that constitute it. The author then defines quiddity as what is sought by the question 'what is it?' (ma huwa), and states that only the essential (dhati) enters into the answer. Next, he divides the essential into universal (al-dhati al-'amm), called genus (jins), and particular (al-dhati al-khass), called species (naw'). The most universal essential is called 'genus of genera' (jins al-ajnas), and the most particular essential is called 'species of species' (naw' al-anwa'). The author notes that this is the logicians' terminology, and it is acceptable as it is also used in the Islamic sciences. He gives an example with the hierarchy: body (jism) → growing (nami) → animal (hayawan) → human (insan). Here, body is genus of genera (no more universal essential above it), human is species of species (no more particular essential below it), and 'growing' and 'animal' are relative middles. The author then raises an objection: if body is the most universal essential, what about 'existent' (mawjud) or 'substance' (jawhar)? And if human is the most particular, what about 'old' (shaykh) or 'scribe' (katib)? The answer is that the criterion is not absolute universality or particularity, but essentiality. The essential is that which enters the answer to 'what is it?' and whose removal from the mind would negate the thing itself. Accidental attributes like 'existent' (which is a concomitant, not part of the quiddity) or 'old' (which is an accident) are not considered in that division. Thus the intended 'universal' and 'particular' in this terminology are restricted to essential attributes.

Quiddity (Mahiyya) and the Essential (Dhati): The Core of Real Definition
Author defines quiddity and the essential as the exclusive content of real definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define quiddity and essential, and explain why only essential attributes enter the answer to 'what is it?'.

Core Idea
Quiddity is what is sought by 'what is it?', and only essential attributes constitute it.

Having identified the real definition as the one that explains the quiddity, the author now defines quiddity (mahiyya) and the essential (dhati). He clarifies that the real definition is nothing but the mention of the complete meanings that constitute the quiddity. Quiddity is what the question 'ma huwa' seeks. And only the essential enters into the answer. This is a foundational concept in logic.

Guided Path
1
Define quiddity (mahiyya).
The author says: 'I mean by quiddity: what the questioner seeks by his saying "what is it?"' So quiddity is the essence or whatness of a thing.
2
Identify what enters the answer to quiddity.
The author states: 'Thus nothing enters into the answer to the quiddity except the essential (dhati).' So only essential attributes are constitutive.
3
Characterize the essential.
The essential is that which, if the mind's assent to it were invalidated, the defined thing would cease to be a concept of the intellect. This is the criterion of essentiality.

The Second Principle: On the Necessity of Distinguishing between Essential and Accidental Attributes. (1) The definer ought to be perceptive of the distinction between essential, necessary, and accidental attributes, as we mentioned in the first art of the book—I mean the seeker of the real definition. Thus nothing enters into the answer to the quiddity except the essential (dhati). And the essential is divided: into universal, called 'genus'; and into particular, called 'species'. Genus and Species: The Hierarchical Division of the Essential
Author divides essential into genus and species, provides hierarchy, and defends against objections using essential/accidental distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify genus and species in a hierarchy and explain why 'existent' and 'old' are not considered in the essential hierarchy.

Core Idea
Genus and species are relative terms based on essential attributes, not on any universal or particular quality.

After defining the essential, the author divides it into universal (genus) and particular (species). He gives a concrete example from the natural world: body, growing, animal, human. He then anticipates an objection: if body is the most universal genus, what about 'existent'? And if human is the most particular species, what about 'old' or 'scribe'? He resolves this by reemphasizing that the hierarchy is based on essential attributes only.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of essential.
The author divides essential into universal (al-dhati al-‘amm) called genus, and particular (al-dhati al-khass) called species. The most universal is genus of genera; the most particular is species of species.
2
Analyze the hierarchy example.
The example: body -> growing -> animal -> human. Body is genus of genera (most universal essential). Human is species of species (most particular essential). Growing and animal are relative: growing is species relative to body but genus relative to animal.
3
Consider the objection.
The objector says: How can body be most universal when 'existent' and 'substance' are more universal? And how can human be most particular when 'old' and 'scribe' are more particular?
4
Understand the author's response.
The author replies that the criterion is not absolute universality but essentiality. 'Existent' is a concomitant (lazim), not essential; 'old' is an accident (arad). They are not part of the quiddity. Only attributes that answer 'what is it?' count.

Existence vs. Essence: The Heptagon Test
Proof that existence is separate from essence via definition test.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a given attribute is part of the essence by checking if the concept survives its removal in thought.

Core Idea
If you can define it without mentioning existence, existence is not part of its essence.

In Islamic logic, one of the most fundamental distinctions is between essence (mahiyya) and existence (wujud). This passage uses a simple test: if you can understand the definition of a thing without knowing whether it actually exists, then existence is not a constituent of its essence. We will work through the author's example of the heptagon to internalise this criterion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The author uses 'heptagon' (musabba') as the example. What exactly is being defined?
2
Extract the definition
The definition given is 'a shape encompassed by seven sides'. Note the components: 'shape' (genus) and 'seven sides' (differentia).
3
Apply the thought experiment
Imagine someone who hears this definition but has no information about whether such a shape exists in reality. Can they still conceive of a heptagon? Yes. So existence is not needed.
4
Compare with a genuine part of essence
Contrast with 'shape': if you remove 'shape' from the definition, the concept collapses. Thus shape is part of the essence, existence is not.

وعلى هذا الاصطلاح فالوجود لا يدخل في الماهية ؛ إذ بطلانه عَنِ الذهن لا يوجب زوال الماهية بيانُهُ : إذا قال القائلُ : ما حدُّ المثلث ؟ فقلنا : شكل يحيط بهِ ثلاثة أضلاع ، أو قال : ما حد المسبع ؟ فقلنا : شكل يحيط به سبعة أضلاع .. فهم السائل حدَّ المسبَّعِ ولو لم يعلم أنَّ المسبَّعَ موجود أم لا في الحالِ ، فبطلان العلم بوجودِهِ لا يبطلُ مِنْ ذهنِهِ فهم حقيقةِ المسبَّعِ ، ولوْ بطلَ مِنْ ذهنِهِ الشكل .. لم يبقَ المسبَّعُ مفهوماً عنده فقد أدركتَ تفرقةً بينَ نسبةِ ( الوجودِ ) إليه ، وبين نسبة ( الشكل ( إليهِ ؛ إذْ زوالُ أحدِهِما عَنِ الذهن مبطل حقيقته ، وزوال الآخرِ غيرُ مبطل ، فهذا هو المراد بهذا الاصطلاح .
And according to this terminology, existence does not enter into the essence; because its negation from the mind does not necessitate the removal of the essence. Its explanation: If someone says: 'What is the definition of triangle?' and we say: 'A shape encompassed by three sides,' or he says: 'What is the definition of heptagon?' and we say: 'A shape encompassed by seven sides,' then the questioner understands the definition of the heptagon even if he does not know whether the heptagon exists or not at the moment. So the absence of knowledge of its existence does not remove from his mind the understanding of the reality of the heptagon. But if the shape is removed from his mind, the heptagon would not remain conceptually in his mind. Thus you perceive a difference between the relation of existence to it and the relation of shape to it; since the removal of one of them from the mind invalidates its reality, while the removal of the other does not invalidate. This is what is meant by this terminology.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a proof that existence is not part of the essence. The author uses the example of definitions of triangle and heptagon. If we define a triangle as a shape with three sides, and a heptagon as a shape with seven sides, the questioner understands the definition (essence) without needing to know whether such a thing actually exists. Hence, existence is not a constituent of the essence. In contrast, removing 'shape' from the definition would destroy the concept entirely. Thus existence is external to the essence, while shape is integral. This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic and metaphysics, as it underpins the discussion of the five universals and the nature of definitions.

Two Conditions for a Real Definition
Author states two necessary conditions for a real definition: including all essential parts and ordering from general to specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given definition meets the two conditions of a real definition: completeness of essential parts and proper ordering from general to specific.

Core Idea
A real definition requires all essential parts and general-before-specific order.

In Islamic logic, definitions are not mere descriptions; they must capture the essence. This passage lays down two fundamental conditions that any real definition must satisfy. Understanding these conditions will allow you to evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences for their logical rigor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum in the passage. The author uses the example of something that grows from the earth (a plant).
The definiendum is the subject of the definition.
2
Extract the genus and differentiae
Determine the broader category (genus) and the specific distinguishing attribute (differentia). In the plant example, 'body' is the genus and 'growing' is the differentia.
The genus must be broader than the species.
3
Check completeness of essential parts
Ensure all essential (dhati) attributes are included, not just accidental ones. The author stresses mentioning all essential parts, even if numerous.
Omitting an essential part makes the definition rasmi or lafzi.
4
Verify ordering from general to specific
Confirm that the more general term (genus) precedes the more specific term (differentia). For example, say 'body, growing' not 'growing body'.
Wrong order disrupts the logical arrangement.
5
Distinguish hadd haqiqi from rasmi/lafzi
If both conditions are met, the definition is real (hadd haqiqi). If one is missing, it becomes descriptive (rasmi) or verbal (lafzi).
This classification determines whether the definition captures the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القانون الثالث في كيفية صناعة الحد . أنَّ ما وقع السؤالُ عَن ماهِيَّتِهِ وأردت أن تحده حداً حقيقياً .. فعليكَ فيهِ وظائف لا يكونُ الحدُّ حقيقياً إلَّا بها ، فإن تركتها . سميناهُ رسميّاً أو لفظياً ، وخرجَ عَنْ كونِهِ مُعْرِباً عَنْ حقيقة الشيء ، ومصوّراً كُنْه معناه في نفس السائل : الحد الحقيقي الأَوَّلُ : أن تجمعَ أجزاء الحدِ مِنَ الجنس والفصول . فإذا قيلَ لكَ مشيراً إلى ما ينبتُ مِنَ الأرض : ما هوَ ؟ فلا بدَّ وأن تقول : ( جسم ) ، ولكن لو اقتصرت عليه .. بطل عليك بـ ( الحجر ) ، فتحتاجُ إلى الزيادةِ ، فتقولُ : ( نام ) ، فتحترزُ عمَّا لا ينمو ، فهذا الاحترازُ يُسمَّى : فَضلاً ؛ إِذْ فصلْتَ بهِ المحدود عَنْ غيرِهِ . ثمَّ شرطُكَ أن تذكر جميعَ ذاتيَّاتِهِ وإِنْ كانَتْ أَلفاً ، ولا تبالي بالتطويل . الثاني : ينبغي أن تقدّم الأعم على الأخص ، فلا تقولُ : نام جسم ، بل بالعكس . وهذا لو تركته .. لتشوَّسَ النظم ، ولم تخرج الحقيقةُ عَنْ
The third law concerning how to construct a definition. That of which the question about its essence is asked, and you want to define it with a real definition—upon you are functions without which the definition is not real; if you omit them, we call it a descriptive or verbal definition, and it exits from being expressive of the reality of the thing and from depicting the core of its meaning in the mind of the questioner. The first real definition: that you collect the parts of the definition from the genus and differentiae. So if you are told, pointing to something that grows from the earth: 'What is it?' you must say: 'a body,' but if you limit yourself to that, you are defeated by 'stone'; so you need to add, and you say: 'growing,' so you guard against what does not grow. This guarding is called a differentia, because you have thereby distinguished the defined from other than it. Then your condition is that you mention all its essential attributes, even if they are a thousand, and do not mind prolixity. The second: it is necessary to present the more general before the more specific, so do not say: 'growing body,' but the reverse. If you omit this, the arrangement becomes disordered, and the reality does not emerge from
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition on the method of constructing a real definition (hadd haqiqi). The author presents this as the 'third law' concerning definition-making. He begins by stating that when asked about the essence of something and you want to give a real definition, there are certain functions (waza'if) without which the definition is not real. If omitted, the result is merely a descriptive (rasmi) or verbal (lafzi) definition, failing to express the true reality or depict the core meaning. The first condition for a real definition is to assemble the parts of the definition from the genus (jins) and differentiae (fusul). He illustrates with an example: pointing to something that grows from the earth (a plant), you must first say 'body' (genus), but then add 'growing' (differentia) to exclude stones. This addition is called a differentia because it distinguishes the defined from others. Importantly, one must mention all essential (dhati) attributes, even if numerous. The second condition is to order the components from the more general (a'amm) to the more specific (akhass), so you say 'body, growing' not 'growing body'. Omitting this ordering disrupts the arrangement and fails to convey the reality. The example, though incomplete in this excerpt, clearly illustrates the priority of genus before differentia.

Key Terms
الحد الحقيقي (al-hadd al-haqiqi): A real definition that expresses the essence of a thing by combining its genus and differentiae. [Note: Stable across the tradition; contrasted with hadd rasmi (descriptive definition).]
الرسمي (al-rasmi): A descriptive definition that does not capture the full essence but suffices for identification. [Note: Stable.]
اللفظي (al-lafzi): A verbal definition that explains the meaning of a word, not the essence of the thing. [Note: Stable.]
الجنس (al-jins): Genus: the broader universal that is predicated of multiple species, e.g., 'body'. [Note: Stable.]
الفصل (al-fasl): Differentia: the specific attribute that distinguishes a species within its genus, e.g., 'growing'. [Note: Stable.]
الأعم (al-a'amm): The more general term, which should precede the more specific (al-akhass) in a definition. [Note: Stable.]

C) Body only
D) Growing only

Key Takeaway
A real definition must include all essential parts and order them from the most general to the most specific.

Order in Definition: Genus Before Differentia
Author explains the second condition: genus before differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly order the components of a real definition by placing the more general before the more specific, and identify the error of reversed order.

Core Idea
In a real definition, always say 'body growing' not 'growing body' – genus first, differentia second.

In defining something, the order of terms matters. The second condition for a valid definition is that the more general (genus) must precede the more specific (differentia). This ensures clarity and avoids verbal confusion. While a reversed order might still convey the meaning, it disrupts the logical order and is considered a fault, though less severe than omitting the differentia entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentia
Find which term is broader (genus) and which distinguishes (differentia). For example, in 'growing body', 'body' is the genus and 'growing' is the differentia.
2
Check the order
Ensure the genus comes before the differentia. Correct: 'body growing' (jism naami). Incorrect: 'growing body' (naami jism). In Arabic, the adjective follows the noun, so the genus is the noun and the differentia is the adjective.
This order is language-specific but the principle is universal: broader category first, then distinguishing feature.
3
Test with another example
Try defining 'human' as 'rational animal'. The genus is 'animal', differentia is 'rational'. Correct order: 'animal rational' (in Arabic: hayawan natiq). Incorrect: 'rational animal' (natiq hayawan).
If you reverse it, the definition becomes confusing and non-standard.
4
Evaluate the severity of the error
The author notes that reversing the order is a lesser fault than omitting the differentia entirely. If you say only 'body', that is a more serious error because it lacks the distinguishing feature.
So ordering is important, but completeness is even more critical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ينبغي أن تقدّم الأعم على الأخص ، فلا تقولُ : نام جسم ، بل بالعكس . وهذا لو تركته .. لتشوَّسَ النظم ، ولم تخرج الحقيقةُ عَنْ كونها مذكورةٌ معَ اضطراب اللفظ ، فالإنكار عليك في هذا أقلُّ ممَّا في الأوَّلِ ؛ وهوَ أنْ تقتصر على الجسم .
The second: you should present the more general before the more specific, so do not say 'growing body', but rather the opposite. If you omit this, the order becomes disordered, and the reality does not escape being mentioned with verbal confusion, so the censure upon you in this is less than in the first, which is to limit yourself to 'body'.

Commentary
This passage presents the second condition for a real definition: the order of components must place the more general (al-a'amm) before the more specific (al-akhass). The author illustrates with the incorrect ordering 'growing body' (naami jism) instead of the correct 'body growing' (jism naami). Violating this order causes confusion (tashawwush al-nazm) and verbal disorder. However, the author notes that this fault is less serious than the first condition's error, which is omitting the differentia altogether and only mentioning the genus.
