Choosing the Proximate Genus in Definitions
Author warns against mixing remote and proximate genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proximate genus for a given definiendum and avoid redundancy or insufficiency in definition.

Core Idea
The proximate genus is the closest class; using a remote genus makes the definition too broad or redundant.

When defining something, the first step is to place it in its immediate category. This lesson examines the author's warning against using a remote genus when a proximate one is available, as it either repeats information or fails to specify adequately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Look at the thing being defined, e.g., wine (خمر) or lion (أسد). This is the term whose essence you are trying to capture.
2
Find the closest immediate genus
Ask: what is the most specific category that contains all instances of the definiendum? For wine, it is 'beverage' (شراب), not 'liquid' or 'body'.
The genus must be the nearest class after which no more specific genus exists.
3
Check for redundancy with remote genus
If you mention both remote (e.g., 'liquid') and proximate ('beverage'), the remote is redundant because 'beverage' already implies 'liquid'.
Avoid saying 'liquid beverage' as in the passage.
4
Ensure the definition is not too broad
Using only the remote genus (e.g., 'body' for wine) makes the definition vague and fails to distinguish the definiendum from other bodies.
The author calls this 'mub'idan' (making it remote).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالثُ : أَنَّكَ إذا وجدت الجنس القريب .. فإيَّاكَ أنْ تذكر البعيد معَهُ ، فيكون مكرَّراً ؛ كما تقولُ : ( مائع شراب ) ، أو تقتصر على البعيد ، فيكون مبعداً ؛ كما إذا قيل : ما الخمرُ ؟ فلا تقل : ( جسم مسكر مأخوذٌ مِنَ العنب ) ، وإذا ذكرت هذا .. فقد ذكرْتَ ما هوَ ذاتي ومطرد ومنعكس ، ولكنَّه مختل قاصر عن تصورِ كُنْهِ حقيقة الخمرِ ، بل لو قلت : ( مائع مسكر ) .. كان أقرب مِنَ الجسم ، وهو أيضاً ضعيفٌ ، بل ينبغي أن تقول : (شراب مسكر ( فإِنَّهُ الأقربُ الأخصُ ، ولا تجد بعدَهُ جنساً أخص منه . فإذا ذكرْتَ الجنس .. فاطلب بعدَهُ الفصل ؛ إذ الشراب يتناول طلب الفصل بعد سائرَ الأشربةِ ، واجتهد أنْ تفصلَ بالذاتيّاتِ إِلَّا إِذا عسُرَ عليكَ في أكثر الحدودِ ، فاعدل بعد ذكرِ الجنس إلى ذكرِ اللوازم . واجتهد أن يكونَ ما ذكرتَهُ مِنَ اللوازم الظاهرة المعروفة ؛ فإِنَّ الخفيَّ لا يُعرَّفُ بهِ ؛ كما إذا قيلَ : ما الأسدُ ؟ قلت : ( سبع أبخرُ ) لتميُّزه بالبَخَرِ عَنِ الكلبِ ، فإِنَّ البَخَرَ مِنْ حَواصِهِ ، ولكنَّهُ مِنَ الخواص الخفيَّةِ ، ولو قلت : ( شجاع عريض الأعالي ) .. لكانَتْ الجنس، وإلا فاللوازم
The third: that when you find the proximate genus, beware of mentioning the remote with it, lest it be redundant; as you say 'liquid beverage', or limit yourself to the remote, in which case it is remote; as when it is said 'what is wine?', do not say 'a body, an intoxicant, taken from grapes'. If you mention this, you have mentioned what is essential, constant, and coextensive, but it is disordered and deficient in depicting the reality of wine. Rather, if you said 'a liquid intoxicant', it is closer than 'body', but still weak. Rather, you should say 'an intoxicating beverage', for it is the nearest and most specific, and after it you will not find a more specific genus. When you have mentioned the genus, seek after it the differentia, for 'beverage' includes other beverages, and strive to differentiate by essential properties unless it is difficult for you in most definitions, then after mentioning the genus turn to mentioning necessary properties. And strive that what you mention of properties be apparent and well-known, for the hidden is not defined by it; as when it is said 'what is a lion?', you say 'a beast that has halitosis' to distinguish it from the dog by halitosis, for halitosis is among its properties, but it is among the hidden properties. And if you said 'courageous, with broad shoulders', that would be the genus, otherwise the properties.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the third condition for a real definition. The author warns against two errors when the proximate genus (al-jins al-qarib) is known: (1) mentioning the remote genus (al-ba'id) along with it, causing repetition (e.g., 'liquid beverage' since liquid is a genus of beverage? Actually 'liquid' is more general than 'beverage', so mentioning both is redundant); (2) limiting oneself to the remote genus alone, which is insufficiently specific (e.g., defining wine as 'an intoxicating body taken from grapes' is too broad and does not capture the essence). The correct approach is to use the proximate genus, such as 'intoxicating beverage' (sharab muskir). After the genus, one must add the differentia (fasl), ideally an essential attribute (dhatiyya). However, when essential differentiae are difficult to identify, one may resort to necessary properties (lawaazim) that are well-known and apparent. The author gives an example: defining a lion as 'a beast with halitosis' (sabu' abkhar) uses a hidden property and is thus weak; a better description would be 'courageous, with broad shoulders' (shuja' 'arid al-a'ali), which is more apparent.

الجنس البعيد
اللوازم

Using Well-Known Properties when Essential Differentiae are Unavailable
Author instructs to use essential differentiae or, if difficult, well-known properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential differentiae and necessary properties, and apply the condition that properties used in definitions must be well-known and apparent.

Core Idea
When essence is hard to define, rely on evident properties; hidden properties defeat the purpose of definition.

Sometimes the essential differentia (fasl dhati) of a thing is difficult to articulate. In such cases, the author permits using necessary properties (lawaazim) that are coextensive with the definiendum. However, these properties must be well-known and apparent, not obscure.

Guided Path
1
Determine if an essential differentia is available
First, try to find an attribute that is part of the essence of the definiendum and distinguishes it from all other species within the same genus.
Essential differentia is preferred because it reveals the true nature.
2
If not, identify necessary properties
Necessary properties (lawaazim) are attributes that always accompany the essence but are not constitutive of it. They must be coextensive (present in every instance and only those).
Example: 'walking on land' for lion is a necessary property but not essential.
3
Select well-known and apparent properties
The property chosen should be obvious to the intended audience. Avoid hidden or technical properties that require expertise to recognize.
The author criticizes defining a lion by 'halitosis' (bad breath) because it is hidden, even if true.
4
Check that the property is distinctive
The property must be unique to the definiendum or at least distinguish it from other members of the genus. For lion, 'courageous, broad-shouldered' is more apparent and distinctive than halitosis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالثُ : أَنَّكَ إذا وجدت الجنس القريب .. فإيَّاكَ أنْ تذكر البعيد معَهُ ، فيكون مكرَّراً ؛ كما تقولُ : ( مائع شراب ) ، أو تقتصر على البعيد ، فيكون مبعداً ؛ كما إذا قيل : ما الخمرُ ؟ فلا تقل : ( جسم مسكر مأخوذٌ مِنَ العنب ) ، وإذا ذكرت هذا .. فقد ذكرْتَ ما هوَ ذاتي ومطرد ومنعكس ، ولكنَّه مختل قاصر عن تصورِ كُنْهِ حقيقة الخمرِ ، بل لو قلت : ( مائع مسكر ) .. كان أقرب مِنَ الجسم ، وهو أيضاً ضعيفٌ ، بل ينبغي أن تقول : (شراب مسكر ( فإِنَّهُ الأقربُ الأخصُ ، ولا تجد بعدَهُ جنساً أخص منه . فإذا ذكرْتَ الجنس .. فاطلب بعدَهُ الفصل ؛ إذ الشراب يتناول طلب الفصل بعد سائرَ الأشربةِ ، واجتهد أنْ تفصلَ بالذاتيّاتِ إِلَّا إِذا عسُرَ عليكَ في أكثر الحدودِ ، فاعدل بعد ذكرِ الجنس إلى ذكرِ اللوازم . واجتهد أن يكونَ ما ذكرتَهُ مِنَ اللوازم الظاهرة المعروفة ؛ فإِنَّ الخفيَّ لا يُعرَّفُ بهِ ؛ كما إذا قيلَ : ما الأسدُ ؟ قلت : ( سبع أبخرُ ) لتميُّزه بالبَخَرِ عَنِ الكلبِ ، فإِنَّ البَخَرَ مِنْ حَواصِهِ ، ولكنَّهُ مِنَ الخواص الخفيَّةِ ، ولو قلت : ( شجاع عريض الأعالي ) .. لكانَتْ الجنس، وإلا فاللوازم
The third: that when you find the proximate genus, beware of mentioning the remote with it, lest it be redundant; as you say 'liquid beverage', or limit yourself to the remote, in which case it is remote; as when it is said 'what is wine?', do not say 'a body, an intoxicant, taken from grapes'. If you mention this, you have mentioned what is essential, constant, and coextensive, but it is disordered and deficient in depicting the reality of wine. Rather, if you said 'a liquid intoxicant', it is closer than 'body', but still weak. Rather, you should say 'an intoxicating beverage', for it is the nearest and most specific, and after it you will not find a more specific genus. When you have mentioned the genus, seek after it the differentia, for 'beverage' includes other beverages, and strive to differentiate by essential properties unless it is difficult for you in most definitions, then after mentioning the genus turn to mentioning necessary properties. And strive that what you mention of properties be apparent and well-known, for the hidden is not defined by it; as when it is said 'what is a lion?', you say 'a beast that has halitosis' to distinguish it from the dog by halitosis, for halitosis is among its properties, but it is among the hidden properties. And if you said 'courageous, with broad shoulders', that would be the genus, otherwise the properties.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the third condition for a real definition. The author warns against two errors when the proximate genus (al-jins al-qarib) is known: (1) mentioning the remote genus (al-ba'id) along with it, causing repetition (e.g., 'liquid beverage' since liquid is a genus of beverage? Actually 'liquid' is more general than 'beverage', so mentioning both is redundant); (2) limiting oneself to the remote genus alone, which is insufficiently specific (e.g., defining wine as 'an intoxicating body taken from grapes' is too broad and does not capture the essence). The correct approach is to use the proximate genus, such as 'intoxicating beverage' (sharab muskir). After the genus, one must add the differentia (fasl), ideally an essential attribute (dhatiyya). However, when essential differentiae are difficult to identify, one may resort to necessary properties (lawaazim) that are well-known and apparent. The author gives an example: defining a lion as 'a beast with halitosis' (sabu' abkhar) uses a hidden property and is thus weak; a better description would be 'courageous, with broad shoulders' (shuja' 'arid al-a'ali), which is more apparent.

Key Terms
اللوازم
الذاتيات
الخواص الخفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Halitosis is a hidden property
C) It is not coextensive with lions

Key Takeaway
When essential differentiae are unavailable, use necessary properties, but ensure they are apparent and well-known to the audience.

Third Rule: Use Proximate Genus and Proper Differentiae
Author explains the third rule of definition, emphasizing the proximate genus and avoiding repetition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a correct definition by selecting the proximate genus and appropriate differentia, and distinguish between a true definition (hadd) and a description (rasm).

Core Idea
The best definitions use the immediate genus and a clear differentia, not hidden properties.

In this passage, al-Ghazali explains the third rule of definition: once you have found the proximate genus, do not mention a distant genus alongside it, as that would be redundant or too remote. Instead, pair the nearest genus with a distinguishing differentia. He illustrates with the definition of wine and the lion, showing that hidden properties are ineffective. He also acknowledges that most definitions in practice are descriptive (rasmiyyat) because essential definitions are extremely difficult.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proximate genus in the wine example
Read the passage to find which genus the author considers 'nearest' for wine. The author says 'intoxicating beverage'—'beverage' is the genus.
Observe that 'liquid' is wider than 'beverage', so 'beverage' is more specific and thus preferable.
2
Extract the differentia
After the genus, the author adds 'intoxicating' as the differentia. This distinguishes wine from other beverages.
The differentia must be essential, not accidental.
3
Compare with the faulty definitions
The passage rejects definitions like 'a body, intoxicating, taken from grapes' because 'body' is too distant, and 'intoxicating liquid' is closer but still not optimal.
The best combines the closest genus with the differentia.
4
Understand when to use necessary properties (lawazim)
If defining by essential attributes is too difficult, the author permits using well-known necessary properties, as in the lion example where 'brave with broad shoulders' is clearer than 'carnivore with halitosis'.
Prefer apparent properties over hidden ones for clarity.
5
Distinguish between hadd and rasm
Note the author's remark that most definitions in books are descriptive (rasm), not true essential definitions (hadd), due to the difficulty of grasping all essential attributes.
The best rasm includes the proximate genus and well-known properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : أَنَّكَ إذا وجدت الجنس القريب فإيَّاكَ أنْ تذكر البعيد معَهُ، فيكون مكرَّراً؛ كما تقولُ: (مائع شراب)، أو تقتصر على البعيد، فيكون مبعداً؛ كما إذا قيل: ما الخمرُ؟ فلا تقل: (جسم مسكر مأخوذٌ مِنَ العنب)، وإذا ذكرت هذا فقد ذكرْتَ ما هوَ ذاتي ومطرد ومنعكس، ولكنَّه مختل قاصر عن تصورِ كُنْهِ حقيقة الخمرِ، بل لو قلت: (مائع مسكر) كان أقرب مِنَ الجسم، وهو أيضاً ضعيفٌ، بل ينبغي أن تقول: (شراب مسكر) فإِنَّهُ الأقربُ الأخصُ، ولا تجد بعدَهُ جنساً أخص منه. فإذا ذكرْتَ الجنس فاطلب بعدَهُ الفصل؛ إذ الشراب يتناول سائرَ الأشربةِ، واجتهد أنْ تفصلَ بالذاتيّاتِ إِلَّا إِذا عسُرَ عليكَ في أكثر الحدودِ، فاعدل بعد ذكرِ الجنس إلى ذكرِ اللوازم. واجتهد أن يكونَ ما ذكرتَهُ مِنَ اللوازم الظاهرة المعروفة؛ فإِنَّ الخفيَّ لا يُعرَّفُ بهِ؛ كما إذا قيلَ: ما الأسدُ؟ قلت: (سبع أبخرُ) لتميُّزه بالبَخَرِ عَنِ الكلبِ، فإِنَّ البَخَرَ مِنْ خواصِّهِ، ولكنَّهُ مِنَ الخواص الخفيَّةِ، ولو قلت: (شجاع عريض الأعالي) لكانَتْ هذه اللوازم والأعراضُ أقرب إلى التفهيم؛ لأنَّها أجلى. وأكثر ما يُرى في الكتب مِنَ الحدود رسمية؛ إذ الحقيقة عزيزة؛ إِذْ دَرَكُ الذاتيَّاتِ حتَّى لا يشذَّ واحدٌ عسير، والتَّمييز بين جميع الذاتي واللازمِ عسير، ورعايةُ الترتيب حتَّى لا تبتدئ بالأخص قبلَ الأعمّ عسير، وطلب الجنس الأقرب عسير؛ فإِنَّكَ ربَّما تقولُ في الأسد: إنَّهُ حيوان شجاع، ولا يحضرُكَ لفظ السبع، فتجتمعُ أنواع مِنَ العُسْرِ. وأحسنُ الرسميَّاتِ: ما وُضعَ فيه الجنس الأقربُ وأُتمَّ بالخواص المشهورة المعروفة.
And the third [rule]: When you find the proximate genus, beware of mentioning the distant genus with it, for it would be redundant; as you say: 'a liquid drink', or you restrict yourself to the distant genus, so it becomes remote; as if it is said: What is wine? Do not say: 'a body, intoxicating, taken from grapes.' And when you mention this, you have mentioned what is essential, coextensive, and convertible, but it is defective, falling short of grasping the true nature of wine. Rather, if you say: 'an intoxicating liquid,' it is closer than 'body,' but it is also weak. Rather, you should say: 'an intoxicating beverage,' for it is the nearest and most specific, and you will not find a more specific genus after it. So when you mention the genus, then after it seek the differentia; for 'beverage' encompasses all drinks. Strive to differentiate by essential attributes, unless it becomes difficult for you in most definitions, then after mentioning the genus, turn to mentioning the necessary properties. And strive that what you mention of the necessary properties be apparent and well-known; for the hidden cannot be defined by it; as if it is said: What is the lion? You say: 'a carnivore with halitosis,' distinguishing it from the dog by halitosis. For halitosis is among its properties, but it is among hidden properties. If you said: 'a brave one with broad shoulders,' these properties and accidents are closer to making understood because they are clearer. Most definitions seen in books are descriptive; for the true definition is rare; because grasping the essential attributes without any omission is difficult, and distinguishing between all essential and necessary attributes is difficult, and observing the order so that you do not begin with the more specific before the more general is difficult, and seeking the nearest genus is difficult; for perhaps you say about the lion: 'It is a brave animal,' and the word 'carnivore' does not occur to you, so various kinds of difficulty accumulate. The best of descriptive definitions is that in which the nearest genus is placed and completed by well-known, familiar properties.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage elaborates the third rule of definition: when you have found the proximate genus, you must not mention the distant genus alongside it, as that would be redundant. The author illustrates with the definition of wine (khamr). If one says 'a body, intoxicating, taken from grapes', the genus 'body' is too remote, and the definition is defective because it does not capture the true essence. A better definition is 'an intoxicating beverage'—'beverage' (sharab) is the proximate genus, and 'intoxicating' (muskir) is the differentia. The author stresses that after giving the genus, one should seek the differentia (fasl). If defining by essential attributes becomes too difficult, one may resort to using necessary properties (lawazim). The example of the lion shows that hidden properties (like halitosis) are not effective; better to use apparent characteristics like bravery and broad shoulders. The passage then notes that most definitions in books are actually descriptive (rasmiyya) rather than true definitions (hadd), because achieving a perfect essential definition is extremely challenging. The best descriptive definition includes the nearest genus and completes it with well-known properties.

Key Terms
الجنس القريب (al-jins al-qarib): The immediate genus that is directly above the species in the hierarchy, closest to the defined thing. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across logic texts.]
الفصل (al-fasl): The differentia that distinguishes the species from other species within the same genus, here exemplified by 'intoxicating' for wine. [Note: No significant shift.]
الرسميات (al-rasmiyyat): Descriptive definitions that use proximate genus and well-known properties, as opposed to true essential definitions (hadd). [Note: Ghazali emphasizes the prevalence of rasm due to difficulty of hadd; this is a pragmatic stance.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A body, intoxicating, taken from grapes.
B) An intoxicating liquid.
C) An intoxicating beverage.
D) A drink that is fermented.

Key Takeaway
The third rule requires using the closest possible genus and a distinguishing differentia, resorting to well-known properties only when essential attributes are too difficult.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Fourth Rule of Definition: Choosing Clear Words
Author sets the fourth rule: avoid strange, metaphorical, and ambiguous words; seek brevity and clarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the fourth rule of definition by evaluating definitions for clarity, identifying problematic words, and determining when metaphor is permissible and how to ensure it remains clear.

Core Idea
Clear definitions use literal words; metaphor is only a backup when no literal word exists.

The fourth rule of definition focuses on word choice: avoid strange, far-fetched metaphorical, and ambiguous terms. But the author also recognizes that not every concept has a clear literal expression, so metaphor is permitted under strict conditions. This lesson will give you a practical method to apply this rule when defining terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Scan the definition for problematic words
Look for strange (gharib), far-fetched metaphorical (majaz ba'id), or ambiguous (mushtarak mutaraddid) words. Write down any you find.
Strange words are unfamiliar to the audience; far-fetched metaphors obscure meaning; ambiguous words have multiple possible interpretations.
2
Check for a literal alternative
For each problematic word, ask: Is there a clear, literal word (nass) that could replace it? If yes, the definition should use that instead.
Literal words are preferred because they are unambiguous and directly convey meaning.
3
Evaluate necessity and appropriateness of metaphor
If no literal word exists, identify the metaphor used. Is it the most appropriate for the purpose? Does the definer clarify the intended meaning?
The author permits metaphor only when necessary, and it must be accompanied by explanation to avoid confusion.
4
Determine if the definition reveals the essence
Even if metaphor is used, does the definition successfully mention all essential attributes (dhatiyyat)? If yes, the definition is acceptable.
The ultimate goal is to reveal the true nature (kashf al-haqiqa); as long as that is achieved, the means are permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن تحترزَ عَنِ الألفاظ الغريبة الوحشيَّةِ، والمجازية البعيدة، والمشتركة المتردّدة. واجتهد في الإيجاز ما قدرت، وفي طلب اللفظ النص ما أمكنكَ، فإنْ أعوزك النص، وافتقرت إلى الاستعارة فاطلب مِنَ الاستعارات ما هو أشدُّ مناسبة للغرض، واذكر مرادَكَ بهِ للسائل، فما كلُّ أمر معقولٍ له عبارةٌ صريحة موضوعةٌ، فلا إنكار على من جوز المجاز في التعريف عند الضرورة، أو أتى بلفظ مشترك وعُرف مراده بالتصريح، أو عُرف بالقرينة فلا ينبغي أن يُستعظم صنيعه ويبالغ في ذمِّهِ إنْ كانَ قد كشف الحقيقة بذكر جميع الذاتيات.
The fourth [rule]: that you guard against strange, uncouth words, and far-fetched metaphorical ones, and ambiguous, equivocal ones. And strive for brevity as much as you can, and in seeking the literal word as much as possible. If the literal word fails you and you have need for metaphor, then seek among metaphors the one most appropriate for the purpose, and mention your intended meaning to the questioner. For not every intelligible matter has a clear, literal expression; so there is no objection to one who permits metaphor in definition when necessary, or who uses an equivocal word but makes his meaning known by explicit statement, or is known by context. So one should not consider his action grave nor exaggerate in blaming him, if he has revealed the truth by mentioning all essential attributes.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth rule of definition: avoid strange, uncouth words, far-fetched metaphors, and ambiguous terms. The author advises seeking brevity and using the literal word (nass) whenever possible. If a literal expression is not available, one may resort to metaphor (isti'ara), but must choose the metaphor that is most appropriate and clarify the intended meaning to the questioner. The rule is grounded in the fact that not every concept has a clear, literal expression. Therefore, if someone uses metaphor out of necessity, or uses an ambiguous word but clarifies his meaning either explicitly or through context, his action should not be condemned as long as he has successfully conveyed the essential attributes. This shows a pragmatic approach: the goal is to reveal the true nature (kashf al-haqiqa), and as long as that is achieved, the means are permissible within reasonable bounds.

Key Terms
الألفاظ الغريبة
الاستعارة
الإيجاز

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Real Definitions Are Rare in Islamic Logic
Author discusses the difficulty of real definitions and the prevalence of rasm definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four difficulties that make real definitions rare and identify the key components of an optimal rasm definition.

Core Idea
Real definitions are rare because essences are hard to grasp; rasm definitions are practical and widespread.

In this passage, al-Ghazali confronts a practical reality: most definitions you encounter in Islamic scholarly works are not real definitions (hadd haqiqi) but descriptions (rasm). He explains why — because capturing the complete essence and distinguishing it from necessary properties is extremely difficult. He then tells us what makes a rasm definition effective.

Guided Path
1
Contrast real vs. rasm
Al-Ghazali states real definitions (hadd haqiqi) are 'aziza' (rare). Most definitions in books are rasm. Why? Because rasm relies on clearer, observable properties (lawaazim and a'raad) which are easier to grasp than hidden essences.
Note the shift: logicians aim for real definitions, but in practice rasm dominates.
2
Identify the four difficulties
Al-Ghazali lists four reasons real definitions are rare: (1) grasping ALL essential properties without missing any, (2) distinguishing essential from concomitant properties, (3) ordering properties from more general to more specific, (4) finding the nearest genus.
These are epistemological and methodological challenges, not just theoretical.
3
Examine the lion example
He illustrates the difficulty of finding the nearest genus: when defining 'lion', one might say 'courageous animal' because the more precise genus 'sab' (beast of prey) does not come to mind. This shows how even a simple definition can fail to hit the nearest genus.
The example shows that practical definition often settles for a more general genus, sacrificing precision for clarity.
4
Determine the best rasm
Al-Ghazali concludes the best rasm includes two elements: (a) the nearest genus (jins aqrab) and (b) well-known distinctive properties (khawass). This balances accuracy with recognizability.
This is a practical guideline for constructing definitions in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفاوت الحــ الرسمية
هذه اللوازم والأعراضُ أقرب إلى التفهيم ؛ لأنَّها أجلى . وأكثر ما يُرى في الكتب مِنَ الحدود رسمية ؛ إذ الحقيقة عزيزة ؛ إِذْ دَرَكُ الذاتيَّاتِ حتَّى لا يشذَّ واحدٌ . . عسير ، والتمييز بين جميع الذاتي واللازمِ عسير ، ورعايةُ الترتيب حتَّى لا تبتدئ بالأخص قبلَ الأعمّ عسير ، وطلب الجنس الأقرب عسير ؛ فإِنَّكَ ربَّما تقولُ في الأسد : إنَّهُ حيوان شجاع ، ولا يحضرُكَ لفظ السبع ، فتجتمعُ أنواع مِنَ العُسْرِ .
وأحسنُ الرسميَّاتِ : ما وُضعَ فيه الجنس الأقربُ وأُتمَّ بالخواص المشهورة المعروفة
The variation of rasm definitions. These necessary concomitants and accidents are closer to making understood because they are clearer. Most of what is seen in books of definitions are rasm; for the real definition is rare. Indeed, grasping the essential properties such that no one is omitted is difficult, and distinguishing between all essential and concomitant is difficult, and observing the order so that you do not begin with the more specific before the more general is difficult, and seeking the nearest genus is difficult. For you might say of the lion: 'It is a courageous animal,' and the word 'sab' does not occur to you, so various types of difficulty accumulate. And the best of rasm definitions is that in which the nearest genus is placed and completed with well-known, famous properties.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of definitions in logic, specifically the distinction between real (haqiqi) and nominal (rasm) definitions. The author, al-Ghazali, begins by noting that the necessary concomitants (lawaazim) and accidents (a'raad) are more effective for conveying meaning because they are clearer. He observes that most definitions found in books are actually rasm (nominal definitions) because the real definition (hadd haqiqi) is rarely achieved. The reason is the difficulty of comprehending all essential properties (dhatiyyat) without missing any, distinguishing them from necessary concomitants (lazim), maintaining the correct order of genera and species (starting with the more general before the more specific), and identifying the nearest genus (jins aqrab). Al-Ghazali gives the example of defining a lion: one might say 'a courageous animal' without recalling the nearest genus 'sab' (beast of prey). This illustrates the practical difficulty. He then states that the best type of rasm definition is one that includes the nearest genus and is completed with well-known, distinctive properties (khawass). This passage is important because it acknowledges the limitations of human knowledge and the practical reliance on rasm definitions in the Islamic sciences, such as in fiqh and kalam where real essences are often unknown.

Key Terms
حد رسمي
ذاتي
لازم
جنس أقرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Rasm definitions rely on clearer, more accessible properties.
D) Islamic scholars dislike essential definitions.

Key Takeaway
Real definitions are rare due to four epistemic difficulties, so practical definitions in Islamic sciences often use rasm, optimally including the nearest genus and well-known properties.

Metaphor and Context in Definitions: Al-Ghazali's Pragmatic Approach
Al-Ghazali permits metaphor and ambiguous terms in definitions when context clarifies meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a definition using metaphor or ambiguous terms is acceptable according to al-Ghazali's criteria.

Core Idea
The goal of definition is understanding, not pedantic literalism.

In this passage, al-Ghazali tackles the question of whether definitions must always use perfectly explicit terms. He argues that when necessity demands, metaphor and even ambiguous terms are acceptable, provided the context clarifies the intended meaning. This lesson will show you how to apply his criteria to real definitions from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definition
Find the term being defined and the words used to define it. For example, in the passage, 'ilm is defined as 'thiqah bi al-ma'lum'.
2
Check for explicitness
Determine whether the definiens uses an explicit word (naṣṣ) or a metaphor/ambiguous term (majaz/mushtarak). 'Thiqah' is ambiguous; it can mean confidence or trustworthiness.
3
Assess the context (qarīna)
Look for contextual clues that resolve the ambiguity. In 'thiqah bi al-ma'lum', the phrase 'bi al-ma'lum' connects confidence to the known, making clear it means cognitive confidence, not trustworthiness.
4
Apply al-Ghazali's criteria
Check if the definition mentions all essential properties (dhātiyyāt). If it does, and any ambiguity is resolved by context, the definition is acceptable.
Al-Ghazali gives the example of 'lawn' defined as 'what is perceived by the sense of sight' – 'ayn' alone is ambiguous, but 'hāssah' specifies sense of sight.
5
Distinguish goal from means
Identify any objections that focus on wording rather than meaning. Al-Ghazali calls such objectors 'mutahadhiqūn' (pretentious pedants) whose criticism is irrelevant.
The goal (maqsūd) is understanding (tafhīm), not the superficial form of words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجاز في التعريف .
عند الضرورة
الرابع : : أن تحترزَ عَنِ الألفاظ الغريبة الوحشيَّةِ ، والمجازية البعيدة ، والمشتركة المتردّدة .
تجویز دخول واجتهد في الإيجاز ما قدرت ، وفي طلب اللفظ النص ما أمكنكَ ، فإنْ أعوزك النص ، وافتقرت إلى الاستعارة .. فاطلب مِنَ الاستعارات ما هو أشدُّ مناسبة للغرض ، واذكر مرادَكَ بهِ للسائل ،
على من تجوز
فما كلُّ أمر معقولٍ له عبارةٌ صريحة موضوعةٌ
المقصود فهـــم ولو طول مطوّل أو استعار مستعير أو أتى بلفظ مشترك وعُرف الحدود ، فلا إنكار مراده بالتصريح ، أو عُرف بالقرينة .. فلا ينبغي أن يُستعظم صنيعه ويبالغ في ذمّهِ إنْ كانَ قد كشف الحقيقة بذكر جميع الذاتيات ؛
فإنَّهُ المقصود ، وهذه المزايا تحسينات وترتيبات ؛ كالأبازيرِ مِنَ
المقصود
وإنَّما المتحذلقونَ يستعظمونَ مثل ذلك ، ويستنكرونه غايةَ الاستنكار ؛ لميل طباعِهِمُ القاصرةِ عَنِ المقصودِ الأصلي إلى الوسائل والتوابع ، حتَّى ربَّما أنكروا قول القائل في حدّ العلم : ، ( إنَّهُ الثقة بالمعلوم، أو إدراك المعلوم ) من حيث إن الثقة مرددة " بين العلم والأمانةِ ، وهذا المعترِضُ مُهَوَّسٌ ؛ فَإِنَّ الثقةَ إِذَا قُرِنَتْ بالمعلوم .. تعيَّن فيه جهة الفهم
ومَنْ قالَ : ( حد اللون : ما يُدرك بحاسة العين على وجه كذا وكذا ) . . فلا ينبغي أن يُنكر هذا لأنَّ العينَ قدْ يُراد بها الذهب والشمس ؛ فإِنَّهُ معَ قرينةِ الحاسَةِ ذهب الإجمالُ ، وحصل التفهيم الذي هو طلب السؤالِ ، واللفظ غيرُ مرادٍ بعينه في الحد الحقيقي إلَّا عند المترسّم الذي يحوم حول العباراتِ ، فيكون اعتراضه عليها وشغفه بها
Metaphor in definition. When necessary. The fourth: that you avoid strange, unfamiliar words, remote metaphors, and ambiguous homonyms. Permit entry and strive for brevity as much as you can, and in seeking the explicit word as much as possible. If the explicit word is lacking and you need a metaphor, then seek from metaphors what is most suitable to the purpose, and mention your intended meaning to the questioner. For not every intelligible matter has an explicit established expression. The goal is understanding, even if by a lengthy explanation, or a metaphor, or by bringing a homonym while the boundaries are known. So do not deny his intended meaning if it is clarified by explicit indication or known by context. It is not appropriate to magnify his action or exaggerate in blaming him if he has revealed the reality by mentioning all the essentials. For that is the goal. These advantages are improvements and arrangements, like spices from the food of the goal. But the pretentious exaggerate such matters and strongly deny them, due to the inclination of their deficient natures away from the original goal to means and subsidiaries. So much so that they might deny the saying of someone in the definition of knowledge: 'It is confidence in the known, or apprehension of the known,' because confidence is ambiguous between knowledge and trust. But this objector is foolish; for when confidence is connected with the known, it is determined in the aspect of understanding. And if someone says: 'The definition of colour is that which is perceived by the sense of sight in such and such a manner,' then this should not be denied because 'eye' may refer to gold or the sun. For with the context of 'sense', ambiguity disappears, and clarification, which is the goal of the question, is achieved. The word itself is not intended in a real definition except by a pedant who hovers around expressions, so his objection is upon them and his obsession with them.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of using metaphorical language (majaz) and ambiguous terms (mushtarak) in definitions when necessary. Al-Ghazali states the fourth rule: avoid strange, remote metaphors, and ambiguous homonyms. However, he qualifies this by saying that if one cannot find an explicit term (nas), one may use metaphor (isti'ara) that is suitable to the purpose, and the definer should explain his intended meaning. The ultimate goal (maqsud) is understanding (tafhim), even if it requires extended explanation, metaphor, or a homonym whose meaning is clarified by context (qarina). He dismisses objections to definitions that use ambiguous words, such as defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'thiqah' (confidence) – objectors say thiqah also means trustworthiness – but with the context of 'al-malum' (the known), it becomes clear. Similarly, defining 'lawn' (colour) as perceived by 'ayn (eye) – though 'ayn can mean gold or sun – but with 'hassah' (sense), it is clear. Al-Ghazali criticizes pedants (mutahadhiqun) who overly focus on wording rather than meaning. He concludes that the word itself is not the goal in real definitions except for those overly concerned with expressions. This passage is significant because it shows the practical, meaning-centered approach in Islamic logic, allowing flexibility for communication while maintaining precision.

Why Pedantic Objections to Definitions Fail: The Role of Context
Critique of pedantic objectors who reject definitions due to word ambiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute pedantic objections to definitions by appealing to contextual resolution (qarīna).

Core Idea
Definitions are judged by meaning, not by isolated wording; context resolves ambiguity.

In this passage, the author takes aim at overly pedantic critics who reject valid definitions because a single word might have multiple meanings. He argues that such objections ignore the clarifying power of context (qarīna). You will learn why this approach is a logical fallacy in definitional discourse and how to defend a definition against such superficial attacks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pedantic objection
Look for objections that focus on a word's ambiguity without considering the context. In the passage, the objector says 'thiqah' is ambiguous between knowledge and trustworthiness.
This is a common tactic among those who miss the forest for the trees.
2
Recognize the author's diagnosis
The author attributes such objections to a deficient nature that focuses on means rather than the intended purpose. He calls such objectors 'muddled' (muhawwis).
3
Understand the role of contextual cues (qarīna)
In both examples, the context determines the intended meaning. With 'thiqah bi al-ma'lūm', the phrase 'bi al-ma'lūm' specifies understanding. With 'sense of sight', it removes the ambiguity of 'eye'.
Qarīna is a standard tool in uṣūl al-fiqh and logic to resolve ambiguity.
4
Apply the principle to defend definitions
When a word is ambiguous in isolation, check if the definition's context (e.g., 'known' or 'sense') removes the ambiguity. If so, the objection is invalid.
This refutes the pedantic objection by showing that the definition is clear in context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّهُ المقصود ، وهذه المزايا تحسينات وترتيبات ؛ كالأبازيرِ مِنَ المقصود . وإنَّما المتحذلقونَ يستعظمونَ مثل ذلك ، ويستنكرونه غايةَ الاستنكار ؛ لميل طباعِهِمُ القاصرةِ عَنِ المقصودِ الأصلي إلى الوسائل والتوابع ، حتَّى ربَّما أنكروا قول القائل في حدّ العلم : إنَّهُ الثقة بالمعلوم، أو إدراك المعلوم من حيث إن الثقة مرددة " بين العلم والأمانةِ ، وهذا المعترِضُ مُهَوَّسٌ ؛ فَإِنَّ الثقةَ إِذَا قُرِنَتْ بالمعلوم .. تعيَّن فيه جهة الفهم . ومَنْ قالَ : حد اللون : ما يُدرك بحاسة العين على وجه كذا وكذا .. فلا ينبغي أن يُنكر هذا لأنَّ العينَ قدْ يُراد بها الذهب والشمس ؛ فإِنَّهُ معَ قرينةِ الحاسَةِ ذهب الإجمالُ ، وحصل التفهيم الذي هو طلب السؤالِ ، واللفظ غيرُ مرادٍ بعينه في الحد الحقيقي إلَّا عند المترسّم الذي يحوم حول العباراتِ ، فيكون اعتراضه عليها وشغفه بها
For it is the intended purpose, and these advantages are improvements and arrangements; like spices from the intended food. And indeed the pedantic ones regard such things as great, and deny them with utmost denial, due to the inclination of their deficient natures from the original intended purpose to means and accessories, until they may deny the saying of one who says in the definition of knowledge: 'It is confidence in the known, or apprehension of the known,' because confidence is ambiguous between knowledge and trustworthiness. But this objector is muddled; for confidence, when connected with the known, becomes determined as the aspect of understanding. And whoever says: 'The definition of color: that which is perceived by the sense of sight in such and such a manner,' then this should not be denied because 'eye' may be intended to mean gold or the sun; for indeed, with the contextual cue of 'sense', ambiguity is removed, and understanding which is the goal of the request is attained. And the word is not intended in itself in the real definition except for the pedantic one who hovers around expressions, so his objection and obsession is about them.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a critique of pedantic objectors who reject definitions based on alleged ambiguity of words, while ignoring the clarifying role of context (qarīna). The author first states that such improvements and arrangements are like spices from the intended food—secondary, not the main point. Then he identifies the pedants (mutaḥaddiqūn) as those who exaggerate these issues and deny valid definitions because their deficient natures incline towards means and accessories rather than the original intent. As an example, some object to defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'thiqah bi al-ma'lūm' (confidence in the known), arguing that 'thiqah' is ambiguous between 'confidence' and 'trustworthiness'. The author dismisses this as muddled (muhawwis), because when 'thiqah' is connected to 'the known', it is determined to mean understanding. Similarly, defining 'lawn' (color) as 'perceived by the sense of sight' might be objected to because 'ayn can mean eye, gold, or sun. But with the contextual cue of 'sense', the ambiguity disappears. The author concludes that in real definitions (al-ḥadd al-ḥaqīqī), the wording is not intended in itself except for the pedant who hovers around expressions, so his objection and obsession are only about the wording, not the meaning.

Why Definitions Cannot Be Proved by Demonstration
Author argues that definition cannot be obtained through demonstration due to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why definitions cannot be proven by demonstration and identify the infinite regress problem.

Core Idea
Definitions are foundational; trying to prove them leads to an endless chain.

This passage reveals a crucial insight: definitions cannot be established through demonstrative proofs. The author shows that any attempt to prove a definition inevitably results in either an infinite regress or stopping at an unproven starting point. This demonstrates that definitions must be known directly, not through intermediate terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The author asserts that definition (hadd) is not obtained by demonstration (burhan). Note the example: 'The definition of wine: it is an intoxicating beverage.'
2
Examine the definition as a claim
Recognize that a definition is a proposition (qadiyya) with a subject and predicate. Here the subject is 'wine' and the predicate is 'intoxicating beverage'.
3
Follow the regress argument
If the proposition is unknown, a middle term (the essence of demonstration) is needed. But that middle term itself requires proof via two new propositions, leading to an infinite chain.
If known without middle term, demonstration is unnecessary.
4
Recognize the stopping problem
If the chain stops at an unproven point, that point cannot be known without a middle term, so the demonstration fails. Definition must be taken as foundational.
5
Apply to another example
Consider defining knowledge as cognition. If challenged, one might try to use 'belief' as an intermediate, but the same regress occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنَّ الحدَّ لا يحصلُ بالبرهان ؛ لأنَّا إذا قلنا : حدُّ الخمرِ : أنَّها شراب مسكرُ فقيل لنا : لِمَ ؟ .. كانَ محالاً أنْ يُقامَ عليهِ برهان ، فإن لم يكن معنا خصم وكنا نطلبُ .. كانَ أيضاً محالاً أن تطلب صحتَهُ بالبرهان ؛ لأنَّ قولَنا : حد الخمرِ : أَنَّها شراب مسكر دعوى ، هي قضية محكومُها : الخمرُ ، وحكمها : أنَّها شراب مسكر ، وهذه القضيةُ إنْ كانَتْ معلومة بغير وسط .. فلا حاجة إلى البرهان ، وإن لم تُعلم وافتقرنا إلى وسط وهو معنى البرهان .. كانَ صحَّةُ ذلك الوسط للمحكوم عليه وصحة الحكمِ للوسط كلُّ واحدٍ قضيةً واحدةً ، فبماذا نعرف صحتَها ؟! فإن احتيج إلى وسط آخرَ .. تداعى إلى غير نهاية ، وإن وقف موضع بغيرِ وسط .. فبماذا نعرفُ في ذلك الموضع صحتَهُ ؟! فلنتخذ ذلك طريقاً في إدراك الأمر . مثالُهُ : لو قلنا : حدُّ العلم : أنَّهُ معرفةٌ فقيل : لِمَ ؟ فقلنا : لأنَّ كلَّ علم فهوَ اعتقاد مثلاً ، وكلَّ اعتقادِ فهو معرفةٌ ، فيقالُ : ولِمَ قلتُمْ : كلُّ علم فهوَ اعتقاد ؟ ولِمَ قلتُمْ : إِنَّ كلَّ اعتقادِ فهوَ معرفةٌ ؟ فيصيرُ السؤالُ سؤالين ، وهكذا يتداعى
Know that definition (hadd) is not obtained by demonstration (burhan); because if we say: 'The definition of wine: it is an intoxicating beverage' and it is said to us: 'Why?', it is impossible to set up a demonstration for it. If we have no opponent and we are seeking, it is also impossible to seek its correctness by demonstration; because our statement 'The definition of wine: it is an intoxicating beverage' is a claim (da'wa), which is a proposition whose subject is wine and whose predicate is that it is an intoxicating beverage. If this proposition is known without a middle term, then there is no need for demonstration; and if it is not known and we need a middle term (which is the meaning of demonstration), then the correctness of that middle term for the subject and the correctness of the predicate for the middle term are each a single proposition. So how do we know their correctness? If another middle term is needed, it leads to an infinite regress; and if it stops at a position without a middle term, then how do we know its correctness at that position? So let us take that as a way in apprehending the matter. Its example: if we say: 'The definition of knowledge: it is cognition' and it is said: 'Why?' and we say: 'Because every knowledge is, for example, belief, and every belief is cognition,' then it is said: 'Why did you say that every knowledge is belief? And why did you say that every belief is cognition?' So the question becomes two questions, and so on ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument demonstrating that definition (hadd) cannot be established through demonstration (burhan). The author begins by stating the thesis: definition is not obtained by demonstration. He provides the example: 'The definition of wine: it is an intoxicating beverage.' If someone asks 'Why?', it is impossible to construct a proof. Even if we are simply inquiring (no opponent), we cannot prove it via demonstration. The reason is that the definition is a claim (da'wa) which is a proposition (qadiyya) with a subject (mahkum 'alayhi: wine) and predicate (hukm: intoxicating beverage). If this proposition is already known without a middle term, demonstration is unnecessary. If it is unknown, we need a middle term (the essence of demonstration). That middle term itself must be connected to the subject and predicate via two new propositions. Then we would need to prove those propositions, leading to either infinite regress (tasalsul) or stopping at an unproven point. In either case, we cannot arrive at certainty. So definition must be grasped directly, not through proof. The author then gives another example: defining knowledge as cognition (ma'rifa). If challenged, one might try to use an intermediate belief (i'tiqad), but then the same regress occurs.

Key Terms
الحد
البرهان
الوسط
دعوى

Key Takeaway
Definitions are foundational claims that must be known directly; attempting to prove them via demonstration leads to an infinite regress.

The Solitary Thinker's Certainty in Definition
Author describes how a lone thinker arrives at certainty that a definition is unique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the process by which a solitary thinker becomes certain that a definition is unique.

Core Idea
When the essence is grasped, the definition becomes self-evidently singular.

In this passage, the author contrasts the solitary thinker with the disputant in a dialectical setting. He describes how, when a person independently clarifies the reality of a thing and sees how the verbal expression matches the mental conception, he intuitively knows that there is only one correct definition. This highlights the epistemological ideal that a correct definition is not multiple but single.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cognitive stages
The passage mentions two stages: first, the reality (haqīqa) is clarified; second, the word's correspondence to the mental conception is realized. Walk through these stages and note that both are internal, not dependent on external debate.
The stages are sequential: clarification then realization.
2
Recognize the result
After these stages, the thinker knows the definition is one (wāḥid li-l-ḥadd). This knowledge is intuitive and certain, not derived from argumentation.
The conclusion is self-evident once the stages are completed.
3
Contrast with dialectical context
The passage begins with 'As for the thinker with himself' implying a contrast with the previous discussion of debate. Understand that certainty here comes from direct insight, not from resolving objections.
This highlights the ideal: a well-formed definition is uniquely determined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الناظر مع نفسه : فإذا تحرَّرَ لَهُ حقيقةُ الشيء ، وتلخّصَ لَهُ كيف يتحقق في نفسه اللفظ الدال على ما تحرَّرَ في ذهنِهِ .. علمَ أَنَّهُ واحِدٌ للحدِ
As for the thinker with himself: when the reality of a thing is clarified to him, and it becomes clear to him how the word indicating what is clarified in his mind is realized in himself, he knows that it is one for the definition.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This short passage shifts from dialectical dispute to solitary reflection. It highlights that once a person independently grasps the true nature (haqīqa) of something and understands how the verbal expression corresponds to that mental conception, he recognizes that the correct definition is singular and unambiguous. This underscores the ideal that a properly formed definition should be unique to the defined.

Testing a Definition via Dialectical Objection
Dialectical objection and response concerning the definition of ghasb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a dialectical objection to a definition by testing whether an added description is essential using a counterexample.

Core Idea
A definition with a non-essential element collapses under a counterexample.

In this passage, the author engages in a jadal exchange to refine the definition of ghasb (usurpation). The opponent defines ghasb as including 'removal' of the rightful hand. The author then queries whether a secondary usurper, who does not remove the rightful hand, is still a usurper. This test reveals that 'removal' is not essential, thus the definition is flawed. You will learn how to apply this dialectical test to any definition.

Guided Path
1
Extract the proposed definition
Find the opponent's definition of ghasb. It must include the genus and differentia. Here, the opponent says: 'establishing the invalid hand that removes the rightful hand.' Note the added description 'removal'.
2
Identify the added description
The author points out that 'removal' is an extra description. Ask: Is this description necessary? The dialectical method tests by imagining a case where ghasb exists without it.
3
Construct a counterexample
The author proposes the case of a second usurper (ghasib min ghasib): he establishes a wrongful hand but does not remove the rightful hand (already removed by first usurper). Yet he is still liable. This shows removal is not essential.
The counterexample must be plausible within the same legal framework.
4
Evaluate the objection
Since the counterexample shows ghasb without removal, the added description is superfluous. The definition is too narrow if removal is required, or incorrect if it is considered necessary.
This is a standard jadal move: muman'aa (prevention) by counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منعكس ، فما الحدُّ عندَكَ ؟ فلا بدَّ مِنْ ذكرِهِ حَتَّى ننظر إلى موضع التفاوت . فيقولُ : بل حدُّ الغصب : إثباتُ اليد المبطلة المزيلة لليدِ المحقة فنقولُ : قد زدت وصفاً ، وهو الإزالة ، فلننظر هل يمكننا أن نقدر اعتراف الخصمِ بثبوتِ الغصب مع عدم هذا الوصفِ ؟ فإنْ قدرنا عليهِ . . بانَ بأنَّ الزيادة محذوفةٌ ، وذلك بأن نقول : الغاصب مِنَ الغاصب يضمنُ للمالكِ ، وقد أثبت اليد العادية ، وما أزالَ المحقَّةَ ، فإنَّ المزالَ لا يزالُ ، وكانَ الأَوَّلُ قدْ أزالَ اليد المحقَّةَ فهذا طريق قطع النزاع وأما الناظر مع نفسه : فإذا تحرَّرَ لَهُ حقيقةُ الشيء ، وتلخّصَ لَهُ كيف يتحقق في نفسه اللفظ الدال على ما تحرَّرَ في ذهنِهِ .. علمَ أَنَّهُ واحِدٌ للحدِ
It is inverted, so what is the definition according to you? It is necessary to mention it so that we look at the point of difference. He says: Rather the definition of ghasb is: the establishment of the invalid hand that removes the rightful hand. We say: You have added a description, which is removal. Let us see whether we can imagine the opponent's admission of the existence of ghasb without this description? If we are able to do so... it becomes clear that the addition is superfluous. That is by saying: The usurper from the usurper is liable to the owner, and he has established the wrongful hand, and did not remove the rightful hand, for what has been removed cannot be removed again, and the first had removed the rightful hand. So this is a way to cut off the dispute. And as for the one who investigates with himself: when the reality of the thing is clarified for him, and it is made clear to him how the word indicating what has been clarified in his mind is realized in itself... he knows that it is one for the definition.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (jadal) about the correct definition of ghasb (usurpation). The interlocutor first says the definition is 'inverted' (mun'akis) and asks for the opponent's definition to see the point of difference. The opponent defines ghasb as 'establishing the invalid hand that removes the rightful hand'. The author then objects that the opponent has added an extra description, namely 'removal' (izala). The author asks whether we can conceive of ghasb occurring without this removal. He then gives the example of a usurper who usurps from another usurper (ghasib min ghasib). In this case, the second usurper establishes a wrongful hand but does not remove the rightful hand because it was already removed by the first usurper. Yet the second usurper is still liable to the original owner. Thus, removal is not an essential part of the definition. This shows that the extra description is superfluous. The author concludes that this is a way to resolve the dispute. Then he adds a note about the individual investigator: when one thoroughly examines the reality of a thing and formulates the word that indicates that reality in his mind, he will know that the definition is unified and complete.

Three Sources of Error in Definition
Author enumerates and illustrates three sources of error in logical definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify three types of errors in definitions: substituting differentia for genus, substituting substrate for genus, and substituting a past state for genus.

Core Idea
A sound definition must use the correct genus and differentia—substituting matter or history for genus leads to error.

This lesson introduces a crucial canon from the logic tradition: a classification of all possible errors in definition into three categories. By mastering these, you can diagnose and avoid defective definitions in Islamic sciences, such as fiqh and usul al-fiqh, where precise definitions are essential for correct rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus of a correct definition
For each example, first determine what the correct genus should be. For instance, the correct genus of 'sword' is 'instrument', not 'iron'. The correct genus of 'love' is 'love' itself, not 'excess'.
The genus is the broader logical category, not the material or a state.
2
Spot error type 1: differentia as genus
When the distinguishing feature (differentia) is placed where the genus should be. Text example: 'excess of love' uses 'excess' (differentia) instead of 'love' (genus). Correction: 'excessive love' where 'love' is genus and 'excessive' is differentia.
This error confuses the part of the definition that distinguishes the species from the category itself.
3
Spot error type 2: substrate as genus
When the material or locus (substrate) is used as the genus. Examples: 'wood upon which one sits' for a chair (genus should be 'furniture' or 'sitting implement'), 'iron with which one cuts' for a sword (genus should be 'instrument').
The substrate (mahall) is the matter, not the logical category; it does not define the essence.
4
Spot error type 3: past state as genus
When a previous state of the object is used as its genus, even though it no longer exists. Examples: 'ashes are burnt wood' (wood no longer exists), 'child is transformed semen' (semen no longer exists).
This error uses 'what was' (ma kana) instead of the current essence; the correct genus for ashes is not 'wood' but 'residue of combustion'.
5
Apply to Islamic definitions
Practice by examining a definition from fiqh or kalam and checking for these errors. For example, 'salah is bowing and prostrating' uses actions (differentia) as genus; the correct genus is 'act of worship'.
This skill is vital for evaluating definitions in Islamic texts and avoiding logical mistakes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القانون الخامس في حصر مداخل الخلل في استحد وهي ثلاثة فإِنَّهُ تارةً يدخلُ مِنْ جهةِ الجنس ، وتارةً مِنْ جهةِ الفَضلِ ، وتارةً من جهة أمر مشترك بينهما . أمَّا الخلل في الجنس : فأن يُؤخذَ الفصلُ بدلَهُ ؛ كما يُقالُ في الخلل الناشئ عن حد العشقِ : ( إِنَّهُ إفراطُ المحبَّةِ ( وينبغي أن يُقالَ : ( إِنَّهُ المحبَّةُ المفرطةُ ) فالإفراط يفصلها عن سائر أنواع المحبَّةِ ومِنْ ذلك : أن يُؤخذ المحل بدل الجنسِ ؛ كقولك في اعتبار المحل بدل الكرسي : ( إنَّهُ خشب يُجلس عليهِ ) ، والسيفِ : ( إِنَّهُ حديد يقطعُ بهِ ) ، بل ينبغي أن يُقالَ للسيفِ : ( إِنَّهُ آلةٌ صناعيَّةٌ مِنْ حديدٍ مستطيل ، عرضُها كذا ، ويقطعُ بها كذا ) فالآله جنس ، والحديد محلّ للصورة ، لا جنس وأبعد منهُ : أن يُؤخذ بدلَ الجنس ما كانَ (١) ، والآن ليس اعتبار ما كان بدل بموجود ؛ كقولك : ( الرماد : خشب محترق ، والولد : نطفة مستحيلةٌ ( فإنَّ الحديد موجود في السيف في الحال ، والنطفة والخشب غير موجودين في الولد والرماد . (١) والعبارة في نسخة من المستصفى » ( ٥٤/١ ) : ( ما كان موجوداً ) ، وكلاهما مناسب ؛ لأن ( كان ) هنا تامة بمعنى ( وُجِدَ )
The fifth canon: On enumerating the sources of defect in definition. And they are three. For sometimes the defect enters from the side of the genus, sometimes from the side of the differentia, and sometimes from a matter common to both. As for the defect in the genus: that the differentia is taken as a substitute for it; as is said in the defect arising from the definition of love: 'It is an excess of love.' But it should be said: 'It is excessive love.' For the excess distinguishes it from other types of love. And among that: that the substrate is taken as a substitute for the genus; like your saying in considering the substrate as a substitute for the chair: 'It is wood upon which one sits,' and for the sword: 'It is iron with which one cuts.' Rather, it should be said for the sword: 'It is an artificial instrument of elongated iron, its width such and such, and one cuts with it such and such.' For the instrument is the genus, and iron is the substrate for the form, not a genus. And more remote than that: that what was is taken as a substitute for the genus, while now it is not existent; like your saying: 'Ashes are burnt wood,' and 'The child is transformed semen.' For iron exists in the sword in the present, while the semen and wood do not exist in the child and ashes. (1) And the expression in one manuscript of al-Mustasfa (1/54): 'what was existent,' and both are appropriate because 'kana' here is complete in the sense of 'existed.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a canon (qanun) listing three sources of error in definitions (hudud). The three sources are: error entering from the side of the genus, error from the side of the differentia, and error from a matter common to both. The author first explains the error in the genus: taking the differentia as a substitute for the genus. Example: defining love as 'excess of love'—here 'excess' is the differentia, not the genus; the correct definition is 'excessive love' where 'love' is the genus and 'excessive' is the differentia. Second error: taking the substrate (mahall) instead of the genus. Example: defining a chair as 'wood upon which one sits'—wood is the substrate, not the genus; the correct genus should be 'furniture' or 'sitting implement'. Similarly, for a sword: 'iron with which one cuts'—iron is the substrate; the correct definition is 'an artificial instrument of elongated iron of certain width with which one cuts', where 'instrument' is the genus. Third and more remote error: taking 'what was' (ma kana) instead of the genus. This means using a past state that no longer exists. Example: 'ashes are burnt wood'—the wood no longer exists; similarly, 'the child is transformed semen'—the semen no longer exists. The author notes that in the sword example, iron exists in the present, whereas in the ashes and child examples, the original matter is absent. The footnote mentions a variant reading from al-Mustasfa, where the phrase is 'what was existent', both versions meaning essentially the same.

ما كان

Key Takeaway
The three sources of error in definition are using the differentia instead of the genus, using the substrate instead of the genus, and using a past state instead of the genus.

Defects from the Genus in Definitions
Enumerates seven errors in the genus part of definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify seven types of genus-substitution errors in definitions and recognize which of them are present in a given definition from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A definition fails when the genus is replaced by the differentia, substrate, material, part, ability, accident, or species.

The author of the passage enumerates three sources of defect in definition. The first and most extensive is from the side of the genus (jins). He gives seven distinct ways the genus can be wrongly substituted. Understanding these will enable you to spot faulty definitions in Islamic legal and theological reasoning, such as those used in usul al-fiqh or kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven sub-types
List each error: (1) differentia as genus (e.g., defining love as 'excess of affection'), (2) substrate as genus (e.g., chair as 'wood on which one sits'), (3) past material as genus (e.g., ash as 'burnt wood'), (4) part as genus (e.g., ten as 'five and five'), (5) ability as genus (e.g., chaste as 'able to avoid pleasures'), (6) non-essential concomitant as genus (e.g., 'one' in definition of sun), (7) species as genus (e.g., evil as 'injustice').
These are from the passage's examples.
2
For each, extract the logical error
Understand why the substitute is not the true genus: e.g., substrate is merely the matter, not the category; ability does not imply actual act; species is narrower than genus.
3
Apply to an Islamic science definition
Take a definition from fiqh, such as 'riba al-fadl is an excess in a sale of the same commodity'. Spot which error is present: the commodity (e.g., wheat) is the substrate, not the genus; the genus should be 'sale' or 'exchange'.
This mirrors the sword example in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القانون الخامس
في حصر مداخل الخلل في الحد
وهي ثلاثة
فإنه تارةً يدخل من جهة الجنس، وتارةً من جهة الفصل، وتارةً من جهة أمر مشترك بينهما. أما الخلل في الجنس: فأن يؤخذ الفصل بدله؛ كما يقال في الخلل الناشئ عن حد العشق: إنه إفراط المحبة (وينبغي أن يقال: إنه المحبة المفرطة) فالإفراط يفصلها عن سائر أنواع المحبة. ومن ذلك: أن يؤخذ المحل بدل الجنس؛ كقولك في حد الكرسي: إنه خشب يجلس عليه، والسيف: إنه حديد يقطع به، بل ينبغي أن يقال للسيف: إنه آلة صناعية من حديد مستطيل، عرضها كذا، ويقطع بها كذا. فالآلة جنس، والحديد محل للصورة، لا جنس. وأبعد من ذلك: أن يؤخذ بدل الجنس ما كان موجوداً، والآن ليس موجوداً؛ كقولك: الرماد: خشب محترق، والولد: نطفة مستحيلة. فإن الحديد موجود في السيف في الحال، والنطفة والخشب غير موجودين في الولد والرماد. (والعبارة في نسخة من المستصفى: ما كان موجوداً، وكلاهما مناسب؛ لأن كان هنا تامة بمعنى وُجِدَ)
ومن ذلك: أن يؤخذ الجزء بدل الجنس؛ كما يقال: حد العشرة: أنها خمسة وخمسة.
ومن ذلك: أن توضع القدرة موضع المقدور؛ كما يقال: حد العفيف: هو الذي يقوى على اجتناب الشهوات واللذات. وهو فاسد، بل هو الذي يترك. وإلا فالفاسق أيضاً يقوى على الترك والاجتناب، ولا يترك.
ومن ذلك: أن توضع اللوازم التي ليست ذاتية بدل الجنس؛ كالواحد والموجود إذا أخذته في حد الشمس أو الأرض.
ومن ذلك: أن يضع النوع مكان الجنس؛ كقولك: الشر هو ظلم الناس. والظلم نوع من الشر.
الخلل الناشئ من جهة الفصل: فبأن يأخذ اللوازم والعرضيات في الاحتراز بدلاً عن الذاتيات، وألا يورد جميع الفصول.
وأما القوانين المشتركة: فمن ذلك: أن تحد الشيء بما هو أخفى منه؛ كقول القائل: حد الحادث ما تعلقت به القدرة. خطأ.
ومن ذلك: حد الشيء بما هو مساو له في الخفاء أو فرع له؛ كقولك: العلم ما يعلم به، أو العلم ما تكون الذات به عالمة.
The fifth principle: On enumerating the sources of defect in definition. They are three. For sometimes it enters from the side of the genus, sometimes from the side of the differentia, and sometimes from the side of a matter common to both. As for the defect in the genus: that the differentia is taken in its place; as is said regarding the defect arising from the definition of love: 'It is excess of affection' (and it should be said: 'It is excessive affection') for excess distinguishes it from other types of affection. And among that: that the substrate (mahall) is taken in place of the genus; like your saying in the definition of a chair: 'It is wood upon which one sits', and of a sword: 'It is iron with which one cuts'; rather it should be said of a sword: 'It is an artificial instrument of elongated iron, its width such-and-such, and one cuts with it such-and-such.' For instrument (āla) is the genus, and iron is the substrate for the form, not the genus. And more remote than that: that what existed [but] is not now existing is taken in place of the genus; like your saying: 'Ash: burnt wood', and 'A child: transformed sperm.' For iron exists in the sword currently, while sperm and wood do not exist in the child and ash. (And the expression in a copy of al-Mustasfā is: 'what existed', and both are appropriate because 'kāna' here is complete in the sense of 'existed'.) And among that: that the part is taken in place of the genus; as is said: 'The definition of ten: that it is five and five.' And among that: that ability (qudra) is placed in the position of the possible (maqdūr); as is said: 'The definition of the chaste person: he who is able to avoid appetites and pleasures.' This is corrupt; rather he is the one who refrains. Otherwise, the immoral person also is able to refrain and avoid, but does not refrain. And among that: that non-essential concomitants are placed in place of the genus; like 'one' and 'existent' if you take them in the definition of the sun or earth. And among that: that the species is placed in the position of the genus; like your saying: 'Evil is injustice to people.' And injustice is a type of evil. The defect arising from the side of the differentia: that one takes concomitants and accidents in the distinction (iḥtirāz) instead of essential properties, and that one does not adduce all the differentiae. As for the common principles: among that: that you define a thing by what is more hidden than it; like the saying: 'The definition of the originated (ḥādith): that which power (qudra) is attached to.' Error. And among that: defining a thing by what is equal to it in obscurity or subsidiary to it; like your saying: 'Knowledge: that by which one knows', or 'Knowledge: that by which the essence is knowing.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three sources of defect in definition (hadd) according to the author. The first source is defect arising from the genus (jins). The author gives multiple sub-types: (1) mistakenly using the differentia (fasl) in place of the genus—e.g., defining love as 'excess of affection' when 'excessive affection' is proper; (2) using the substrate (mahall) instead of the genuine genus—e.g., defining a chair as 'wood on which one sits' (wood is the substrate, not the genus; the genus should be 'instrument' or 'furniture'); similarly for sword, defining it as 'iron with which one cuts' (iron is substrate; the genus is 'artificial instrument'); (3) using something that existed but no longer exists as the genus—e.g., defining ash as 'burnt wood' (wood no longer exists in ash); (4) using the part instead of the genus—e.g., defining ten as 'five and five' (parts, not genus); (5) using ability (qudra) in place of the act (maqdūr)—e.g., defining the chaste as 'able to avoid pleasures' (ability is not the act of refraining; the immoral also has ability); (6) using non-essential concomitants (lawāzim ghayr dhātiyya) as the genus—e.g., 'one' or 'existent' in definitions of sun or earth; (7) using the species (naw') in place of the genus—e.g., defining evil as 'injustice' (injustice is a species of evil). The second source is defect from the differentia (fasl): taking accidental concomitants or accidentals (ʻawāriḍ) in the distinguishing part instead of essential properties (dhātiyyāt), and not including all the essential differentiae. The third source is defects arising from common matters (qawānīn mushtaraka): (1) defining a thing by something more obscure than the thing itself—e.g., defining the originated (al-ḥādith) as 'that to which power is attached' (the concept of power may be more obscure); (2) defining a thing by something equally obscure or subsidiary to it—e.g., defining knowledge (al-ʻilm) as 'that by which one knows' (a circular or equally obscure definition). The passage uses examples from logic and everyday concepts to illustrate logical errors in definitions.

Key Terms
الخلل
الجنس
الفصل
المحل
اللازمة
العرضيات

C) Using only some differentiae in a definition.
D) Defining knowledge as 'that by which one knows' (circular definition).

Key Takeaway
The genus must be a true broader category; substituting the differentia, substrate, material, part, ability, accident, or species produces a defective definition.

Defects from the Differentia and Common Matters
Lists defects from differentia and common matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify errors in the differentia (using accidents instead of essentials) and errors common to both parts (defining by what is more obscure or equally obscure).

Core Idea
A definition fails if it uses accidental properties for the differentia or if it defines the definiendum by something more obscure or equally obscure.

The second and third sources of defect in definition concern the differentia (fasl) and matters common to both genus and differentia. The author warns against using accidental concomitants instead of essential properties, and against circular or obscure definitions. These errors are frequent in theological definitions, such as those defining 'ilm or hadith.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the defect in the differentia
The differentia must be an essential property (dhātī) that distinguishes the species. Using accidental concomitants (ʻaraḍiyyāt) or not including all essential differentiae renders the definition defective. Example from the passage: defining the chaste person by ability (accidental) instead of actual refraining (essential).
This complements the genus errors.
2
Identify common defects
Two common errors: (1) defining by something more obscure than the definiendum (e.g., defining 'originated' as 'that to which power is attached' – 'power' is more obscure). (2) defining by something equally obscure or circular (e.g., 'knowledge is that by which one knows').
3
Apply to a theological definition
Consider the definition 'al-ilm (knowledge) is the attribute by which one knows'. This is circular, similar to the passage's example. A better definition would be 'al-ilm is a necessary attribute of God that encompasses all things'.
In kalam, such definitions must be clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القانون الخامس
في حصر مداخل الخلل في الحد
وهي ثلاثة
فإنه تارةً يدخل من جهة الجنس، وتارةً من جهة الفصل، وتارةً من جهة أمر مشترك بينهما. أما الخلل في الجنس: فأن يؤخذ الفصل بدله؛ كما يقال في الخلل الناشئ عن حد العشق: إنه إفراط المحبة (وينبغي أن يقال: إنه المحبة المفرطة) فالإفراط يفصلها عن سائر أنواع المحبة. ومن ذلك: أن يؤخذ المحل بدل الجنس؛ كقولك في حد الكرسي: إنه خشب يجلس عليه، والسيف: إنه حديد يقطع به، بل ينبغي أن يقال للسيف: إنه آلة صناعية من حديد مستطيل، عرضها كذا، ويقطع بها كذا. فالآلة جنس، والحديد محل للصورة، لا جنس. وأبعد من ذلك: أن يؤخذ بدل الجنس ما كان موجوداً، والآن ليس موجوداً؛ كقولك: الرماد: خشب محترق، والولد: نطفة مستحيلة. فإن الحديد موجود في السيف في الحال، والنطفة والخشب غير موجودين في الولد والرماد. (والعبارة في نسخة من المستصفى: ما كان موجوداً، وكلاهما مناسب؛ لأن كان هنا تامة بمعنى وُجِدَ)
ومن ذلك: أن يؤخذ الجزء بدل الجنس؛ كما يقال: حد العشرة: أنها خمسة وخمسة.
ومن ذلك: أن توضع القدرة موضع المقدور؛ كما يقال: حد العفيف: هو الذي يقوى على اجتناب الشهوات واللذات. وهو فاسد، بل هو الذي يترك. وإلا فالفاسق أيضاً يقوى على الترك والاجتناب، ولا يترك.
ومن ذلك: أن توضع اللوازم التي ليست ذاتية بدل الجنس؛ كالواحد والموجود إذا أخذته في حد الشمس أو الأرض.
ومن ذلك: أن يضع النوع مكان الجنس؛ كقولك: الشر هو ظلم الناس. والظلم نوع من الشر.
الخلل الناشئ من جهة الفصل: فبأن يأخذ اللوازم والعرضيات في الاحتراز بدلاً عن الذاتيات، وألا يورد جميع الفصول.
وأما القوانين المشتركة: فمن ذلك: أن تحد الشيء بما هو أخفى منه؛ كقول القائل: حد الحادث ما تعلقت به القدرة. خطأ.
ومن ذلك: حد الشيء بما هو مساو له في الخفاء أو فرع له؛ كقولك: العلم ما يعلم به، أو العلم ما تكون الذات به عالمة.
The fifth principle: On enumerating the sources of defect in definition. They are three. For sometimes it enters from the side of the genus, sometimes from the side of the differentia, and sometimes from the side of a matter common to both. As for the defect in the genus: that the differentia is taken in its place; as is said regarding the defect arising from the definition of love: 'It is excess of affection' (and it should be said: 'It is excessive affection') for excess distinguishes it from other types of affection. And among that: that the substrate (mahall) is taken in place of the genus; like your saying in the definition of a chair: 'It is wood upon which one sits', and of a sword: 'It is iron with which one cuts'; rather it should be said of a sword: 'It is an artificial instrument of elongated iron, its width such-and-such, and one cuts with it such-and-such.' For instrument (āla) is the genus, and iron is the substrate for the form, not the genus. And more remote than that: that what existed [but] is not now existing is taken in place of the genus; like your saying: 'Ash: burnt wood', and 'A child: transformed sperm.' For iron exists in the sword currently, while sperm and wood do not exist in the child and ash. (And the expression in a copy of al-Mustasfā is: 'what existed', and both are appropriate because 'kāna' here is complete in the sense of 'existed'.) And among that: that the part is taken in place of the genus; as is said: 'The definition of ten: that it is five and five.' And among that: that ability (qudra) is placed in the position of the possible (maqdūr); as is said: 'The definition of the chaste person: he who is able to avoid appetites and pleasures.' This is corrupt; rather he is the one who refrains. Otherwise, the immoral person also is able to refrain and avoid, but does not refrain. And among that: that non-essential concomitants are placed in place of the genus; like 'one' and 'existent' if you take them in the definition of the sun or earth. And among that: that the species is placed in the position of the genus; like your saying: 'Evil is injustice to people.' And injustice is a type of evil. The defect arising from the side of the differentia: that one takes concomitants and accidents in the distinction (iḥtirāz) instead of essential properties, and that one does not adduce all the differentiae. As for the common principles: among that: that you define a thing by what is more hidden than it; like the saying: 'The definition of the originated (ḥādith): that which power (qudra) is attached to.' Error. And among that: defining a thing by what is equal to it in obscurity or subsidiary to it; like your saying: 'Knowledge: that by which one knows', or 'Knowledge: that by which the essence is knowing.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three sources of defect in definition (hadd) according to the author. The first source is defect arising from the genus (jins). The author gives multiple sub-types: (1) mistakenly using the differentia (fasl) in place of the genus—e.g., defining love as 'excess of affection' when 'excessive affection' is proper; (2) using the substrate (mahall) instead of the genuine genus—e.g., defining a chair as 'wood on which one sits' (wood is the substrate, not the genus; the genus should be 'instrument' or 'furniture'); similarly for sword, defining it as 'iron with which one cuts' (iron is substrate; the genus is 'artificial instrument'); (3) using something that existed but no longer exists as the genus—e.g., defining ash as 'burnt wood' (wood no longer exists in ash); (4) using the part instead of the genus—e.g., defining ten as 'five and five' (parts, not genus); (5) using ability (qudra) in place of the act (maqdūr)—e.g., defining the chaste as 'able to avoid pleasures' (ability is not the act of refraining; the immoral also has ability); (6) using non-essential concomitants (lawāzim ghayr dhātiyya) as the genus—e.g., 'one' or 'existent' in definitions of sun or earth; (7) using the species (naw') in place of the genus—e.g., defining evil as 'injustice' (injustice is a species of evil). The second source is defect from the differentia (fasl): taking accidental concomitants or accidentals (ʻawāriḍ) in the distinguishing part instead of essential properties (dhātiyyāt), and not including all the essential differentiae. The third source is defects arising from common matters (qawānīn mushtaraka): (1) defining a thing by something more obscure than the thing itself—e.g., defining the originated (al-ḥādith) as 'that to which power is attached' (the concept of power may be more obscure); (2) defining a thing by something equally obscure or subsidiary to it—e.g., defining knowledge (al-ʻilm) as 'that by which one knows' (a circular or equally obscure definition). The passage uses examples from logic and everyday concepts to illustrate logical errors in definitions.

Key Terms
الفصل
اللوازم
العرضيات
الذاتيات
القوانين المشتركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Defining the chaste as 'able to avoid pleasures'.
D) Defining evil as 'injustice'.

Key Takeaway
A definition fails if its differentia uses accidents instead of essentials, or if the definition as a whole is more obscure or equally obscure as the definiendum.
