Errors from the Aspect of Genus
Lists four fallacies in definition where genus is mishandled.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four types of errors in definitions where the genus is mishandled: partitive, potentiality, accidental, and species substitution.

Core Idea
A proper definition uses the real essential genus, not a part, potentiality, accident, or species.

The genus (jins) is the broader essential category that includes the defined thing. Errors occur when something else is mistakenly placed in the genus position. This lesson walks through four common mistakes that make a definition corrupt. Knowing these helps you evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its supposed genus
Take each example and note what is being defined (definiendum) and what is placed as the genus. For instance, in 'ten is five and five', the definiendum is 'ten' and the genus is 'five and five'.
The genus should be a broader category, not a part.
2
Check if the genus is a part of the thing
If the genus is actually a part (juz') of the definiendum, it's an error. 'Five and five' is a part of 'ten' (partitive definition). Compare with a proper genus like 'number'.
A part cannot be a genus because the genus includes the whole species.
3
Check if potentiality replaces actuality
If the definition uses potential (qudra) instead of actual (fi'l), it fails. For 'chaste', 'one who can avoid' is potential, but the immoral can also avoid; the actual act of avoiding is essential.
Definitions must capture what the thing is actually, not merely what it can do.
4
Check if accidental attributes are used as genus
Attributes like 'one' or 'existent' are accidental (arad) and not essential genera. Using them in defining the sun or earth is wrong because they apply to many things and are not specific.
Genus must be a category that divides species by essential differences.
5
Check if a species is used instead of a genus
If the genus is actually a species (naw') under the definiendum, the definition is too narrow. 'Oppression' is a species of 'evil', so 'evil is oppression' excludes other evils.
The genus must be broader than the definiendum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جهة الفصل ومِنْ ذلكَ : أن يُؤخذ الجزء بدل الجنس ؛ كما يُقالُ : ( حد العشرة : أنَّها خمسة وخمسةٌ ) . ومن ذلك : أن تُوضعَ القدرة موضعَ المقدور ؛ كما يُقالُ : ( حد العفيف : هوَ الذي يقوى على اجتناب الشهواتِ واللَّذاتِ ) وهوَ فاسد ، بل هو الذي يتركُ ، وإلَّا .. فالفاسقُ أيضاً يقوى على التركِ والاجتناب ، ولا يتركُ . ومِنْ ذلك : أن تُوضعَ اللوازم التي ليسَتْ ذاتيَّةٌ بدل الجنس ؛ كالواحدِ والموجودِ إذا أخذتَهُ في حدِ الشمس أو الأرضِ . ومِنْ ذلكَ : أن يضعَ النوعَ مكانَ الجنس ؛ كقولك : ( الشرُّ : هوَ ظلمُ الناسِ ) والظلم نوعٌ مِنَ الشرّ .
From the aspect of genus: Among that is taking the part in place of the genus, as it is said: 'The definition of ten is that it is five and five.' And among that is placing potentiality in place of the actual, as it is said: 'The definition of the chaste is one who has the power to avoid desires and pleasures' — and this is corrupt; rather he is the one who actually avoids, otherwise the immoral also has the power to avoid and abstain, but he does not abstain. And among that is placing accidents that are not essential in place of the genus, like 'one' or 'existent' if you use them in defining the sun or the earth. And among that is placing the species in place of the genus, as in your saying: 'Evil is oppression of people' — but oppression is a species of evil.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage lists four common errors in definitions related to the genus. The first error is substituting a part for the whole genus, as in defining 'ten' as 'five and five' — this is a partitive definition rather than a proper genus. The second error is using potentiality (qudra) instead of actuality (fi'l), like defining the chaste person as 'one who can avoid sins' — but a definition must state what the thing actually is, not what it is capable of; otherwise, the immoral person also has the capacity. The third error is using accidental attributes (like 'one' or 'existent') as genus, when they are not essential; for example, defining the sun as 'a one existent star' is insufficient because 'one' and 'existent' are transcendentals, not true genera. The fourth error is using a species instead of a broader genus, like defining 'evil' as 'oppression' — oppression is a type of evil, so the definition is too narrow.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins): The essential genus: the broader category that includes the defined species and other species.
الفصل (al-fasl): The differentia: the characteristic distinguishing a species within its genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which error does the definition 'Evil is oppression of people' illustrate?
A) Using a part as the genus
B) Using potentiality instead of actuality
C) Using a species as the genus
D) Using an accidental attribute as the genus

Key Takeaway
A correct definition must have a true essential genus that is broader than the definiendum, not a part, potentiality, accident, or species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Analyze the following definition: 'Salah is standing and bowing and prostrating.' Which error(s) does it commit? Correct it by supplying the proper genus.

The First Error: Using Accidents as Differentia
Describes the error of using accidents as differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an accidental property is mistakenly used as the differentia in a definition.

Core Idea
A differentia must be essential; accidental properties cannot distinguish species.

Definitions require precise differentiae to separate species. The first error highlighted here is confusing essential attributes with necessary accidents. For instance, laughter is a necessary accident of humans but not part of the essence. Recognizing this error protects definitions from being incomplete or misleading.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and candidate differentia
Locate the term being defined and the property offered as its distinguishing feature. For example, if 'human' is defined as 'laughing animal', the definiendum is 'human' and the candidate differentia is 'laughing'.
The passage warns against taking accidents like 'laughing' as differentiae.
2
Determine if the differentia is essential or accidental
Ask: Is this property part of the essence of the species, or is it a non-essential accompaniment? If it can be removed without changing the species, it is accidental.
3
Check if the property applies to other species
If the property can be found in other species under the same genus, it fails to distinguish properly. For instance, many animals laugh potentially, so 'laughing' does not uniquely identify humans.
Use the test of mutual exclusivity.
4
Conclude whether a defect exists
If the differentia is accidental, the definition is flawed. A valid definition must use an essential attribute (like 'rational' for humans).
This corresponds to the first error in the source: 'taking necessary accidents and accidentals in the qualification instead of the essential attributes'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلل الناشئ من وأَمَّا مِنْ جهةِ الفصل : فبأن يأخذ اللوازم والعرضيَّاتِ في الاحتراز بدلاً عَنِ الذاتيَّاتِ ، وألا يورد جميع الفصول
As for the defect arising from the aspect of the differentia: it is by taking necessary accidents and accidentals in the qualification instead of the essential attributes, and by not mentioning all the differentiae.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage identifies two errors related to the differentia (fasl) in definitions. The first error is using accidental or necessary accidents (like 'lāzim' or 'ʿaraḍī') as the distinguishing feature, instead of the true essential differentia. For example, defining 'human' as 'laughing' is using an accident, not a differentia, because laughter is not part of the essence. The second error is omitting some of the required differentiae: a proper definition must include every differentia that separates the species from others within the same genus. If only one of multiple differentiae is mentioned, the definition is incomplete.

Key Terms
الفصل
اللازم

D) Defining 'human' as 'mortal animal'

Key Takeaway
The first error in differentia is substituting essential attributes with accidental properties, which undermines the definition's accuracy.

The Second Error: Omitting Required Differentiae
Describes the error of omitting necessary differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition is incomplete due to missing differentiae.

Core Idea
A complete definition must include every differentia that separates the species from all others under the same genus.

The second error concerns omission: when a species requires multiple differentiae to distinguish it from all other species, a definition must include each one. For example, in defining a type of contract in fiqh, all distinguishing features must be listed. The passage warns against 'not mentioning all the differentiae'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and list all species in it
Determine the broader category (genus) and think of all species that fall under it. For example, under the genus 'contract' ('aqd) in fiqh, there are several types: sale, marriage, lease, etc.
The differentiae will distinguish one species from others.
2
Determine the set of properties that uniquely identify the target species
For the target species, list the properties that it alone possesses among all species under the genus. For instance, to define 'sale' (bayʿ), you need properties like 'transfer of ownership for a price' and 'consent of parties'. Some properties may be shared.
3
Compare the definition's differentiae with the required set
Check if the definition includes all necessary differentiae. If any is missing, the definition is too broad (includes other species). For example, defining 'sale' as 'transfer of ownership' is incomplete because loans also transfer ownership.
The source says 'and by not mentioning all the differentiae'.
4
Conclude whether a defect exists
If a required differentia is omitted, the definition fails to uniquely specify the intended species. It must be supplemented.

As for the defect arising from the aspect of the differentia: it is by taking necessary accidents and accidentals in the qualification instead of the essential attributes, and by not mentioning all the differentiae.

The Fallacy of Defining by What Is More Obscure
States the fallacy of defining something by a term more obscure than itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify definitions that commit the fallacy of defining by what is more obscure and propose clearer alternatives.

Core Idea
A definition must clarify the unknown by the known, not the reverse.

The scholars of Islamic logic established common rules for definitions that apply regardless of whether the defect lies in the genus or differentia. One such rule is that a term must not be defined by something even more obscure than itself. This lesson examines that rule through a classic example from theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the example. Here it is 'al-ḥādith' (the temporally originated).
2
Identify the definiens
Look at what is used to define it: 'that to which power is attached' (ma taʿallaqat bihi al-qudra).
3
Compare obscurity
Ask yourself: is 'power' (al-qudra) more or less known than 'originated'? In Islamic discourse, 'power' is an abstract theological term that itself needs definition.
4
Assess the definition
Conclude that the definition fails to clarify because it uses a less clear term. This violates the rule.
5
Propose a correct definition
Think of a definition using better-known terms, e.g., 'that which comes into existence after not existing' or 'a contingent existent'. Avoid relying on obscure concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلل الناشئ من وأَمَّا القوانين المشتركةُ : فمِنْ ذلكَ : أنْ تحدَّ الشيء بما هو أخفى منه ؛ كقول القائل : ( حدُّ الحادثِ : ما تعلَّقَتْ بهِ القدرة )
As for the defect arising from common rules: among that is defining a thing by what is more obscure than it, like the statement: 'The definition of the originated thing is that which power is attached to.'
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage introduces the category of common rules (qawānīn mushtaraka) for definitions, then gives the first example: defining something by a term that is even more obscure than the original. The example defines 'al-ḥādith' (the originated, i.e., a temporal event) as 'that to which power (al-qudra) is attached' — but 'power' is itself an abstract concept that is less clear than 'originated'. A proper definition must explain the unknown by the known, not the reverse.

Key Terms
القوانين المشتركة
الحادث
أخفى

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must use terms that are better known than the definiendum; defining by what is more obscure is a logical fallacy.

The Fallacy of Defining by an Equal or Subordinate Term
Discusses the fallacy of defining by a term equally obscure or subordinate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and avoid definitions that use terms equally obscure or subordinate to the definiendum, especially in Islamic legal and theological terminology.

Core Idea
A definition must clarify, not just repeat – using a cognate or synonym obscures rather than explains.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a proper definition must be clearer than the definiendum. Here, the author addresses a common mistake: defining a term by something equally obscure or by a derived form that depends on the term itself. This error is particularly insidious because it appears to explain but actually provides no new information. For example, defining 'knowledge' ('ilm) as 'that by which one knows' uses a cognate (the verb 'knows' from the same root), which is circular and fails to advance understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic passage. In the example, it is 'al-ʿilm' (knowledge).
2
Extract the definiens
Isolate the words used to define the term. For 'ilm, the definiens is 'mā yuʿlamu bihī' (that by which one knows).
3
Check for cognates
Look for words from the same root as the definiendum in the definiens. 'Yuʿlamu' shares the root ʿ-l-m with 'ʿilm'.
4
Assess clarity
Determine whether the definiens provides new information or merely restates the definiendum. Here, the definition is circular and does not increase understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ ذلكَ : حدُّ الشيء بما هو مساو له في الخفاء أو فرع له ؛ كقولك : ( العلمُ : ما يُعلمُ بهِ ، أو العلمُ : ما تكونُ الذاتُ به عالمة )
And among that is defining a thing by what is equal to it in obscurity or a subordinate of it, like your saying: 'Knowledge is that by which one knows', or 'Knowledge is that by which the self is knowing.'
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage gives the second common rule error: defining a term by something equally obscure or by a derivative that depends on the term itself. The example is defining 'knowledge' (al-ʿilm) as 'that by which one knows' — this is a definition that uses a cognate (derivative) of the definiendum, which is circular and does not increase understanding. Similarly, defining it as 'that by which the self is knowing' uses 'knowing' (ʿālima) which is from the same root. Such definitions provide no new clarity.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be clearer than the term defined; using a cognate or equally obscure term only obscures meaning.

The Fallacy of Defining by Opposite
Illustrates the fallacy of defining by opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of defining a term by its opposite and explain why such definitions fail.

Core Idea
Defining a term by its opposite leads to circularity or obscurity, not clarity.

You may think that defining a term as 'what it is not' is a quick way to give meaning. But as this passage shows, such definitions often rely on a chain of other definitions that never end or become circular. A proper definition must be positive and built from clearer terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Look at each example: the term being defined is 'knowledge' or 'even'. Note that these are what we are trying to explain.
2
Extract the definition given
The passage offers a definition for 'knowledge': 'what is not opinion, not doubt, and not ignorance'. For 'even': 'what is not odd'.
3
Examine the logical form
These are negative definitions, stating what the term is NOT, rather than what it IS. They rely on listing all opposites, which is often incomplete or impractical.
4
Check for circularity
If 'even' is defined by 'odd' and 'odd' by 'even', the definitions become interdependent and provide no real explanation. This circularity is the core problem.
5
Conclude why it fails as a definition
A definition should be clearer than the definiendum. Negative definitions using opposites are often more obscure or circular, and therefore fail to provide genuine understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ ذلكَ : أن تعرّفَ الضدَّ بالضدِّ ، فتقول : ( حد العلم : ما ليس بظنّ ولا شلّ ولا جهل ) وهكذا حتَّى تحصر الأضداد و حد الزوج : ما ليس بفرد ( ثمَّ تقدر أن تقول : ( وحد الفردِ : ما ليس بزوج ( فيدورُ الأمرُ ، ولا يحصل به بيان
And among that is defining a thing by its opposite, so you say: 'The definition of knowledge is what is not opinion, nor doubt, nor ignorance' — and so on until you enumerate all opposites. And 'the definition of even is what is not odd' — then you can say 'the definition of odd is what is not even', so the matter becomes circular and no clarification is achieved.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the error of defining a term by its opposite (ḍidd). The example tries to define 'knowledge' as 'what is not opinion, doubt, or ignorance' — this is a negative definition that relies on listing all opposites, which is impractical and often incomplete. Worse, if you define 'even' as 'not odd' and 'odd' as 'not even', the definitions become circular and no understanding is gained. A proper definition should be positive and use terms that are clearer than the definiendum.

Key Terms
الضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It uses a part instead of genus
B) It is circular with the definition of 'odd'
C) It uses an accident as differentia

Key Takeaway
Defining a term by its opposite often leads to circularity or incompleteness; proper definitions must be positive and use clearer terms.

Defining a Relative by Its Correlate
Explains the fallacy of defining a relative by its correlate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular definitions that use a relative term's correlate and construct a non-relational essential definition.

Core Idea
Defining a relative by its correlate creates a circle, not knowledge.

When we define terms like 'father' and 'son' by each other, we get no real understanding. This passage from Ibn Sina's logic explains why this is a fallacy and how to give a proper definition using essential, non-relational properties. We'll learn to spot such circular definitions and fix them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relative terms
Look for two terms (muḍāf and muḍāf ilayh) being defined with each other. Example: 'father' defined as 'one who has a son' – both are relatives.
The definiendum and definiens are mutually dependent.
2
Notice the circularity
If you don't know what a 'son' is, you can't understand 'father' defined that way, and vice versa.
They are 'equal in ignorance and knowledge' – circular.
3
Extract the essential genus and differentia
The correct definition gives the genus (e.g., 'animal') and the differentia (e.g., 'from whose sperm an animal of his type is generated') without mentioning the correlate.
This makes the definition standalone and informative.
4
Generalize the rule
For any relative term, avoid using its correlate in the definition. Describe the essence using absolute properties.
This rule applies in Fiqh, Kalam, and other Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ ذلكَ : أن يُوجدَ المضافُ في حدِ المضاف إليه ، وهما متكافئان في الإضافةِ ؛ كقول القائل : ( حدُّ الأَبِ : مَن لهُ ابنُ ) ثم لا يعجز أن يقول : ( وحد الابنِ : مَن لَهُ أب ) بل ينبغي أن يقول : ( الأبُ : حيوان تولَّدَ مِنْ نطفتِهِ حيوان هوَ مِنْ نوعِهِ ) فهوَ أبٌ مِنْ حيثُ هو كذلك ، ولا يحيل على الابن ؛ فإنَّهُما في الجهلِ والمعرفة يتساويان .
And among that is including the relative in the definition of its correlate, while they are equivalent in relation, like the statement: 'The definition of father is one who has a son' — then he is not unable to say 'and the definition of son is one who has a father'. Rather, one should say: 'The father is an animal from whose sperm an animal of his type is generated' — thus he is a father as such, and does not rely on the son; for they are equal in ignorance and knowledge.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage addresses the error of defining a relative (muḍāf) by its correlate (muḍāf ilayh) when both are mutually dependent. For example, defining 'father' as 'one who has a son' and 'son' as 'one who has a father' creates a circular dependency that provides no real understanding. The correct approach is to define each independently using non-relational terms: for instance, define 'father' as 'an animal from whose sperm an animal of his species is generated' — this avoids reliance on the correlate and gives a proper essential description.

Key Terms
المضاف (al-muḍāf): A relative term; something that is defined in relation to something else.
المضاف إليه (al-muḍāf ilayh): The correlate; the term in relation to which the muḍāf is defined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is too long.
B) It is circular because 'son' also depends on 'father'.
C) It includes an animal genus.
D) It is a rasm not a hadd.

Key Takeaway
To properly define a relative, give its essential nature without relying on the correlate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Find a circular definition in classical Islamic logic texts (e.g., 'the possible is that which may be or not be') and rewrite it non-circularly.

Defining Cause by Effect: A Vicious Circle
Fallacy of defining cause by effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the fallacy of defining a cause by its effect in any given definition.

Core Idea
Defining a cause by its effect creates a vicious circle.

Have you ever encountered a definition that seems to go in circles? The classical logicians warned against defining a cause by its effect, because the effect itself depends on the cause. This lesson examines a clear example and equips you to spot such circularity in definitions, a common pitfall in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause and effect
In the passage, the sun is the cause (ʿilla) and day (nahār) is the effect (maʿlūl). The sun's rising causes the day. Recognize the dependency: the effect is defined by the cause, but here the cause is defined by the effect.
In many definitions, the cause brings about the effect; the effect cannot be understood without the cause.
2
Locate the circularity
The definition of the sun is 'a planet that rises by day'. But what is 'day'? It is defined as 'time from sunrise to sunset'. So the sun is defined using 'day', and day is defined using the sun. This is a circular definition (idrāj al-maʿlūl fī ḥadd al-ʿilla).
Circular definitions make the definiendum and definiens interdependent, failing to clarify the term.
3
Test the definitions for completeness
A correct definition of the sun should not refer to day. For example, 'the star around which the earth revolves' (in the Ptolemaic context) would avoid circularity. Check if each definition stands on its own without requiring the other term.
The passage emphasizes that the effect is only defined by including the cause, not the reverse.
4
Generalize the fallacy
This error occurs wherever a cause is defined by its effect. In Islamic jurisprudence, defining an effective cause (ʿilla) by the ruling (ḥukm) it produces can lead to circular reasoning, invalidating analogical deduction (qiyās).
Recognizing this pattern helps avoid flawed definitions in fiqh, kalām, and tafsīr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ ذلكَ : أن يُؤخذ المعلولُ في حدِ العلَّةِ معَ أَنَّهُ لا يُحدُّ المعلولُ إلَّا بأن تدخل العلَّةُ في حدِهِ ؛ كَمَنْ يقولُ : (حد الشمس : أنَّهُ كوكبٌ يطلعُ نهاراً ( فيُقالُ : وما حد النهار ؟ فيلزمه أن يقول : ( النهارُ : هو زمان طلوع الشمس إلى غروبها ( إِنْ أَرادَ الحد الصحيح . ولذلك نظائر يكثرُ إحصاؤُها ، وسنزيدها شرحاً في الامتحانات .
And among that is taking the effect in the definition of the cause, even though the effect is not defined except by including the cause in its definition, like one who says: 'The definition of the sun is that it is a planet that rises by day.' Then it is asked: 'What is the definition of day?' He is forced to say: 'Day is the time from the rising of the sun to its setting' if he intends a correct definition. And there are many similar examples, which we will explain further in the Examinations (al-Imtiḥānāt).
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage discusses the error of defining a cause by its effect, when the effect itself is defined by the cause—leading to circularity. The example defines the sun as 'a planet that rises by day', but 'day' is itself defined by the sun's rising. The two definitions become interdependent. The correct definition of the sun should not refer to day, e.g., 'a star around which the earth revolves' (in the medieval context). The author notes that many similar examples exist and promises further elaboration in the section on Examinations (al-Imtiḥānāt).

Key Terms
العلة
المعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It defines the cause by its effect, which is circular
C) It uses an opposite
D) It is too narrow

Key Takeaway
A cause must never be defined by its effect; otherwise, the definition becomes circular and fails to clarify.

Three Circular Definitions: Opposite, Relative, Effect
Author identifies three fallacies in definition that lead to circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correct circular definitions involving opposites, relatives, and causes/effects in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Defining by the unknown leads nowhere.

The author warns us that definitions must give new knowledge. Using the very thing you're defining – or its equal in obscurity – makes the definition useless. This lesson teaches you to spot and fix three common circular patterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the circular pattern
Look at the definition. Does it mention the opposite (ḍidd), the correlate (muḍāf), or the effect (maʿlūl) of the term being defined? If so, you have a candidate for circularity.
The three patterns are: opposite-opposite, relative-relative, cause-effect mutual dependence.
2
Check if both terms are equally known
Ask: is the defining term (e.g., 'odd' for 'even') any clearer than the defined term? If they are equally obscure, the definition fails. True definitions must move from known to unknown.
The author says 'they are equal in ignorance and knowledge'.
3
Propose an independent definition
Try to define the term using its genus and differentia (jins and fasl). For example, instead of 'father = one who has a son', define 'father = an animal from whose sperm an animal of his kind is generated'.
This alternative does not rely on the correlate.
4
Test for remaining circularity
Check that the new definition does not implicitly refer back to the original term through a chain. If it does, refine further. The goal is a definition that stands alone.
The author promises more examples in the 'tests' (imtiḥānāt).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ ذلكَ : أن تعرّفَ الضدَّ بالضدِ ، فتقول : ( حد العلم : ما ليس بظنّ ولا شكّ ولا جهل ) وهكذا حتَّى تحصر الأضداد وحد الزوج : ما ليس بفرد ( ثمَّ تقدر أن تقول : ( وحد الفردِ : ما ليس بزوج ( فيدورُ الأمرُ ، ولا يحصل به بيان ومِنْ ذلكَ : أن يُوجدَ المضافُ في حدِ المضاف إليه ، وهما متكافئان في الإضافةِ ؛ كقول القائل : ( حدُّ الأَبِ : مَن لهُ ابنُ ) ثم لا يعجز أن يقول : ( وحد الابنِ : مَن لَهُ أب ) بل ينبغي أن يقول : ( الأبُ : حيوان تولَّدَ مِنْ نطفتِهِ حيوان هوَ مِنْ نوعِهِ ) فهوَ أبٌ مِنْ حيثُ هو كذلك ، ولا يحيل على الابن ؛ فإنَّهُما في الجهلِ والمعرفة يتساويان . ومِنْ ذلكَ : أن يُؤخذ المعلولُ في حدِ العلَّةِ معَ أَنَّهُ لا يُحدُّ المعلولُ إلَّا بأن تدخل العلَّةُ في حدِهِ ؛ كَمَنْ يقولُ : ( حد الشمس : أنَّهُ كوكبٌ يطلعُ نهاراً ( فيُقالُ : وما حد النهار ؟ فيلزمه أن يقول : ( النهارُ : هو زمان طلوع الشمس إلى غروبها ( إِنْ أَرَادَ الحد الصحيح . ولذلك نظائر يكثر إحصاؤُها ، وسنزيدها شرحاً في الامتحانات .
And among that [is] that you define the opposite by the opposite, so you say: 'The definition of knowledge: what is not doubt nor ignorance' and so on until you enumerate the opposites, and the definition of even: what is not odd. Then you are able to say: 'The definition of odd: what is not even.' So the matter becomes circular, and no clarification is obtained. And among that [is] that the relative is placed in the definition of its correlate, and they are equal in relation; like someone saying: 'The definition of a father: one who has a son.' Then he is not prevented from saying: 'The definition of a son: one who has a father.' Rather, he ought to say: 'A father is an animal from whose sperm an animal of his kind is generated.' So he is a father insofar as he is such, and does not depend on the son, for they are equal in ignorance and knowledge. And among that [is] that the effect is taken in the definition of the cause, whereas the effect is only defined by including the cause in its definition; like someone saying: 'The definition of the sun: it is a planet that rises in the daytime.' Then it is said: 'And what is the definition of daytime?', so it follows that he says: 'Daytime is the time from sunrise to sunset,' if he intends the correct definition. And there are similar cases too numerous to count, and we will give more explanation of them in the tests.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage critiques three types of circular definitions. The first is defining a thing by its opposite (ضد), such as defining knowledge as 'not doubt, not forgetfulness, not ignorance.' One could then define odd as 'not even' and even as 'not odd,' leading to a circle. The problem is that the opposite is equally unknown. The second is defining a relative term by its correlate (مضاف), like defining father as 'one who has a son' and son as 'one who has a father.' Both are equally unknown, so no knowledge is gained. The correct method is to define the essence independently, e.g., father as 'an animal from whose sperm an animal of his kind is generated.' The third is defining a cause by its effect (علة بمعلول) or vice versa, e.g., defining the sun by daytime and daytime by the sun. This also circles. The author notes there are many such examples and promises more in the tests. The lesson teaches that any definition that uses a term equally or more obscure than the definiendum fails to achieve its purpose.

Key Terms
ضد
مضاف
معلول
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It uses too many terms.
B) It is a circular definition because the opposite terms are equally unknown.
C) It is too broad.
D) It is not in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must not rely on terms equally or more obscure than the definiendum, especially opposites, correlates, or effects.

Simple Concepts Cannot Be Defined by Real Definition
Author explains the sixth law: simple concepts can only be defined by verbal explanation or description, not real definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between verbal explanation, description, and real definition, and identify why simple concepts can only be defined by the first two.

Core Idea
Simple concepts have no parts, so real definition via genus and differentia is impossible.

In this lesson, we explore the sixth law: a simple concept (معنى مفرد) that has no composition cannot be given a real definition (حد حقيقي) because a real definition requires breaking the concept into genus and differentia. Instead, the only options are verbal explanation (شرح اللفظ) using a more familiar synonym, or description (رسم) by listing necessary properties. The author illustrates this with the example of 'al-mawjud' (the existent) and explains the limitations of each method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept type
Determine if the concept is simple (معنى مفرد) or composite. Simple concepts lack parts, like 'existent'.
Simple concepts cannot be broken down into genus and differentia.
2
Recognize the impossibility of real definition
Real definition (حد حقيقي) requires genus and differentia; simple concepts have none, so real definition is impossible.
3
Distinguish between verbal explanation and description
Verbal explanation (شرح اللفظ) uses synonyms, while description (رسم) lists properties. Both fall short of expressing essence.
Only real definition expresses essence (ذات).
4
Apply the condition for verbal explanation
Verbal explanation is only helpful if the synonym is more familiar to the questioner. Otherwise, it adds no clarity.
Example: explaining 'al-ʿuqār' as 'wine' benefits only if 'wine' is better known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعنى المفرد القانون السادس في كون المعنى البسيط لا يحد ولا يرسم .. ولا يُحد أنَّ المعنى الذي لا تركيب فيهِ ألبتة لا يمكن حدهُ إلَّا بطريق شرح اللفظ ، أو بطريق الرسم ، وأمَّا الحد الحقيقي .. فلا سبيل إليه. والمثل: (الموجود) فإذا قيلَ لكَ : ما حد الموجود ؟ فغايتُكَ أنْ تقول : هو الشيء ، أو الثابت ، فتكون قد أبدلت اسماً باسم مرادفٍ لهُ ربَّما يتساويان في التفهيم . وربَّما يكونُ أحدُهُما أخفى في وضع اللسانِ ؛ كَمَنْ يقولُ : ما العُقارُ ؟ فنقولُ : هوَ الخمرُ ، وما القَسْورة ؟ فنقولُ : هوَ الأسد. وهذا أيضاً إنَّما يحسنُ بشرط أن يكون المذكور في الجواب أشهر عند السائلِ مِنَ المذكور في السؤال ، ثمَّ لا يكونُ إِلَّا شرحاً للفظ ، وإلا ؛ فمَنْ يطلب تلخيص ذاتِ الأسد .. فلا يتلخص ذلكَ في عقلِهِ إلا بأن يقولَ : سَبْعٌ مِنْ صفتِهِ كيت وكيت ، فأما تكرار الألفاظ المترادفة .. فلا يغنيه ولو قلتَ : حد الموجودِ : أَنَّهُ المعلوم أو المذكورُ ، وقيَّدتَهُ بقيدٍ احترزتَ بهِ عَنِ المعدوم .. كنتَ ذكرت شيئاً من لوازمه وتوابعه ، فكانَ حدُّكَ رسميّاً غير معرب عَنِ الذاتِ ، فلا يكون حقيقياً .
The simple meaning – the sixth law: concerning that the simple concept is not defined nor described. And a concept that has absolutely no composition cannot be defined except by way of verbal explanation or by way of description. As for the real definition – there is no way to it. An example: (the existent). So if you are asked: 'What is the definition of the existent?' Your utmost is to say: 'It is the thing' or 'the fixed,' then you have substituted a name with a synonymous name that perhaps are equal in conveying understanding. And perhaps one of them is more obscure in the usage of the tongue; like someone saying: 'What is al-ʿuqār?' We say: 'It is wine.' And 'What is al-qaswara?' We say: 'It is the lion.' This also is only good on condition that the mentioned in the answer is more well-known to the questioner than the mentioned in the question. Then it is nothing but a verbal explanation. Otherwise, if someone seeks a clarification of the essence of the lion, it will not become clarified in his mind except by his saying: 'A beast whose description is such and such.' As for repeating synonymous words, it does not suffice him. And if you say: 'The definition of the existent: that it is the known or the mentioned,' and you qualify it with a qualification by which you guard against the non-existent, you have mentioned something from its necessary properties and consequences, so your definition is a description (rasm), not expressive of the essence, so it is not real.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage states the sixth law: a simple concept (معنى مفرد) that has no parts cannot be given a real definition (حد حقيقي) because a real definition requires genus and differentia. Instead, only two methods are possible: verbal explanation (شرح اللفظ) by giving a more familiar synonym, or description (رسم) by listing its properties. For example, 'al-mawjud' (the existent) can only be defined as 'al-shay' (the thing) or 'al-thabit' (the fixed) – synonyms that are equally abstract. If the synonym is less known, like 'al-ʿuqār' (an uncommon word for wine) defined as 'wine,' it only helps if the answer is more familiar. But this is just verbal. True understanding of the essence requires describing properties, e.g., lion as 'a beast with such-and-such characteristics.' If you describe the existent as 'the known' with a qualifier to exclude the non-existent, that is a description (rasm), not a real definition. The key point: simple concepts cannot be dismantled into genus and differentia, so real definition is impossible.

Key Terms
معنى مفرد
حد حقيقي
شرح اللفظ
رسم
مرادف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the term 'Allah' not be given a real definition (حد حقيقي)?
A) Because it is a proper name.
B) Because it is a simple concept without parts.
C) Because it is not an Arabic word.
D) Because it is too sacred.

Key Takeaway
A simple concept without parts cannot be defined by real definition; only verbal explanation or description are possible.

Why Simple Concepts Cannot Be Defined
Author explains why simple concepts like existence cannot have a real definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why simple concepts like existence cannot have a real definition (had haqiqi) and identify the conditions required for a real definition.

Core Idea
Simple concepts lack the multiplicity of essential parts needed for definition.

In this passage, the author tackles a fundamental question: can everything be defined? He argues that simple concepts—those not composed of multiple essential attributes—cannot be given a real definition. Understanding this limitation prevents us from futilely attempting to define foundational concepts like existence, which are presupposed by all other definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
The author asserts that existence (al-mawjud) has no definition because it is the beginning of every explanation. Note the reason: it is simple, not composite.
This is the core thesis of the lesson.
2
Understand the analogy of the house
A real definition is like specifying the boundaries of a house, which has multiple sides. If there were only one side, you could not talk about 'limits' in the same way.
3
Examine the example of blackness
Blackness has many attributes, but only some are essential (dhati), like being a color. The definition of blackness would compile these essential parts from general to specific.
The author distinguishes essential, inseparable (lazim), and accidental attributes.
4
Grasp the sphere analogy
A spherical world has only one uniform surface—no multiple boundaries. Thus it cannot be defined by enumerating limits. This illustrates that simple concepts resist definition.
5
Understand the mental composition distinction
When we say blackness is composed of color-ness and blackness-ness, this is mental composition, not physical. In reality, blackness is a single entity; the mind analyzes it. Simple concepts cannot be analyzed even mentally in this way.
This prevents a misunderstanding that would imply simple things are really composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا ؛ الموجودُ لا حدَّ له ؛ فإنَّهُ مبتدأ كل شرحٍ ، فكيفَ يُشرحُ في نفسه ؟! وإنّما قلنا : المعنى المفرد ليس له حد حقيقي .. لأنَّ معنى قول القائل : ما حدُّ الشيء ؟ كقولك : ما حد هذه الدار ؟ وللدار جهات متعدّدة إليها ينتهي الحدُّ ، فيكون تحديد الدار بذكرِ جهاتها المتحدة المتعددة التي هي - أعني : الدار - محصورة مسورة بها . فإذا قيلَ : ما حد السوادِ ؟ فكأنَّهُ يطلبُ بهِ المعاني والحقائق التي بائتلافها تتمُّ حقيقةُ السوادِ ؛ فإنَّ السواد سواد ولون وموجود وعرض ومرئي ومعلوم وموصوف ومذكور وواحد وكثير ومشرق وبرَّاقٌ وكَدِرٌ وغيرُ ذلكَ ممَّا يُوصفُ بهِ مِنَ الأوصافِ ، وهذهِ الصفاتُ بعضُها عارضةٌ تزولُ ، وبعضُها لازمةٌ لا تزول ولكن ليست ذاتيَّةً ؛ ككونه معلوماً وواحداً وكثيراً ، وبعضُها ذاتي لا يُتصوَّرُ فهمُ السوادِ دونَ فهمِهِ ؛ ككونِهِ لوناً ، فطالب الحد كأنَّهُ يقولُ : إلى كم معنى تنتهي حدود حقيقة السوادِ ؟ لنجمعَ له تلكَ المعاني المتعدّدة بتلخيص ؛ بأن نبدأ بالأعم ونختم بالأخص ، ولا نتعرض للعوارض ، وربَّما طلب ألَّا يُتعرّض أيضاً للوازم ، بل للذاتيَّاتِ فقط . فإذا لم يكن المعنى مركَّباً مِنْ ذاتيَّاتٍ متعدّدةٍ كالوجود .. كيف يُتصوَّرُ تحديدُهُ ؟! وكانَ السؤال عنه كقول القائل : ما حد الكرة ؟ ولنقدِرِ العالَمَ كلَّهُ على شكل كرةٍ ، فَلَوْ سُئِلَ عَنْ حَدِهِ كما سُئِلَ عَنْ حدودِ الدار .. كانَ محالاً ؛ إذْ ليس له حدود ، وإِنَّما حده منقطعه ، ومنقطعُهُ سطحه الظاهرُ ، وهو سطح واحدٌ متشابه ، وليس بسطوح مختلفة ، ولا هي منتهيةٌ إلى مختلف حتَّى يُقال : أحد حدودِهِ ينتهي إلى كذا ، والآخرُ إلى كذا . فهذا المثالُ المحسوس ربَّما يُفهمُ منه مقصدي مِنْ هذا الكلام . فلا تفهمَنَّ مِنْ قولي : ( إِنَّ السواد مركَّبُ مِن معنى اللونية والسواديَّةِ ، واللونية جنس ، والسوادية نوع ( أنَّ في السوادِ ذوات متباينةً متفاصلةً ، فلا نقولُ : السوادُ : لون وسواد ، بل هو لون ، ذلك اللون بعينه هو سوادٌ ، ومعناه يتركَّبُ ويتعدَّدُ للعقل ، حتَّى يعقلُ اللونية مطلقاً ، ولا تخطرُ له الزرقة مثلاً ، ثم يعقل الزرقةَ ، فيكون العقل قد عقل أمراً زائداً لا يمكنه أن يجحد تفاصيله في الذهنِ ، ولا يمكنُهُ أن يعتقد تفاصيلَهُ في الوجودِ .
So, existence has no definition, because it is the beginning of every explanation, so how can it be explained in itself?! And we only say: the simple concept has no real definition... because the meaning of the saying 'What is the definition of a thing?' is like your saying 'What are the limits of this house?' And the house has multiple sides to which the boundary ends, so the definition of the house is by mentioning its unified multiple sides by which it—meaning the house—is enclosed and bounded. So if it is said 'What is the definition of blackness?' it is as if one is asking for the meanings and realities by whose combination the reality of blackness is completed; for blackness is blackness and color and existence and accident and visible and known and described and mentioned and one and many and bright and shiny and dull and other such attributes. Some of these attributes are accidental and cease, some are inseparable but not essential—like being known, one, and many—and some are essential such that understanding blackness is inconceivable without understanding them, like being a color. So the requester of the definition is saying: to how many meanings do the limits of the reality of blackness extend? So that we may gather for him those multiple meanings in summary, by starting with the more general and ending with the more specific, without mentioning accidents. And perhaps one might request that inseparable attributes also not be mentioned, but only the essentials. So if the concept is not composed of multiple essentials, like existence, how can its definition be conceived?! And the question about it is like the saying of one who says 'What is the definition of a sphere?' Suppose the entire world is in the shape of a sphere; then if one asks about its definition as one asks about the limits of a house, it would be impossible, because it has no limits; its limit is its boundary, and its boundary is its apparent surface, and it is a single uniform surface, not multiple different surfaces, nor do they terminate in something different such that it could be said: one of its limits ends at such-and-such, and another at such-and-such. So this tangible example may convey my intent from this speech. Do not understand from my saying 'Blackness is composed of the meaning of color-ness and blackness-ness, and color-ness is genus, and blackness-ness is species' that blackness contains distinct separate entities. We do not say 'Blackness is color and blackness'; rather it is color, that same color is blackness, and its meaning is composed and multiple for the mind, such that it understands color-ness absolutely without, for example, blueness occurring to it; then it understands blueness, so the mind has understood an additional matter that it cannot deny its details in the mind, nor can it believe its details in existence.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why simple concepts—those not composed of multiple essential attributes—cannot be defined with a real definition. The author begins by asserting that existence (al-mawjud) has no definition because it is the starting point of all explanation; anything that is presupposed by every definition cannot itself be defined. He then clarifies what is meant by a 'real definition' (had haqiqi): it is like specifying the boundaries of a house, which has multiple sides. Applying this to blackness, the author lists many attributes that can be said of blackness, but only some are essential (like being a color). The process of definition is to compile the essential meanings in order from general to specific, excluding accidents and even inseparable attributes if they are not essential. If a concept, like existence, has no such composition of essentials, it cannot be defined. To illustrate, the author gives the example of a spherical world: it has only one uniform surface, not multiple boundaries, so it cannot be defined by listing sides. Finally, he warns against misunderstanding his claim about blackness being composed of genus and species: this composition is mental, not extramental; blackness is a single reality that the mind analyzes into color-ness and blackness-ness. The point is that simple concepts resist real definition because they lack the multiplicity of essential aspects needed for a definition to enumerate.

Key Terms
حد حقيقي (had haqiqi): A real definition that lists the essential parts (genus and differentia) of a thing. [Note: In later logic, had haqiqi is contrasted with had lafzi (verbal definition). No significant shift.]
مفرد (mufrad): A simple concept with no multiple essential parts, like existence. [Note: In grammar, mufrad means singular; here it means conceptually simple. No shift in logic.]
ذاتي (dhati): Essential attribute without which the essence is inconceivable, e.g., color for blackness. [Note: Stable across the tradition. Not to be confused with 'essential' in physics.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because existence is a genus, not a species.
B) Because existence is a simple concept composed of no multiple essential parts.
C) Because existence is an accident, not a substance.
D) Because existence is self-evident and needs no definition.

Key Takeaway
A simple concept like existence cannot be defined because it has no multiple essential components; any definition would require composing parts that it does not have.

Refutation of the Denier of States
Author argues that denying states leads to tautology or contradiction in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the dilemma against deniers of states and explain why it undermines their ability to produce valid definitions.

Core Idea
Denying states makes definitions impossible—every addition is either redundant or admits two distinct realities.

This passage presents a powerful argument against those who deny the reality of states (ahwal). The author shows that if you reject states, you cannot define anything without falling into either tautology or contradiction. This refutation is crucial for understanding the role of essential attributes in definitions within Islamic logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denier's dilemma
The denier either stops at the genus or adds a differentia. If they add something, is it identical to the genus or other than it?
This is the core logical dilemma.
2
Apply to substance
The author tests defining substance as 'existent' – this fails because accidents also exist. Then tries 'occupying space' – this leads to the dilemma.
The example of jawhar is used to illustrate the problem.
3
Trace the contradiction
If the added term is identical to the genus, it is repetition (useless). If it is different, then you have admitted two distinct realities, contradicting the denial of states.
Both options are unacceptable for the denier.
4
Generalize the argument
The same dilemma applies to any definition attempt by a denier of states, making it impossible for them to produce a valid definition.
This refutes the position comprehensively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أثر اعتقاد ( الحال ) ولا تظنّنَّ أنَّ منكر الحالِ يقدرُ على حدّ شيءٍ ألبتةَ ؛ لأنَّهُ إِذا ذكرَ الجنس واقتصرَ .. بطل عليهِ ، وإذا زادَ شيئاً للاحتراز .. فيقالُ : الزيادة عين الأوَّلِ أَوْ غيرُهُ ؟ فإنْ كانَ عينه .. فهو تكرار فاطرحه ، وإنْ كانَ غيره .. فقد اعترفت بأمرين . وإذا قال في حد الجوهر : ( إنَّهُ موجود ) . . قلنا : بطل بالعرض وإذا قالَ : ( متحيّز ) .. قلنا : قولُكَ : ( متحيّز ) مفهومه غير مفهوم
The effect of belief in the state (al-hal). And do not think that the denier of the state can define anything at all; because if he mentions the genus and stops, then his definition is invalid against him; and if he adds something for precaution, then it is said: is that addition identical to the first or other than it? If it is identical, then it is repetition, so discard it; and if it is other than it, then you have admitted two things. And if he says in the definition of substance: 'It is existent,' we say: it is invalidated by accident; and if he says: 'occupying space,' we say: your saying 'occupying space'—its concept is not understood
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against those who deny the metaphysical concept of 'hal' (state or mode). The author attacks the position that one who rejects states cannot produce a correct definition. The reasoning is a dilemma: when defining something, one must mention at least the genus. If one stops at just the genus, the definition is too broad (e.g., defining substance as 'existent' fails because accidents also exist). To fix this, one adds a differentia. But the denier of states, who holds that there are no real attributes distinct from the essence, faces a problem: either the added term is identical to the genus (mere repetition, useless) or it is different (admitting two distinct things, which contradicts the denial of states). Thus the denier cannot provide a valid definition. The author gives the example of defining jawhar (substance): saying it is 'existent' (mawjud) is too broad and includes accidents; trying to refine by saying 'occupying space' (mutahayyiz) is problematic because the concept of 'occupying space' itself is not understood without additional explanation, again leading to the same dilemma. The argument underscores the necessity of distinct essential attributes for definition.

تكرار
مرادف

Key Takeaway
Denying states forces a logical deadlock in definition: every added term is either redundant or admits a separate reality, making sound definitions impossible.

Why a Sphere Cannot Be Defined by Limits
Author uses sphere to show boundaryless things cannot be defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a sphere lacks the necessary boundaries for a physical definition (hadd) and relate this to the logical concept of definition.

Core Idea
Definition requires boundaries; a sphere has none, so no external limits to define.

The author uses a vivid example of the entire world as a sphere to illustrate a key condition for definition: the definiendum must have distinct boundaries. Without boundaries, a definition in terms of limits is impossible. This lesson unpacks the sphere example and its logical implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's thought experiment
Imagine the entire world shaped like a perfect sphere. Think about what it would mean to define its boundaries.
The sphere is a closed, uniform surface with no edges or corners.
2
Identify the problem with defining a sphere
A sphere's surface is a single continuous curved surface; there are no distinct boundaries where one part ends and another begins. So you cannot say 'one boundary ends here, another there.'
The author says: 'its terminal point is its apparent surface, and it is one uniform surface, not different surfaces.'
3
Relate to logical definition (hadd)
A proper logical definition requires identifying genus and differentia – limits that separate the thing from others. Without clear limits, no proper definition.
In Islamic logic, hadd literally means 'limit' or 'boundary'.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
In Usul al-Fiqh, defining the effective cause ('illa) of a ruling requires clear boundaries. If the 'illa is vague, analogy becomes invalid.
For example, defining 'intoxicating effect' as 'inebriating' requires a clear limit of inebriation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And let us estimate the entire world in the shape of a sphere. So if one were asked about its definition as one is asked about the boundaries of a house, it would be impossible because it has no boundaries. Its definition is only its terminal point, and its terminal point is its apparent surface, and it is one uniform surface, not different surfaces, nor are they ending in different [things] such that one says: one of its boundaries ends here and another there. This sensible example might make my intention from this speech understood. So do not understand from my saying: 'Blackness is composed of the meaning of color-ness and blackness, and color-ness is a genus and blackness is a species' that in blackness there are distinct separate entities. So we do not say: Blackness is color and blackness; rather, it is color; that very color is blackness, and its meaning is composed and multiple for the intellect, so that it intellects color-ness absolutely without, for example, blueness occurring to it, then it intellects blackness, so the intellect has intellected an additional matter which it cannot deny its details in the mind, and it cannot believe its details in existence.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage addresses two related points about definition. First, the author gives an example: if one pictures the world as a perfect sphere, asking for its definition (hadd) in the sense of external boundaries would be impossible because a sphere has no distinct boundaries; it is a single continuous surface. This illustrates that definition requires limits. Second, the author clarifies a common misunderstanding about his earlier statement that blackness is composed of color-ness (genus) and blackness (species). He insists this composition is conceptual, not physical. In the mind, we can separate the notion of color in general from the specific hue, but in reality blackness is a single thing, not a combination of separate parts. The example uses the genus/species relation, which is fundamental in Islamic logic for defining things by their essential attributes.

Key Takeaway
For a proper definition (hadd) to exist, the thing must have identifiable limits; a sphere's uniform surface lacks such differentiation.

Mental Composition of Genus and Species
Author clarifies composition of genus and species is mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental composition (conceptual) and physical composition (real) in the context of genus-species definitions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Composition in definition is mental, not physical – the intellect separates aspects that are one in reality.

After showing the sphere example, the author clarifies a common misunderstanding about his earlier statement that blackness is composed of color-ness (genus) and blackness (species). He insists that this composition is purely intellectual: in reality, blackness is a single entity, but the mind can analyze it into genus and species. This lesson unpacks that crucial clarification.

Guided Path
1
Recall the statement being clarified
The author had said 'blackness is composed of the meaning of color-ness and blackness, and color-ness is a genus and blackness is a species.'
This might lead one to think blackness is physically two things.
2
Identify the potential misunderstanding
One might imagine that blackness contains two separate entities: a generic 'color' and a specific 'blackness', like ingredients in a mixture.
The author warns: 'Do not understand... that in blackness there are distinct separate entities.'
3
Follow the author's clarification
He says: 'we do not say: Blackness is color and blackness; rather it is color, that very color is blackness.' In reality it is one thing; the mind conceptually separates the genus from the species.
The author uses the phrase 'its meaning is composed and multiple for the intellect' – the composition is only for the intellect.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
In definitions of theological or legal concepts, we often use genus and species. For example, 'knowledge' is defined as a 'necessary attribute' (genus) that distinguishes (differentia) – but this does not mean knowledge is two separate things in reality.
The mental composition allows precise definitions without implying physical parts.

الكرة ؟ ولنقدِرِ العالَمَ كلَّهُ على شكل كرةٍ ، فَلَوْ سُئِلَ عَنْ حَدِهِ كما سُئِلَ عَنْ حدودِ الدار .. كانَ محالاً ؛ إذْ ليس له حدود ، وإِنَّما حده منقطعه ، ومنقطعُهُ سطحه الظاهرُ ، وهو سطح واحدٌ متشابه ، وليس بسطوح مختلفة ، ولا هي منتهيةٌ إلى مختلف حتَّى يُقال : أحد حدودِهِ ينتهي إلى كذا ، والآخرُ إلى كذا . فهذا المثالُ المحسوس ربَّما يُفهمُ منه مقصدي مِنْ هذا الكلام . فلا تفهمَنَّ مِنْ قولي : ( إِنَّ السواد مركَّبُ مِن معنى اللونية والسواديَّةِ ، واللونية جنس ، والسوادية نوع ( أنَّ في السوادِ ذوات متباينةً متفاصلةً ، فلا نقولُ : السوادُ : لون وسواد ، بل هو لون ، ذلك اللون بعينه هو سوادٌ ، ومعناه يتركَّبُ ويتعدَّدُ للعقل ، حتَّى يعقلُ اللونية مطلقاً ، ولا تخطرُ له الزرقة مثلاً ، ثم يعقل الزرقةَ ، فيكون العقل قد عقل أمراً زائداً لا يمكنه أن يجحد تفاصيله في الذهنِ ، ولا يمكنُهُ أن يعتقد تفاصيلَهُ في الوجودِ .
And let us estimate the entire world in the shape of a sphere. So if one were asked about its definition as one is asked about the boundaries of a house, it would be impossible because it has no boundaries. Its definition is only its terminal point, and its terminal point is its apparent surface, and it is one uniform surface, not different surfaces, nor are they ending in different [things] such that one says: one of its boundaries ends here and another there. This sensible example might make my intention from this speech understood. So do not understand from my saying: 'Blackness is composed of the meaning of color-ness and blackness, and color-ness is a genus and blackness is a species' that in blackness there are distinct separate entities. So we do not say: Blackness is color and blackness; rather, it is color; that very color is blackness, and its meaning is composed and multiple for the intellect, so that it intellects color-ness absolutely without, for example, blueness occurring to it, then it intellects blackness, so the intellect has intellected an additional matter which it cannot deny its details in the mind, and it cannot believe its details in existence.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage addresses two related points about definition. First, the author gives an example: if one pictures the world as a perfect sphere, asking for its definition (hadd) in the sense of external boundaries would be impossible because a sphere has no distinct boundaries; it is a single continuous surface. This illustrates that definition requires limits. Second, the author clarifies a common misunderstanding about his earlier statement that blackness is composed of color-ness (genus) and blackness (species). He insists this composition is conceptual, not physical. In the mind, we can separate the notion of color in general from the specific hue, but in reality blackness is a single thing, not a combination of separate parts. The example uses the genus/species relation, which is fundamental in Islamic logic for defining things by their essential attributes.

The Necessity of al-Hal and the Problem of Synonymy in Definition
Author argues that denying 'al-hal' makes definition impossible, and explains why synonyms fail to refine a definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why denying 'al-hal' invalidates definitions and distinguish between repetition, synonym, and genuine differentia in definition.

Core Idea
Without 'al-hal', every definition collapses into tautology or contradiction.

In this passage, the author challenges those who deny the existence of 'al-hal' (states or attributes). He demonstrates that such a position makes it impossible to formulate a valid definition, because any attempt to add a distinguishing feature either repeats the original concept (rendering it useless) or admits a new attribute—which the denier cannot allow. The example of defining 'jawhar' (substance) as 'existent' and then adding 'occupying space' illustrates the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
The author is attacking the 'munkir al-hal' (denier of states). Understand what this position entails: rejecting any extra-mental reality for attributes beyond existence and non-existence.
2
Understand the dilemma on adding a qualifier
When a definer adds a word for caution (to avoid objections), it must be either the same as the first word or different. If same, it's repetition (takrar) and adds nothing; if different, the denier admits two distinct attributes, contradicting his denial.
Trace this argument in the Arabic: 'فإن كان عينه .. فهو تكرار فاطرحه، وإن كان غيره .. فقد اعترفت بأمرين'.
3
Analyze the definition of 'jawhar'
The author uses 'jawhar' (substance) as an example. Defining it as 'existent' (mawjud) fails because accidents also exist. Adding 'occupying space' (mutahayyiz) is then examined: Is it identical to 'existent' or different? If identical, it's repetition; if different, it admits two attributes.
The key distinction is between conceptual identity and linguistic difference.
4
Apply the 'lion' analogy
The author compares this to saying 'lion' (asad) in response to an objection. Repeating 'lion' or using a synonym does not remove the objection. This shows that synonyms (muradif) are as useless as repetition in defining terms.
The analogy reinforces that genuine differentiae must add new conceptual content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أثر اعتقاد ( الحال ) ولا تظنّنَّ أنَّ منكر الحالِ يقدرُ على حدّ شيءٍ ألبتةَ ؛ لأنَّهُ إِذا ذكرَ ثبوتاً ونفياً في الحد الجنس واقتصرَ .. بطل عليهِ ، وإذا زادَ شيئاً للاحتراز .. فيقالُ : التكرار والمرادف يدفع النقض الزيادة عين الأوَّلِ أَوْ غيرُهُ ؟ فإنْ كانَ عينه .. فهو تكرار فاطرحه ، وإنْ كانَ غيره .. فقد اعترفت بأمرين . وإذا قال في حد الجوهر : ( إنَّهُ موجود ) . . قلنا : بطل بالعرض وإذا قالَ : ( متحيّز ) .. قلنا : قولُكَ : ( متحيّز ) مفهومه غير مفهوم ( الموجودِ ) أو عينه ؟ فإنْ كانَ عينه .. فكأنَّكَ قلت : ( موجود موجود ) فإنَّ المترادفة كالمتكررة ، فهوَ إذا باطل بالعَرَض ، وإِنْ كانَ قولُكَ : ( موجودٌ ( لا يدفعُ النقض ، وقولُكَ : ( متحيّز ) يدفعُ .. فهو غيرُه بالمعنى لا باللفظ ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ لفظ لا يدفعُ نقضاً فتكراره لا يدفعُ ، ومرادفه لا يدفعُ ، فمهما انتقض قولُكَ : ( أسدٌ ) بشيء .. لم يندفع النقصُ بقولِكَ : ( ليت ) كما لا يندفع بقولِكَ : ( أسد أسد )
The effect of believing in (al-hal). And do not think that the denier of al-hal is able to define anything at all; because if he mentions a positive and negative (attribute) in the definition of the genus and restricts himself, it is invalid for him; and if he adds something for caution, it is said: 'The increase is either the same as the first or other than it.' If it is the same, it is repetition, so discard it; if it is other, then you have admitted two things. And if he says in the definition of substance: 'It is existent', we say: it is invalidated by accident. And if he says: 'occupying space', we say: is the concept of 'occupying space' other than the concept of 'existent' or the same? If it is the same, then it is as if you said: 'existent existent', for synonyms are like repetitions, so it is therefore invalidated by accident; and if your saying 'existent' does not repel the objection, and your saying 'occupying space' repels it, then it is other in meaning not in wording; because every word that does not repel an objection, its repetition does not repel, nor does its synonym repel. So whenever your saying 'lion' is objected to by something, the objection is not repelled by your saying 'lion', nor by your saying 'ass'.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage focuses on the debate surrounding al-hal (the state/attribute) and its role in definition. The author states that one who denies the reality of states (a theological position) cannot define anything. He then explains the dilemma: if the denier of states simply repeats the same word or uses a synonym, it adds nothing and the definition fails. The example given is defining 'jawhar' (substance). If one says 'jawhar is existent', it is invalid because accidents also exist. If one adds 'occupying space', then this added concept must be either identical to 'existent' – in which case it is mere repetition – or different, which would admit two distinct attributes, implying the reality of states. The author uses the analogy of 'lion' and its objectors: if someone says 'lion' and it is objected, saying 'lion' again or a synonym like 'asad' does not remove the objection. This illustrates that definitions require genuine differentiating features, not mere verbal variation.

Distinguishing Verbal, Descriptive, and Real Definitions
Author classifies definitions for simple terms into verbal and descriptive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given definition as verbal, descriptive, or real based on the criteria from this passage.

Core Idea
Simple terms cannot be defined essentially; only verbal or descriptive definitions are possible.

This passage introduces the idea that simple terms (mufrad) resist real definition. Instead, they can only be defined verbally (by synonym) or descriptively (by listing necessary properties). Let's learn how to identify each type using the example of 'al-mawjud' (the existent).

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the simple term 'al-mawjud' in the Arabic passage. The author gives two types of definitions for it.
The term is explicitly mentioned in both examples.
2
Distinguish verbal from descriptive
The verbal definition is 'it is a thing' — explaining the word with a known synonym. The descriptive definition lists properties like 'divided into agent and object' etc. These are necessary concomitants, not essence.
Descriptive definitions often use 'divided into' or list inseparable attributes.
3
Explain why real definition is impossible
The passage states that simple terms have no real definition because they have no genus and differentia. They are simple, so they cannot be broken into essential parts.
Real definition requires knowing the essence (mahiyya) via genus and differentia.
4
Apply to a new case
Consider the term 'wudu' in fiqh. Is its definition 'washing specific limbs with water' real, verbal, or descriptive? Think about whether it reveals essence or describes properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الرسمي واللفظي
فقد عرفْتَ أنَّ المفردَ لا يمكن أن يكونَ لهُ حلٌّ حقيقي إلا انحصار حق المفرد لفظي ؛ كقولك في حدّ ( الموجود ) : إنَّهُ الشيء
أو رسميٌّ ؛ كقولك في حد ( الموجود ) : إِنَّهُ المنقسم إلى الفاعل والمفعول ، أو الخالق والمخلوقِ ، أو القادر والمقدور ، أو الواحد والكثير ، أو القديم والحادثِ ، أو الباقي والفاني ، أو ما شئت من لوزام الموجودِ وتوابعه ، فكلُّ ذلك ليس نباً عَنْ ذاتِ الموجودِ ، بلْ عَنْ تابع لازمٍ لا يفارقُ ألبتةَ .
واعلمُ : أَنَّ توجَّهَ المركَّبَ إذا حددتهُ بذكرِ آحادِ الذاتيّاتِ .. توجه حـد المركبات بتأليف بعض
السؤالُ عَنْ حدِ الأَحادِ .
فإنَّكَ إذا قيلَ لكَ : ما حدُّ الشجر ؟ فقلت : نبات قائم على ساق ، فقيل لكَ : ما حد النباتِ ؟ فتقولُ : جسم نام ، فيُقالُ : وما
(۱) في ( و ) : ( بقولك : أسد ) .
المفردات
On the descriptive and verbal [definition]. You already know that the simple term cannot have a real definition; rather, the right of the simple term is limited to verbal definition, like your saying in the definition of 'existent': it is a thing; or descriptive definition, like your saying in the definition of 'existent': it is divided into agent and object, or creator and created, or powerful and empowered, or one and many, or eternal and originated, or everlasting and perishing, or whatever you wish of the necessary concomitants of 'existent' and its attachments. All that is not informative about the essence of 'existent', but rather about a necessary concomitant that never separates from it. And know: that attention to the compound, when you define it by mentioning the individual essentials, then the definition of compounds [proceeds] by composing some [of them]. The question [arises] about the definition of simple terms. For if you are asked: 'What is the definition of tree?' and you say: 'A plant standing on a trunk', then you are asked: 'What is the definition of plant?' and you say: 'A growing body', then it is said: 'And what [is the definition of body]?' ... (the simple terms).
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between real definition (hall haqiqi) and two types of non-real definitions for simple terms: verbal definition (lafzi), which merely explains the word, and descriptive definition (rasmi), which lists necessary but non-essential properties. The example uses 'existent' (mawjud). A verbal definition says 'it is a thing', equating the term with a known synonym. A descriptive definition says 'it is divided into agent and object, creator and created,' etc., listing its necessary concomitants (lawaazim). These do not reveal the essence, only inseparable attributes. Then the author warns about defining compounds: if you define a compound by listing its essential parts, you will then be asked to define each part, leading to an infinite regress. The example of 'tree' defined as 'plant on a trunk' leads to asking for the definition of 'plant', which is 'growing body', and then for 'body', and so on, until you reach simple terms that cannot be defined essentially. This shows the limitation of definitions and the need to stop at simple primitives.

Key Terms
مفرد
حل حقيقي
حل لفظي
رسمي
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Real definition (hall haqiqi)
B) Verbal definition (hall lafzi) or descriptive definition (rasmi)
C) Only verbal definition
D) No definition at all

Key Takeaway
Simple terms cannot have real definitions; only verbal or descriptive definitions are possible.

The Infinite Regress Problem in Defining Compounds
Author warns that defining compounds leads to regress unless stopped by simple terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a chain of definitions leads to an infinite regress and explain why simple terms halt the process.

Core Idea
Defining compounds by their parts leads to an infinite regress unless we accept primitive simple terms.

The passage then warns that if you define a compound term by listing its essential parts, you will be asked to define each part, leading to a chain that never ends until you reach simple terms that cannot be defined essentially. This highlights the limitation of definitions and the need for primitive terms.

Guided Path
1
Locate the regress example
Find the chain in the passage: 'tree' (shajar) → 'plant' (nabat) → 'body' (jism). Each term is defined by a broader category, which itself must be defined.
The passage shows this sequence to illustrate how the regress works.
2
Understand the mechanism
Each definition of a compound uses a genus (e.g., 'plant' for tree). The genus is itself a term that can be defined further. This creates a chain of definitions.
The regress occurs because we keep asking for the definition of the genus.
3
Identify the stopping point
The chain ends when we reach simple terms (like 'body' or 'existent'), which cannot be defined essentially. They are the primitives that halt the regress.
Simple terms are the foundation; they can only be defined verbally or descriptively.
4
Recognize the consequence
If we insist on real definitions for every term, we would never reach a definitive definition. The author implies that we must accept some terms as primitive.
This is a foundational point in logic: not everything can be defined.

Simple Terms Cannot Be Defined Essentially
Author explains that simple terms have only verbal or descriptive definitions, and definitions of composites end in primitive concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real, verbal, and descriptive definitions, and identify why a simple term like 'existent' cannot have a real definition.

Core Idea
Simple terms cannot be defined essentially; only verbal or descriptive definitions are possible.

In this passage, the author explains the limits of definition. Some terms are so basic that any attempt to define them by their essence ends in absurdity. Understanding this prevents fruitless debates over foundational concepts.

Guided Path
1
Examine the 'existent' example
The author shows that defining 'existent' as 'existent' is circular and does not repel objections. Note that a definition must be informative and different from the definiendum.
The condition of repelling objections is crucial.
2
Distinguish verbal vs descriptive definitions
A verbal definition explains the word's meaning without revealing essence. A descriptive definition lists necessary concomitants, like 'existent' divided into Creator and creature.
Neither is a real (essential) definition.
3
Identify why simple terms lack real definitions
Simple terms are not composed of more basic elements; they are the building blocks of knowledge. Their essences cannot be unpacked further.
