المنقسم إلى الفاعل والمفعول ، أو الخالق والمخلوقِ ، أو القادر والمقدور ، أو الواحد والكثير ، أو القديم والحادثِ ، أو الباقي والفاني ، أو ما شئت من لوزام الموجودِ وتوابعه ، فكلُّ ذلك ليس نباً عَنْ ذاتِ الموجودِ ، بلْ عَنْ تابع لازمٍ لا يفارقُ ألبتةَ . واعلمُ : أَنَّ توجَّهَ المركَّبَ إذا حددتهُ بذكرِ آحادِ الذاتيّاتِ .. توجه حـد المركبات بتأليف بعض السؤالُ عَنْ حدِ الأَحادِ . فإنَّكَ إذا قيلَ لكَ : ما حدُّ الشجر ؟ فقلت : نبات قائم على ساق ، فقيل لكَ : ما حد النباتِ ؟ فتقولُ : جسم نام ، فيُقالُ : وما حد الجسم ؟ فتقولُ : جواهرُ مؤتلفةٌ ، فيُقالُ : وما حد الجوهر ؟ وهكذا . فإِنَّ كلَّ مؤلَّفٍ فيهِ مفردانِ ، وكلُّ مفردٍ فله حقيقةٌ ، وحقيقته أيضاً تأتلفُ مِنْ مفردات ، فلا تظنّ أنَّ هذا يتمادى إلى غير نهاية ، بل ينتهي إلى مفردات يعرفُها العقل والحس معرفةً أوليةً ، لا يحتاج إلى طلبها بصيغة الحدّ . كما أنَّ التصديقات تنتهي إلى علوم أولية التصديقيَّةَ يُطالب بالبرهان عليها ، وكلُّ برهان العلوم مِنْ مقدمتين ، ولا بدَّ لكلّ مقدمةٍ أيضاً مِنْ برهان بمقدمتين ، وهكذا يتسلسلُ إلى أن ينتهي إلى أوَّليَّاتٍ في العلومِ أَوَّلِيَّاتٍ .. فكذا في المعارفِ ، فطالبُ بالعقل تحتاج إلى بيان لفظي أو حدودِ الأَوَّليَّاتِ إِنَّما يطلب شرحَ اللفظ ، لا الحقيقة ؛ فإنَّ الحقيقة تكون ثابتة في عقلِهِ بالفطرةِ الأولى كثبوت حقيقة ( الوجودِ ) في العقل ، فإن طلب الحقيقة .. فهوَ معاند ؛ كمَنْ يطلب البرهان على أنَّ الاثنينِ أَكثرُ مِنَ الواحدِ . فهذا ما أردنا إيرادَهُ منَ القوانين ، ولنشتغل بالامتحانات بحدودٍ ذُكرَتْ في فنّ الكلام والفقه ؛ ليحصل بها الدربةُ بكيفية استعمال القوانين .
Is it (the term 'existent') or its very self? If it is its very self, then it is as if you said 'existent existent', for synonyms are like repetition; thus it is invalid accidentally. If your statement 'existent' does not repel the objection, and your statement 'extended' repels it, then it is different in meaning, not in wording. Because any wording that does not repel an objection, its repetition does not repel, and its synonym does not repel. So whenever your statement 'lion' is refuted by something, the refutation is not repelled by your statement 'brave', just as it is not repelled by your statement 'lion lion'. (On the descriptive and the verbal.) You have known that a simple term cannot have a real definition; its right is confined to a verbal definition, like your saying in defining 'existent': it is a thing, or a descriptive definition, like your saying in defining 'existent': it is that which is divided into agent and patient, or creator and created, or capable and capable-of, or one and many, or eternal and originated, or abiding and perishing, or whatever you wish of the concomitants and followers of 'existent'. All that is not a report about the essence of 'existent', but about a necessary concomitant that never separates from it. And know that when you define a composite by mentioning its individual essential attributes, the definition of composites is directed through the composition of some (parts) and the question about the definition of simples. For if you are asked: What is the definition of 'tree'? You say: A plant standing on a trunk. Then you are asked: What is the definition of 'plant'? You say: A growing body. Then it is said: And what is the definition of 'body'? You say: Combined substances. Then it is said: And what is the definition of 'substance'? And so on. For every composite contains two simple terms, and every simple term has an essence, and its essence also is composed of simples. Do not think that this continues without end; rather it ends at simples that the intellect and sense know by primary knowledge, not needing to seek them in the form of definition. Just as assents end in primary sciences: the seeker of a demonstration for them is asked for a proof, and every proof is from two premises, and every premise also must have a proof from two premises, and so on, until it ends in primaries in the sciences, primaries. Thus it is with cognitions. So the seeker of a verbal explanation or definition of primaries only asks for an explanation of the wording, not the essence; for the essence is established in his mind by the first innate disposition, like the establishment of the essence of 'existence' in the mind. If he seeks the essence, he is obstinate, like one who seeks a proof that two is greater than one. This is what we intended to present of the rules. Let us now engage in examinations of definitions mentioned in the art of kalam and fiqh, so that practice may be gained in how to apply the rules.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of definition, distinguishing between definitions of simple and composite terms, and emphasizes that definitions ultimately rest on primitive concepts. The author begins with an analysis of defining the term 'existent' (al-mawjud). If one attempts to define 'existent' by saying it is 'existent', that is merely a repetition and invalid. If one uses a synonym or a term that does not repel an objection, it fails. For example, defining 'lion' as 'brave' does not repel an objection that a brave person is not a lion. A proper definition must provide a different meaning that repels objections, such as 'extended' for 'existent'. The author then states that simple terms (mufradat) cannot have a real definition (hadd haqiqi) that reveals their essence; they can only have a verbal definition (lafzi) that explains the word, or a descriptive definition (rasmi) that lists necessary concomitants (lawazim) like dividing 'existent' into agent/patient, creator/created, etc. These do not report the essence but rather a necessary inseparable attendant. Next, the author explains the definition of composites. When defining a composite like 'tree', one mentions its essential attributes (e.g., a plant standing on a trunk). This leads to asking for definitions of the simpler terms (plant, body, substance), which in turn are composed of even simpler terms. This chain must eventually end in primitive, simple concepts known by the intellect and sense through primary knowledge (ma'rifa awwaliyya) without need for definition. The author draws an analogy with assents (tasdiqat): just as every proof requires premises that ultimately trace back to primary, self-evident propositions (awwaliyat), so every definition of non-primitive terms traces back to primitive concepts. Thus, when someone demands a definition of a primitive concept like 'existence', they are really asking for a verbal explanation, not the essence, which is already known by innate disposition. If they insist on the essence, they are obstinate, like asking for a proof that two is greater than one. The passage concludes by stating the intention to apply these rules to definitions in kalam and fiqh for practice.

Key Takeaway
Simple terms cannot have real definitions; they can only be explained verbally or by their inseparable attributes.

The Chain of Definitions Ends in Primitive Concepts
Author explains that simple terms have only verbal or descriptive definitions, and definitions of composites end in primitive concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the chain of definitions from a composite term to primitive concepts, and explain why definitions must eventually terminate in self-evident knowledge.

Core Idea
Definitions of composites lead to simpler concepts, eventually reaching primitives known by innate knowledge.

The author uses the example of defining 'tree' to show that definitions of composites require defining their components, and this chain must end. Understanding this prevents infinite regress in definitions and mirrors the structure of proof.

Guided Path
1
Start with a composite definition
Taken the definition of 'tree': a plant standing on a trunk. Note that 'plant' and 'trunk' are themselves in need of definition.
2
Trace the chain of definitions
Ask for the definition of 'plant' → 'growing body'. Then 'body' → 'combined substances'. Then 'substance' → ? Recognise that each step leads to simpler terms.
3
Identify the endpoint: primitive concepts
The chain cannot go infinitely. It must end in terms known directly by intellect and sense, such as 'existence'. These are self-evident and require no definition.
Analogous to proofs ending in primary propositions (awwaliyat).
4
Apply the analogy with tasdiqat
Just as every proof traces back to primary premises, every definition traces back to primitive concepts. Demanding a real definition for these is obstinate.

The Fallacy of Pseudo-Disagreement: Homonymy in Definitions
Author critiques the notion that there is a genuine disagreement about the definition of 'hadd'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two definitions of the same term are not in genuine disagreement due to homonymy, and apply the concept of tawārud to evaluate whether a debate is about the same subject.

Core Idea
Homonymy makes two definitions non-rival — disagreement requires convergence on one subject.

When scholars offer different definitions of a key term like 'hadd', it is tempting to think they are in conflict. But the author shows that a proper logical analysis reveals they may be defining entirely different things using the same word. This lesson teaches you to diagnose the fallacy of pseudo-disagreement by checking for tawārud (convergence) and ism mushtarak (homonymy).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions on the definition of hadd
List the three opinions: (1) hadd as reality/essence, (2) hadd as verbal definition that includes and excludes, (3) a third person who thinks they are rivals and takes sides.
2
Understand the condition for genuine disagreement (tawārud)
The author states that disagreement is only possible after agreement (toward one subject). So we must check if the two definitions refer to the same subject.
3
Apply the condition to the two definitions
Do definitions (1) and (2) refer to the same thing? No: one talks about the thing itself, the other about the verbal explanation. They have not converged on a single subject.
4
Identify the source of the error (homonymy)
The author points to heedlessness of ism mushtarak. The word 'hadd' has multiple meanings, so the third person mistakenly treats them as alternatives.
5
Test with author's examples (mukhtar and ayn)
Apply the same logic: different definitions of 'mukhtar' and 'ayn' do not conflict because they define different aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الامتحان الأول في تعريف الحد. اختلف الناسُ في حد (الحد): فمِنْ قائل يقولُ: حدُّ الشيء: هوَ حقيقته ونفسه وذاته. ومِنْ قائل يقولُ: حدُّ الشيء: هوَ اللفظ المفسِّرُ لمعناه على وجه يجمع ويمنعُ. ومِنْ قائل ثالث: يقدِرُ هذه مسألة خلافية، فينصر أحد الحدين على الآخر! وانظرْ كيف تخبَّطَ عقلُ هذا الثالث، فلم يعلم أنَّ الاختلاف لا يتصور إلا بعد التوافق. إنَّما يُتصوَّرُ بعدَ التوارد على شيءٍ واحدٍ، وهذان قد تباينا وتباعدا، وما تواردا على شيء واحد. وإِنَّما منشأ هذا الغلط: الذهولُ عَنْ معرفةِ الاسم المشتركِ الذي ذكرناه، فإِنَّ مَنْ يحدُّ (المختار) بأنَّهُ القادر على تركِ الشيء وفعله .. ليس مخالفاً لمَنْ يحدُّهُ بأنَّهُ الذي خُلِّيَ ورأيَهُ، كما أَنَّ مَنْ حدَّ (العينَ) بأنَّهُ العضو المدركُ للألوان بالرؤية .. لم يخالف
The first examination concerning the definition of 'definition'. People disagreed about the limit (definition) of definition: Some said: The definition of a thing is its reality, itself, and essence. Another said: The definition of a thing is the utterance that explains its meaning in a way that includes and excludes. And a third person considers this a matter of disagreement, and supports one of the two definitions over the other! Look how confused the mind of this third person is, for he did not know that disagreement can only be conceived after agreement. It is only conceived after convergence on one thing, but these two have diverged and distanced, and they have not converged on one thing. The source of this error is heedlessness of the knowledge of the homonym (al-ism al-mushtarak) that we mentioned. For whoever defines 'al-mukhtar' as 'the one able to abandon or do a thing' does not differ from whoever defines it as 'the one left to his own opinion,' just as whoever defines 'al-ayn' as 'the organ that perceives colors by sight' has not differed.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage critically examines a common logical error regarding definition. The author presents three positions on the definition of 'hadd' (definition) itself. The first holds that the definition of a thing is its very essence (dhāt). The second holds that definition is a linguistic utterance (lafẓ) that explains the meaning by combining essential attributes (jāmi') and excluding non-essential ones (māni'). The third person mistakenly thinks these are rival viewpoints in genuine disagreement and picks one side. The author diagnoses this error: genuine disagreement can only occur when both parties are talking about the same thing (tawārud). But here the two definitions are so different that they are not even addressing the same concept—the first is about the reality itself, the second about the verbal explanation. Therefore, they are not in conflict. The source of the error is ignorance of homonymy (ishtirāk lafẓī): the word 'hadd' has multiple meanings. To illustrate, the author gives two examples: (1) 'al-mukhtar' defined as 'the one capable of doing or leaving an action' vs. 'the one left to his own choice'—these are not contradictory but different definitions for different aspects. (2) 'al-ayn' defined as 'the organ perceiving colors'—obviously not differing from other definitions because they define different things. The text ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: confusion arises when people think they are arguing when they are actually defining different meanings of a homonym.

اسم مشترك
توارد

Key Takeaway
Genuine disagreement requires that both parties refer to the same subject (tawārud); when a word is a homonym, definitions may be compatible rather than contradictory.

The Four Senses of 'al-Hadd' and How to Define Ambiguous Terms
Author explains the four meanings of 'hadd' and how to define ambiguous terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four meanings of 'al-hadd' and correctly identify which one is intended in a given definitional context.

Core Idea
Al-hadd is ambiguous across four distinct meanings, so a proper definition must first resolve this ambiguity.

This passage tackles a foundational issue in logic: the term 'hadd' (definition) itself is ambiguous. The author explains that 'hadd' can refer to the reality of a thing, the explanation of a word, a collection by accidents, or the indication of quiddity. Unless we clarify which sense is meant, logical discussions become confused. This lesson will guide you through recognizing these four senses and applying the method for defining ambiguous terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguity
Recognise that the term 'hadd' is not univocal; the author explicitly states it is shared (mushtarak) between four meanings.
The term 'hadd' is like 'al-‘ayn' which has multiple meanings.
2
List the four meanings
The author enumerates: reality (al-haqīqa), word explanation (sharḥ al-lafẓ), collection by accidents (al-jam‘ bi-l-awāriḍ), indication of quiddity (al-dalāla ‘alā al-māhiyya).
These correspond to three types of definition: verbal, descriptive, and real.
3
Understand each type
For each meaning, note the corresponding definition type: verbal (lafẓī) substitutes a word with a clearer synonym; descriptive (rasmī) enumerates essential or necessary attributes; real (ḥaqīqī) indicates the complete quiddity. The author differentiates them by purpose and condition.
The real definition does not need to mention comprehensiveness/exclusivity because they follow necessarily.
4
Apply the method
When asked for a definition, first check if the term is ambiguous; if so, determine how many meanings it has and provide a separate definition for each. Example: 'insān' has multiple meanings (pupil, human, depiction, corpse) each requiring its own definition.
The author gives practical examples like cut-off organs being named based on form.
5
Practise with a real example
Use the term 'kitāb' (book) which can mean a written text, a scripture, or a binding; define each separately following the four-sense framework.
This mirrors the author's instruction to 'seek the number of meanings in which it is shared'.

الشيء ) ، ولا يُحتاج في هذا أن يُذكر الطرد والعكس؛ لأنَّ ذلك يتبع الماهية بالضرورة ، ولا يحتاجُ إلى أن يتعرَّضَ للازم والعرضي ؛ فإنَّهُ لا يدلُّ على الماهِيَّةِ إلا الذاتيات . فقد عرفتَ أنَّ اسم ( الحدّ ) مشترك في الاصطلاحاتِ بين : الحقيقة ، وشرح اللفظ ، والجمعِ بالعوارض ، والدلالة على الماهيَّةِ ، وهذهِ أربعة أمور مختلفةٍ ، كما دل لفظ ( العين ) على أمور مختلفة . فتعلَّمْ صياغة الحدّ ؛ فإذا ذُكِرَ لكَ اسمٌ ، وطُلِبَ حده .. فانظُرْ ؛ كيف تصنع تعريفاً الاسم ما ؟ فإنْ كانَ مشتركاً .. فاطلب عدَّةَ المعاني التي فيها الاشتراك ، فإن من كانتْ ثلاثةً .. فاطلب ثلاثة حدودٍ ؛ لأنَّ الحقائق إذا كانَ يُسمَّى بِهِ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهَا آلة البطش وآلة الوقاع ، وبعد القطع يُسمَّى بِهِ مِنْ حِيثُ إِنَّ شكلَهُ شكل آلة البطش والوقاع ، فإن بطل بالتقطيعاتِ الكثيرة شكله .. سُلِبَ الاسم ، ولَوْ صُنعَ شَكلُهُ مِنْ خشب أو حجرٍ .. أُعطي الاسم . الحدود عند تعدد المعاني
The definition of writing, which is the fourth, and not of knowledge, which is the second, but rather it is shared between reality and the word. And every word shared between two realities... then necessarily it must have two different definitions, like the word (al-‘ayn, al-mukhtar) and their parallels. So then, when applied to the thing itself, the definition of (al-hadd) is: that it is the reality of the thing and its essence. And when applied to the second, the definition of (al-hadd) is: that it is the comprehensive, exclusionary expression. However, those who applied it to the word also differed in their convention, as we mentioned regarding verbal, descriptive, and real definitions. Verbal definition of (al-hayya) — so the definition of (al-hadd) according to one who is satisfied with repeating the word, like his saying: 'the existent is the thing,' 'motion is transfer,' 'knowledge is cognition' — is the substitution of the word with what is clearer to the questioner, on a condition that it includes and excludes. Descriptive definition of (al-hayya) — as for the definition of (al-hadd) according to one who is satisfied with descriptive definitions, it is: that it is the expression explaining the thing by enumerating its essential or necessary attributes in a way that distinguishes it from others in a comprehensive and coextensive manner. Real definition of (al-hadd) — and as for its definition according to one who does not apply the name 'definition' except to the real: (it is the statement indicating the complete quiddity of the thing), and in this there is no need to mention comprehensiveness and exclusivity, because that necessarily follows the quiddity, and there is no need to address the necessary and accidental, because only the essential indicates the quiddity. So you know that the name (al-hadd) is shared in conventions between: reality, explanation of the word, collection by accidents, and indication of quiddity; these are four different matters, just as the word (al-‘ayn) indicates different matters. So learn the formulation of definition; when a name is mentioned to you and its definition is requested, then look: how do you construct a definition? What is the name? If it is shared, then seek the number of meanings in which it is shared. If they are three, then seek three definitions, because if realities differ, then definitions must necessarily differ. So if it is said: 'What is al-insān?' — do not aspire to a single definition, for indeed al-insān is shared among matters; it is applied to the pupil of the eye (insān al-‘ayn) and has a definition, and to the well-known human and has another definition, and to the human depicted on the wall and has another definition, and to the dead human and has another definition. Likewise, the cut-off male organ and the cut-off hand are called 'male organ' and 'hand', not in the sense they were previously called, for they were called that from the aspect that they are instruments of striking and copulation, and after cutting they are called that from the aspect that their form is like the form of the instrument of striking and copulation; if their form is destroyed by many cuttings, the name is removed, and if their form is made from wood or stone, the name is given. Definitions when meanings are multiple.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the ambiguity of the term 'al-hadd' (definition) itself. The author, likely Ibn Taymiyya, begins by stating that 'al-hadd' is shared between two fundamental meanings: the reality of a thing (al-haqīqa) and the word (al-lafẓ). He then states that any word shared between two realities must have two different definitions, giving the example of 'al-‘ayn' (eye/spring/spy) and 'al-mukhtār' (the chosen/one with free will). Next, he elaborates on the three types of definition: 1) verbal definition (al-hadd al-lafẓī), which merely substitutes a word with a clearer synonym; 2) descriptive definition (al-hadd al-rasmī), which identifies the thing by enumerating its essential or necessary attributes; and 3) real definition (al-hadd al-ḥaqīqī), which indicates the complete quiddity (māhiyya) of the thing and does not require explicit mention of comprehensiveness and exclusivity because they follow necessarily. The author then summarizes that the term 'al-hadd' is shared among four different senses: reality (al-ḥaqīqa), explanation of the word (sharḥ al-lafẓ), collection by accidents (al-jam‘ bi-l-awāriḍ), and indication of quiddity (al-dalāla ‘alā al-māhiyya). He emphasizes that one must first determine which sense is intended before defining. Finally, he gives practical instructions: when asked for a definition of a term, first check if it is ambiguous (mushtarak); if it has multiple meanings, then one must provide multiple definitions. He illustrates this with the word 'al-insān' (human), which can mean the pupil of the eye, a living human, a depicted human, or a dead human, each requiring its own definition. He also gives the example of a cut-off male organ or hand, which are called by the same name due to similarity of form, not function. The passage emphasizes the importance of distinguishing between different meanings to avoid confusion in logical discourse.

The First Source of Disagreement in Definitions: Ambiguous Revealed Terms
Author refutes objection and clarifies first cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definitional dispute arises from an ambiguous term in a revealed text and classify it as a matter of tafsir rather than logic.

Core Idea
Disagreement on a revealed definition is about the speaker's intent, not the definition itself.

A critic objects: 'People disagree about definitions, so how can logic be certain?' The author responds by first acknowledging that disagreement is possible, but only in specific situations. One such situation is when the term being defined occurs in a revealed text (Quran, Sunnah, or an Imam's statement) and that term is ambiguous (mushtarak). In that case, the disagreement is about the intended meaning of the speaker, not about the logical definition. Such disputes belong to the science of tafsir, not to logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The critic claims that disagreement about definitions disproves logical certainty. Locate the objection in the passage: 'I see people differing about definitions... do you think they are not rational?'
The objection is a reductio ad absurdum against the claim that disagreement in definition is impossible.
2
Recognize the condition of tawarud
Tawarud means both disputants are referring to the same subject. Without tawarud, there is no real dispute. The author gives examples: 'the sky is eternal' and 'man is created upon movements' have no tawarud.
Tawarud is a prerequisite for meaningful disagreement.
3
Apply the distinction for revealed terms
When a term is in a revealed text and is ambiguous (mushtarak), the dispute is about the speaker's intention. This is a legitimate disagreement after tawarud.
The example here is a term like 'qur'' in the Quran, which can mean menstruation or purity.
4
Classify the dispute
Such disputes belong to the craft of exegesis (tafsir), not to the craft of rational examination (logic). The author explicitly says: 'clarifying that belongs to tafsir, not to rational examination.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : فأرى الناس يختلفون في الحدودِ ، وهذا الكلام يكاد يحيلُ الخلاف في الحدِ ، أفترى أنَّ المتنازعين فيه ليسوا عقلاء ؟ فاعلم : أنَّ الخلاف في الحدِ يُتصوَّرُ في موضعينِ : أحدهما : أن يكونَ اللفظ في كتاب الله تعالى ، أو سنة رسوله ، أو قول إمام منَ الأئمة ، ويكون ذلك اللفظ مشتركاً ، فيقع النزاع في مرادِهِ بهِ ، فيكون قد وُجِدَ التوارد على مرادِ القائل ، والتباين بعد التواردِ ، فالخلافُ تباين بعد التواردِ ، وإلا .. فلا نزاعَ بينَ مَن يقولُ : السماءُ قديمٌ ، وبينَ مَن يقولُ : الإنسانُ مجبول على الحركاتِ ، أو : المغصوب مضمون ؛ إذْ لا توارد . فلوْ كانَ لفظ الحدِ في كتابِ اللهِ تعالى ، أو في كتاب إمام .. لجاز أن يُتنازعَ في مرادِهِ ، ويكون إيضاح ذلكَ مِنْ صنعةِ التفسير ، لا مِنْ صناعة النظرِ العقلي . الثاني : أن يقع الخلافُ في مسألة أخرى على وجهِ محقق ، ويكون المطلوب حدُّه أمراً ثانياً ، ولا يتَّحِدُ حده على المذهبين ، فيختلف ؛ كما يقولُ المعتزلي : حدُّ العلم : اعتقاد الشيء ، ونحنُ نخالفُهُ في ذكرِ ( الشيء ) فإنَّ المعدوم عندنا ليس بشيء ، فالخلافُ في مسألة أخرى تتعدَّى إلى الحدّ . وكذلك يقولُ القائلُ : حدُّ العقلِ : بعض العلوم الضروريَّةِ على وجه كذا ، ويخالفُ مَن يقولُ في حدِهِ : إِنَّهُ غريزةٌ يُتهيَّأُ بها لإدراكِ المعقولات ؛ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ ينكرُ وجود غريزة ، بل يقولُ : ليس في الإنسانِ إلَّا جسمٌ وفيه حياةٌ وعلومٌ ضروريَّةٌ ، أما غريزة يتميز بها القلبُ عَنِ العقبِ ، والإنسانُ عَنِ الذباب .. فلا ؛ فإنَّ الله تعالى قادر على خلقِ العلوم النظرية في العقب وفي الذباب ، فيشير الخلافُ في غير الحدِ خلافاً في الحدِ. فهذا وإن أوردناه في معرض الامتحانِ .. فقد أفدناك به ما يجري على التحقيقِ مَجْرى القوانين .
If you say: 'I see people differing about definitions, and this speech almost prevents disagreement in definition. Do you think that those who dispute it are not rational?' Then know: Disagreement in definition is conceivable in two places. First: that the word occurs in the Book of Allah, or the Sunnah of His Messenger, or the saying of an Imam from the Imams, and that word is ambiguous (mushtarak), so contention occurs about what is meant by it. Thus there is convergence (tawarud) on the intention of the speaker, and divergence after convergence. So the disagreement is divergence after convergence. Otherwise, there is no dispute between one who says 'the sky is eternal' and one who says 'man is created upon movements' or 'the usurped property is guaranteed', because there is no convergence. So if the word of the definition occurs in the Book of Allah or in the book of an Imam, it is permissible to contend about its intended meaning, and clarifying that belongs to the craft of exegesis (tafsir), not to the craft of rational examination. Second: that disagreement occurs about another issue in a verified manner, and the thing whose definition is sought is a secondary matter, and its definition is not uniform across the two schools, so it differs. As the Mu'tazili says: 'The definition of knowledge is belief of the thing', and we oppose him in mentioning 'the thing' because the non-existent (ma'dum) according to us is not a thing. So the disagreement is about another issue that extends to the definition. Similarly, one says: 'The definition of intellect is some necessary sciences in such-and-such manner', and he is opposed by one who says in its definition: 'It is an instinct by which one is prepared to apprehend the intelligibles', insofar as he denies the existence of an instinct. Rather, he says: 'There is nothing in man except a body in which there is life and necessary sciences. As for an instinct by which the heart is distinguished from the heel, and man from the fly, no: for Allah is able to create theoretical sciences in the heel and in the fly.' So the disagreement about what is not the definition becomes a disagreement about the definition. This, even though we have brought it in the context of examination, we have thereby benefited you with something that runs, upon verification, in the course of principles.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to an objection. The author anticipates a critic who says: 'If definitions are so precise, why do rational people disagree about them? Your claim that disagreement is impossible in definition seems false.' The author refutes this by distinguishing two legitimate sources of disagreement about definitions, neither of which undermines the certainty of logical definitions themselves.

The argument proceeds in order: First, the author affirms that disagreement about definitions is indeed conceivable, but only in two specific scenarios. The first scenario is when the term being defined appears in a revealed text (Quran, Sunnah, or a qualified Imam's statement) and that term is ambiguous (mushtarak). In that case, the dispute is about the intended meaning of the speaker, not about the logical definition per se. The disputants agree on the subject of discussion (tawarud), but differ on the interpretation. This disagreement belongs to the science of tafsir, not to logic or rational investigation. For example, if someone says 'the sky is eternal' and another says 'man is created upon movements', there is no tawarud, so no real dispute. But if both are interpreting the same word in a revealed text, there is tawarud followed by ikhtilaf.

The second scenario is when a prior disagreement about another fundamental issue causes a difference in the definition of a secondary term. The author provides two examples: First, the Mu'tazili defines 'ilm (knowledge) as 'belief of the thing' (i'tiqad al-shay'), but his opponent rejects the word 'shay'' because the non-existent (ma'dum) is not a thing according to Ahl al-Sunna. The real disagreement is about whether the non-existent is a thing, and this dispute extends to the definition of knowledge. Second, the definition of 'aql (intellect) is disputed: some say it is a set of necessary sciences, others say it is an innate instinct (ghariza) that enables one to grasp intelligibles. The latter view is opposed by someone who denies the existence of any such instinct, arguing that man is only body and life and necessary sciences, and that Allah can create theoretical sciences even in a lifeless thing like a heel or a fly. Thus, the underlying disagreement about the existence of an instinct leads to a difference in definition.

In both scenarios, the disagreement is not about the logical definition itself but about prior metaphysical or theological commitments. Therefore, the author concludes that disagreements about definitions do not invalidate logical certainty; they merely reflect disputes in other domains. This passage serves as a methodological clarification: the science of logic deals with definitions abstractly, and when disagreements occur, they stem from ambiguity in revealed texts or from prior doctrinal differences, not from any defect in logical methodology.

All examples are drawn from Islamic theological debates: Mu'tazili vs. Sunni on the non-existent, and different views on the nature of intellect. This reinforces the rule that only Islamic examples are used.

Key Terms

الخلاف
التوارد
اللفظ المشترك

Key Takeaway
Not all disagreement about definitions undermines logic; when a revealed term is ambiguous, the dispute is about interpretation, not definition.
